BEFORE THE MIRROR
By “JEANNETTE.”
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and
all of us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow
over the lives of many of us women, and while perhaps a few —the more
stoical among us —can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, we
may all, with a little care and forethought, succeed in preventing the results
of worry from being noticeable in our appearance. To these fortunate
few this little chat will, however, be as interesting and instructive as to
those who stand in real need of help in toilet matters, and may be the
means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown method of retain-
ing or regaining that beauty of face and figure which is the birthright of
every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present, not
generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Wrinkles, and How to Remove them.—Worry,
late hours or the passing of old Father Time
may cause those ugly little lines which so
detract from the freshness and charm of a
woman’s face; but the cure is always the
same. Feed the tissues beneath the skin, and
massage them with a reliable skin food, which,
while nourishing the fatty tissues, will not
coarsen the skin nor grow hairs upon the
face, as so many inferior creams do. lo
remove the wrinkles, bathe the face with hot
water, wipe it dry, then, while it is still
warm, smear a little mercolized wax over the
whole face, carrying it well down on the neck,
and, with the tips of the fingers, rub it gently
into the pores, working in an upward and
outward direction. Wipe off any superfluous
wax, leaving a little on the skin all night.
In the morning wash off with warm water
and Pilenta soap. The use of the wax not
only feeds the skin, but removes the dead
outer cuticle, giving the fresh young skin
beneath an opportunity to breathe and show
itself.
Beautiful Hair —How to Shampoo.—Whether
the hair is luxuriant and glossy, or thin and
lanky, depends very largely upon the care
bestowed upon it. To keep the hair in good
condition it should be shampooed once in
every two or three weeks. Before shampooing,
massage a little pure olive oil or almond oil
into the scalp, then mix one teaspoonful of
stallax granules in a cup of hot water, fill a
basin with warm water, into which the ends
of the hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax
mixture iuto the scalp in the usual way.
Pdnse and dry by fanning with a palm fan, or
in the open air, if the weather permits. Should
the hair be naturally .very greasy, the oil
massage should be omitted. Stallax can be
bought at any chemist’s in quarter-pound
sealed packages; it keeps indefinitely, and one
package contains sufficient for 25 or 3d
shampoos,
A Greasy Skin and Blackheads.— Blackheads
are absolutely fa'tal to beauty, for they give a
coarse, dirty look to the face which no cos-
metics can ever hide. The way to remove
blackheads quickly and effectively has only
recently been discovered. For many years those
who suffered in this way were restricted in
their diet, denied sweets and had to suffer
other privations. Modern science has, how-
ever, provided a simple, effective and quick
remedy. Dissolve one stymol tablet in a
glass of hot water, and when the effervescence
has subsided, dip a small, soft sponge into the
liquid and bathe the face. Leave for a few
minutes, then dry with a towel, and the black-
heads will come off. For a greasy skin bathe
the face three times a week with stymol and
spray with cold water every morning, using a
vulcanite throat spray.
To Improve the Eyebrows and Eyelashes.—
Long, curling eyelashes make the most in
different eyes look attractive, and while no
one can alter the color, shape or size of the
eyes, all may possess luxuriant eyelashes and
well-shaped eyebrows. To increase the growth
of the eyelashes rub a little mcnnaline into
the roots very gently every night. Mermnliue
is a perfectly harmless and delicate pomade-
like substance which may be obtained at any
chemist’s. For the eyebrows smear a little of
the mennaline on a small soft brush, and
brush into the eyebrows in a straight or
arched direction, according to how you wish
them to grow. A month’s treatment should
see a marked improvement.
ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
CAIRO—No, I am not surprised at hearing
from a reader so far away, as I get letters
from every part of the world. I expect it is
the heat that has caused you to put on weight
so rapidly, and can quite understand how
much it adds to your age. Get a small quan-
tity of clynol berries and eat one after each
meal They will permanently reduce your
weight, are quite harmless, and you need
make no alteration in your diet.
QUEEN BESS.—Thirty is far too young to
have grey hairs, and you must not allow the
trouble to increase. Get two ounces of tamma-
lite. mix it with three ounces of bay rum, and
apply to the hair with a small, soft sponge.
This will restore the grey and fading hair to
its original color.
EMMELINE.—For your mliddy-looking skin
get a tin of mercolised Wax and rub a little
well into tlie face and neck every night. Leave
on till morning, then wash off, using Pilenta
soap and warm water. This will remove the
dead outer skin and leave the complexion soft,
fresh and smooth.
JESSIE.—To remove the hairs from the lip
and chin get an ounce of powdered pheminol
and use as directed. Then apply tekko paste
several times daily for some weeks. This com-
bined treatment will permanently remove all
trace of hair from your face.
FRECKLED LASS.—To prevent the freckles
from appearing use a eleminite lotion. Get an
ounce of eleminite and mix it with four ounces
of warm water, allow to cool, then dab over
the face. This will not only protect the skin
from sunburn and freckles, but will give it a
dainty fresh look far superior to the bloom
imparted by the finest powder. [3

The Red Page
WIDE SPACES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When niv last long-beer has vanished and the truth
is left unsaid ;
When each sordid care is banished from my chair
and from my bed,
Audi tf.y common people sadly murmur: “’Arry
Lawson dead,”
When the man I was denounces all the things that
I was not,
When the true souls stand like granite, while the
souls of liars roi—
When the quids 1 gave are counted, and the trays
I cadged ' forgot;
Shall my spirit see the country that it wrote for
once again?
Shall it see the old selections, and the common street
ar.d lane?
Shall it nass across the Black Soil and across the
Red Soil Plain ?
Shall it see the gaunt Bushwoman “slave until she’*
fit to drop,”
For the distant trip to Sydney, all depending on the
crop ?
Or the twinkling legs of kiddies, running to the
lollie-shop?
Shall my spirit see the failures battling west and
fighting here?
Shall it see the darkened shanty, or the bar-
room dull and drear?
Shall it whisper to the landlord to give Bummer
Smith a beer?
Will they let me out t>f Heaven, or Valhalla, on my
own—
Or the Social Halls of Hades (where I shall not be
alone)
Just to bring a breath of comfort to the hells that
I have known?
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
War Behind the Lines
In ancient wars —wars down to the tail-
end of last century—nobody cared much
what the press said. It wasn’t the people who
made wars ; and the people had little say in
stopping them. Kitchener introduced us to
a new idea. To him, a soldier and nothing
much else, the press was just a damned
nuisance. War-correspondents, who had
come to be held in honor, he simply held in
check; and a system of hard censoring was
introduced.
The great war soon got beyond the
soldiers. This was an affair of nations, not
merely of armies. So, as the nations had to
be organised, the press had to be used. And
more than organised ; nations had sometimes
—as in the case of the United States —to
be captured. Propaganda thus became hardly
second in importance to actual warfare.
On tlfe terrific struggle to capture the
U.S.A. both sides spent millions. The Ger-
mans strove to persuade Americans that this
was no business of theirs; and for 18 months
they succeeded. It was very nearly two
years after the war started that President
Wilson publicly declared that lie didn't know
what the war was all about, and would be
greatly obliged if somebody would tell him.
The Allies were more directly conceimed to
tell the public—the hundred millions of very
mixed people who are the President’s mas-
ters. To them the Allies poured out, not
arguments as to the things that caused the
war. but instances of how Germany was
fighting so that these people might know the
manner of men the Germans were. That, as
every good journalist knows, counted for far
more than academic dissertations upon the
rights of nations, small or great.
How and by whom that campaign of
enlightenment was conducted cannot, of
course, be told here. But there is in N’York
what used to be camouflaged as the British
Pictorial Bureau, which probably knows a
good deal about it. An Oxford
protege of A. J. Balfoups—-and pro-
there ; with him
were other English professors, an English
novelist or two, and a miscellaneous lot of
writers and business men. To do President
Wilson justice, there is no doubt that, as
the Huns disclosed themselves, he became
sincerely anxious that the tide should run
so strongly that he might be justified in
taking prompt and decisive action; hence, for
12 months before the U.S.A. entered the war,
the Allied propagandists in the U.S.A. had
steadily increasing support from official
quarters at Washington. '
The British propaganda work was divided
into three sections—propaganda in (1)
enemy countries, (2) allied and friendly
countries, and (3) at home. Ministers were
appointed to superintend each of these fre-
quently overlapping activities. Northcliffe
—a man of tremendous driving force —had
charge of the work in enemy countries.
Jeaverbrook, the son of a Canadian parson,
who had also become a London newspaper
proprietor, was Minister of Information. In
the offices of these propagandists you might
find men like Newbolt, the poet; Mase-
field ; Arnold Bennett; lan Hay, who
wrote “The First Hundred Thousand,”
and who, by the way, promises to come to
Australia very soon ; Conan Doyle; Mason,
the novelist; and Evelyn Wrench, one of the
Bound Table’s young men, besides dozens
who had been taken out of khaki to do this
special and often delicate job—men of all
ranks from generals to sergeants; one found
there, for instance, a major who had been
in private life a lawyer in Winnipeg; a
colonel who had been Chief of S. African
Mounted Police; and a sergeant who had
been a journalist in the backblocks of N. S.
Wales,
Broadly, their work was twofold—to
hearten the Allies and to demoralise the
enemy. Often it was hoped to hit the two
targets with the one bullet. For instance,
Beaverbrook got the idea of inviting to
Europe representative journalists from every
part of the Empire and the United States,
with the object, first, of whooping up the
spirits of the British people and then of con-
vincing the Germans that the “Dominions”
and the U.S.A. were determined to see the
business through. For three years the Ger-
man people had been sustained on lying pro-
mises that the Allies were crumbling, that
the British “colonies” were full up of the
war (Northcliffe’s secret and other agents
were kept busy for months explaining away
the results- of the Australian Conscription
votes), and, later on, that, though the U.S.A.
were nominally in the war, there was no
earnestness in their effort, no sting in their
punch. The time came when the Hun no
longer believed that the Allies were on the
point of dissolution and the American help
a myth; and when that happened the great
tragedy was really over.
Incidentally, not the least of the work of
the propaganda departments has been to
counteract insidious Hun efforts to make
trouble between the Allies. When, for in-
stance, direct attacks upon the Allies were
no longer tolerated on the part of German
agents in the United States, the Hearst and
other papers began to magnify France at the
expense of Britain. Millions fell into this
trap. Even the American Y.M.C.A. pub-
lished, and for a time put into the hands of
every Sammy who sailed for Europe, a pam-
phlet titled “France, Our Ally.” It was not
openly an attack upon Britain; but no good
American could accept its teachings and
regard Britain as a possible friend of the
great Republic.
P.X.
Mary Gilmore
It is many years since the last collected
volume of Mary Gilmore’s verse charmed
Australian readers. Ilers was a new voice,
a voice full of feeling and sympathy, getting
very close to the heart-song of the world.
The Commonwealth has produced no other
singer quite like her; and her new volume,
“The Passionate Heart,” will make an appeal
to circles of readers far beyond the hounds
of the literary coteries. There is a stir of
real humanity in all that Mary Gilmore
does; and always she warms into enthusiasm
for the great things hidden deep down under
the rags of poverty and misfortune. If her
note is not always clear or very strongly
sustained, it is at any rate different from
that of any other singer and it comes straight
from the heart of one who has known and
suffered and triumphed.
Mary Gilmore walks far from the orthodox
metrical track of the master singers. While
she never quite descends to the chopped prose
misnamed “free verse,” there is an irregu-
larity about much of her work which makes
it less memorable than it might otherwise be.
Very frequently, through this irregularity,
she achieves striking and beautiful results;
sometimes she comes down from the heights
with an apparent fall which is in reality
more attractive than the sustained flight;
but the general effect is, from the merely
metrical point of view, so wild and untamed
that it makes difficult reading.
But this singer’s place is not among the
delicate etchers of the sonnet or the makers
of the old lace that drapes the Spenserian
stanza. Her desire is often to arrest and
startle, to shout large memorable words of
wisdom in the ear; and it is more as the
ancient prophetess that she stands than as
the modern versifier of the book-club and
magazine. She has real things to say; and
she says them with a vigor which, as always
in the case of the prophet, has a tendency to
become a little incoherent through stress of
utterance. But when her trumpet-blast lias
blown itself out she comes back to the sim-
plicities of life very sweetly, and sits down
by the fireside with the old men and the old
women or watches the children play their
noisy games in the dust of the street with a
heart as young as theirs.
“The Passionate Heart” is a good book
and one to treasure. It must be taken as a
whole; for Mary Gilmore is not one easily
represented by quotation. Still, there is
very much that is compelling. This, for
instance, from - “Memorial”
Even the old, long roads will remember
“Hither came They!’’
And the rain .shallriui—-’- ,
And the cne luts hke tears;
Cj.,- mm shine on them all the years,
—“These are the roads they trod’’—
They who are away with God!
Again, from “Two Songs of the Grass”:—
The grass is a warrior.
With his sword, with his spear,
He marches through the world
At the springing of the year.
His border is the mountain,
The ocean runs beside.
He makes the winds his ministers
To bear h.is pennons wide.
Here is a word of our own fighting soldiers
in “The King”:—
The King! The King! The Anzae in his pride
Adown the endless ages shall he ride, *
Upon his pennons faithful set
Names that the world may not forget.
Flanders and France: O God, what flame
Leaps at the naming of a name!
0 God, what kindling echoes ring
When all men stand as at the King!
Not great poetry this, perhaps, but good,
resonant, passionate verse. Here is a typical
poem that wins success from the very verge
of metrical failure :
THE WOMAN.
1 am not very patient,
Yet patient 1 must be.
With him beside my pillow,
And the babe upon my knee.
I am not very patient ;
I would have wings to fly;
Yet am I tied to cradles
Until the day I die.
One is a rocking cradle
(Sleep babe, nor heed alarms!)
But for the other baby
The cradle of my arms.
Strange that I was given
Thoughts that soar to Heaven,
Yet must I sit and keep
Children in their sleep!
Giving in much commonplace phrasing, a
bushel of bad rhymes, an insecurity and
irregularity of step which makes the road
difficult for a marching companion, there re-
mains over and above in Mary Gilmore’s
work so much to be thankful for that all the
rest is forgotten. (The Passionate Heart by
Mary Gilmore, Angus and Robertson, Syd-
ney.)
India in Transition
His Highness the Aga Khan writes
another of the mediocre books so plen-
tifully issued nowadays by Indians about
Indian affairs. It is above the average
in the sense that it is a two-storey book
standing out above a mass of one-storey liter-
ature. but it isn’t a sky-scraper. The really
intelligent Indian writer is a painstaking

In War or Peace, the service
of Oxy- Acetylene is SAVING
'“THE story of the service which Oxy-Acetylene Welding

VARIOV VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sonnet.
At twelve o’clock, when all the house is still,
I look across the darkness of the lawn
Where one tall rowan-tree with berries, chill
Stands in the hedgerow waiting for the
dawn.
Frost tingles in the air; but up the hill,
Crackling along the short, white grass is
drawn
An elfin chariot that the starlights fill
With all the airy jewels of Ivnockawn.
The driver blows a horn and plies his whip,
And nearer, nearer come the silver wheels.
I stand and fear in that fine fellowship
Of wonder —with cold truth upon its
heels.
For well 1 know where the long shadow
steals
To catch the dream on its devouring lip.
N.S.W.
DAVID MCKEE WRIGHT.
love in Idleness.
And do you ever remember?
And can I ever forget?
Though love be a dying ember
My passion endureth yet.
for the love-god’s feast
In the purple east
The sails of our barque I set.
Then youth was a dream of gladness
That moved to a lilting song,
Love a melodious madness,
And virtue a shameful wrong;
All the years too short
For our joyous sport.
And moments apart too long.
The sun to the sea laughed gaily,
The beach was a golden throne
On which you were seated daily,
While I sat apart, alone,
And devised strange ways
To ensnare your gaze—
With a man of your very own!
How can you ever remember?
How can I ever forget
Those weeks of a near November
When, somehow, we never met?
Which, perhaps, is why,
As the days sped by,
I loved you—and love you yet!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
Angelns.
Grey spires, wherein no longer hang those
bells
That rang the dewy springtime’s fairy peals—
Those bells that shone like silver, sunset-
kissed,
Or budding roses wreathed in morning mist—
Bare are your belfries now that twilight
steals
About your turrets, autumn asphodels.
Green spires, that freshen as the day declines,
And grow and strengthen with the falling
year,
Though to the eye no bell your storeys bear,
The fancy feigns them hung with bells of air
Wherein your thousand bronze-bright tongues
appear
Ringing their carillons, ye Autumn pines.
Ah. ghostly asphodels ! Ah, young pines gay !
Bell-towers of fancy, in autumnal dream,
Yours is a world of music, heard and seen,
Thought and remembered in these shadows
green, >
Where sound and silence, sway and stillness
seem
All a soft angelus of closing day.
SPAIN.
JAMES WEBB.
First Kiss.
Seventeen lost her heart to you,
All on a morning gold and blue,
With a hey ho! hey ho!
For the tune that the blue bells rang.
Up and away before she knew,
Her heart ran over the hill to you,
With a tra la la la, la la la,
While the wind sang, and the birds sang.
The grasses still were wet with dew,
And she was shy, and so were you,
With a hey ho ! hey ho!
For the clover red in the fields.
High on the hills when the day was new
Her mouth was red, and her eyes were
blue,
Oh it’s tra la la la, la la la,
Sing the birds when the maiden yields.
All on a morning gold and blue,
I know who lost her heart to you !
N.S.W.
NORA MCAULIFFE.
Voice from an Ash-tray.
A half-burned brand from a funeral pyre,
That once glowed clear with a sacred fire,
A flower crushed in Life’s sordid mire,
And the crime of a broken trust;
Drifting smoke from a cigarette,
Red lips with wine and kisses wet,
Lips that avowed they would never forget,
And for me—a little grey dust.
A poppy that flamed on an altar white,
In the still strange hush of the velvet night,
When the beat of our hearts was a long
delight,
And the Future behind us thrust;
Stars that were framed in a casement high,
Rale, pale stars as the dawn drew nigh,
Sad, sad stars as we said good-bye
And for me —a little grey dust.
N.S.W.
HORACE HALLORAN.

Voi. 40.-No. 2031.
THURSDAY, JANUARY 16, 1919.
Wab-time Pkice, Sd.
REFUSE SHOT HERE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“'When asked if there was not a danger in sending the Kaiser to Holland, Count von Bernstorff replied: ‘Where in hell were we to send him?’”
Now where in hell could this person go?
All gates were shut that we used to know;
He had barred them with weeping and blood and
woe.
The valiant Swiss, on their mountains high,
Looked out with a cold, unkindly eye;
They had suffered much and they well knew why.
The Danes, in the midst of the world’s alarms,
Stood to their guarded posts in arms;
They knew the Prussian in all his charms.
Across the Russian frontier red,
Where his own loud mouth had the tumult bred,
A king must travel without his head.
The gates were barred, not a door was seen;
The sea was not what the sea had been,
Tor mutiny stirred on the submarine.
So they tossed him over the low Dutch wall—
I rayer, bravado and crown and all —
To wait till the dirt-box man should call.
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents a/ this number is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned or preserved unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, . those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —trill be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 16. 1919.
THE SHAPELESS, NAMELESS WAR.
Whether the new war will be big and long
like the old one, or whether it will be a
mere untidy scramble, more annoying than
dangerous, no prophet is available to. tell.
It may be just about ending through inter
nal collapse, or it may be only beginning. It
is intended to be a world war and the in-
tention may come off. No one knows for
certain what the parties will be. Russia, or
the biggest part of Russia, appears certain.
If the Bolshevik element gets the upp t band
in Germany the great Hun country will
almost assuredly be the ally of Russia, and
there is wild talk of the Hun and the Slav
fighting side by side on the Rhine for the
ruin of civilisation. The present boundaries
of the enemy are undecided, but they seem to
be somewhere about Poland, in the West,
seeing that Huns and Bolsheviks are making
a common annoyance against the northern
section of Paderewski’s republic, while
Bolsheviks and Ukrainians are .doing the
same further south. There are mixed forces
scrapping in the north near the \Y h-.te feea,
but the withdrawal of the Americans, who
view the Bolshevik mix-up as something out-
side their jurisdiction, may weaken . the
Allied position there. There are more mixed
forces scrapping in Siberia, but the with-
drawal of the Americans and Japanese
doesn’t improve the position. The mixed
Franeo-Roumanian handful, rumored to be
in Bessarabia, and the isolated Czecho-
slovaks on the Volga, and the dubious arma-
ments of the few little republics which exist
as yet mostlv on paper, and a small British
and assorted force in the Baku region about
complete the picture. It suggests a number
of flies attacking the outskirts of a cheese.
The nominal headquarters of the R ipublic
of Anarchy are known; the real head-
quarters are uncertain. There is no coherent
Government to negotiate with, and the con-
duct of what calls itself a Government, but
is really a slaughter-house, makes negotia-
tion impossible. The first condition of any
kind of peace would be the restoration of
some semblance of internal calm, so that
the lives and assets of foreigners would be
safe. To do that the existing rulers would
have to hang or imprison their principal
followers and make them disgorge much
plunder, and the rulers probably wouldn’t
last a week on these conditions. It would
be putting a tiger on a vegetable diet, where-
upon he Would eat his master for the sake
of meat. Even the amount of temporary
repose which would allow a Peace Commis-
sion to visit Lenin and Trotsky in their
lair and inquire what had happened to
French, British and other alien subjects
might give the sane elements of the popula-
tion time to organise and make an end of
Lenin and Trotsky. Bolshevism, accord-
ing to the rules of the game, has to conquer
Europe and spread its doctrine of the de-
struction of things far and wide, or die an
uncomfortable death.
What sort of army the Republic of An-
archy may raise if luck comes its way is a
matter of the wildest speculation. There
is talk of 1.000,000 troops already in exist-
ence, and the number seems really small.
The discipline that sweeps men into the army
appears to be the only strenuous discipline
in the country. The army is well-paid (in
paper), and apparently, though the military
training is severe, it doesn’t forfeit its privi-
leges of loot. And it is something of a place
of refuge from the tribunals which order
executions promiscuously. There is loose
talk —perhaps not so loose as it appears—
of an army of 3,000,000 or 4,000,000 to he
ready when the snows melt. This force is
supposed to be partly officered by capable
Germans, some of whom dare not go home
for obvious reasons, and to be armed, not
only with the ddbris of the old Russian army,
but with munitions which the retreating. Hun
forces left behind them, not altogether in an
unfriendly spirit. Should the Bolshevik or
Spartacus party come on top in Germany
the new Republic of Anarchy may spread a
long way towards the Rhine. The obstruc-
tion of Paderewski’s very unfinished repub-
lic may prove formidable or it may not.
The Hun army is supposed to .be largely dis-
banded, but disbanded armies sometimes
come together again to a considerable extent
under sufficient inducement. It can’t have
been wholly disarmed. The Republic of
Anarchy has an attractive programme to
offer to the Hun soldier who comes back,
demoralised by years spent in the joyous
wrecking of cities and in riot among the
women of an enslaved population, to settle
down to the plain hard work he has for-
gotten, and the bitter grind of paying off
an indemnity. He is offered some sort of
chance to collect any indemnity that may be
collectable for himself—not for the Allies.
He is to get square with his old oppressors
the junkers, the aristocrats, the imperial
clan and the bureaucracy. He is to have
another shot at the hated enemy who defeated
him, and to fight only on one front this
time, and with a bigger—possibly better —
ally than Austria behind him. Further, he
expects to find friends in the enemy’s camp
-—friends that didn’t amount to much when
he represented the worst kind of imperialism.
In a new capacity as the upholder of the
frantic banner of liberty he will appeal to
the new crowd which runs the Australian
Labor party, and whose organ is already
Bolshevik pure and simple; also to the
I.WAV. and “Direct Action’’ element all the
world over. In other words, he will call
on the push of the globe. For the first
time the globe-push has a political centre
and a Push Government as a rallying point.
* * * *
The whole business, it has been already
mentioned, may. collapse as does a bubble,
or it may grow. If it grows to the bound-
aries that now seem possible it must be
extinguished inside these borders, else it
will spread further. It is the fire in the
adjacent paddock which takes no notice of
fences. The war that is just about ended
was fouaht to impose on the world outside
Hun-land the new Hun civilisation. It was
to be a brutish system of Hun slave-owners
and their innumerable slaves, but still it
was some kind of civilisation. The
war that may be just beginning is worse,
inasmuch as it will be for the finish of
civilisation, the wholesale slaying of “intel-
lectuals.” and the re-establishment of chaos.
Out of the chaos a new class of despots would
doubtless evolve in the course of centuries,
but it is a great deal to go through for so
very little. Still, the Republic of Anarchy
expects to have a smaller alliance to contend
with than took the field in the last disturb-
ance, for two of the big Allies show signs
of regarding the riot as a mere domestic and
internal affair which doesn’t concern them.
There will be no marine outrages to arouse
neutrals this time, for the Republic of
Anarchy won’t go to sea. The remaining
Allies are very, very weary. The distances
that may be involved seem almost illimitable.
They are the distances which swallowed up
the power of Napoleon and Charles XII.
before him, and which almost swallowed up
Darius I. before either of them. It would
possibly be found, by the time the war got
there, that the destruction of railways had
made them almost the same raw, crude,
boggy, featureless distances that Darius
found them, save for the fact that the air-
ship is a factor that was wanting half a
millennium B.c. Probably it is capable of
sdving civilisation by itself. But even with
it taken into account the shapeless, nameless
war is a matter of large possibilities.
RACE.
At tlie British elections the three princi-
palities, Man and the two Channel Island
dominions, of course didn’t vote; they have
Parliaments of their own, on the ground that
the populations are non-English. In fact,
the Channel Islands even have coinages of
their own, English is only “permissible” in
their legislative debates, and George V.
reigns there on the plea that he is Duke
of Normandy, which he isn’t. But if these
places had voted it would have made no
difference. The outstanding fact is that
the Sinn Feiners —the Irish irreconcilables —•
are now the most numerous party outside
the Government, and would presumably be
the Opposition if they chose to take their
seats. Pursuing a new line of action in-
stead, they propose to stay away and create
a Parliament of their own, which will have
no legal status to begin with, but hopes to
acquire one. The long waiting for autonomy
after the fashion of that enjoyed by Tas-
mania and the Isle of Man has had its natu-
ral result. A new generation has arisen
which demands, not merely autonomy, but
separation. However, it would probably
compromise at the old figure. Most business
men will accept a reduction when trade
is slack.
* * * *
There is, or was, a certain similarity be-
tween the Irish and Scotch position. Eng-
land decided almost in the same hour to ex-
tend the blessing of good government to
France, Scotland. Wales, Ireland and Man.
France was too large a proposition, though
it took 100 years’ fighting to prove it. Wales
was practically taken after 346 years’ argu-
ment, which for so small and disorganised a
place was a wonderful amount of argument.
The kingdom of Man was only a selection.
Scotland was irreconcilable. It was a curi-
ous little spot divided between two popula-
tions which seldom spoke to each other ex-
cept in bitterness and in different languages.
When the kings moved from the North to the
South, and began to talk in bad English in-
stead of Gaelic, and posed as new Saxon-Nor-
mans instead of old Scots, they gave a chance
for the formation of a new northern State.
So the Lords of the Isles ran an intermittent
show of their own, and for very many years
the nominal kings of a very small country
very seldom ventured into the top half of
that country. It was a land of dissension.
It never evolved real Parliamentary Govern-
ment, though it had erratic assemblies of
barons with ill-defined authority. Great
nobles were a law- to themselves —in fact,
one family of them practically ran the coun-
try for the better part of two centuries.
When Elizabeth was making order in Eng-
land the peers of Scotland were still prig-
ging their monarchs and ruling in their
names. One very great battle between the
army of the Mikado, James Stewart of
Lothian, and the Tycoon, Donald of the
Isles, nearly destroyed the army of the lower
half of Scotland and yet left the position
unfixed.
* * * *
So this harassed State fought a wild cat
fight, with French assistance, for nearly
300 years to avoid the advantages of orderly
English government. It even fought the dis-
astrous battle of Pinkie to avoid a peaceful
union being brought about by a marriage
between its young queen and the heir to the
English throne. However, a certain affa-
bility began to supervene at last, because
the half of Scotland which Europe recog-
nised—the Edinburgh half, not the Inverness
half—was largely of the same race as the
English, so there was a community of inter-
ests —a community not to be found in Ire-
land. In fact, the southern Scots were to a
great extent fln intruding or Belfast element
in Scotland, only they were a big element,
while Belfast is a small one. They were
also drawn to the English by a partial
similarity of religion, which isn’t the case
with the Irish. But even then Scotland was
only saved from being another Ireland —a
place which England was bound to conquer
in the end, but which would have had an
undying hatred in consequence—by an acci-
dent. The Tudors having died out, there
was an opening for a king, and Scotland
was appeased by Its sovereign being allowed
in theory to annex the old big enemy where
his descendants reign to-day. Yet even then
the old problems weren’t settled. It was in
Captain Cook’s day that the last army of
Scotch Sinn Feiners invaded England in
their petticoats and made their final effort
to alter the line of succession. If Scotland
had been as unanimous then as Ireland is
to-day they might have succeeded. After
that the Scotch irreconcilables mostly went
abroad like the Irish, but as they only repre-
sented half a nation they had no status. They
were the illiterate half and wore skirts.
* * * *
Racial questions are of very long con-
tinuance and they can’t be got rid of by
saying fiercely that they are wicked and
shouldn’t exist. Considering the rather poor
response the French Canadians made to the
war call as compared' with the rest of their
countrymen, Britain begins to doubt if there
has been complete assimilation even there.
The Boers secured liberal terms for them-
selves : one of their old capitals was made
the administrative centre of the South
African Union; and a Boer general has
become Prime Minister of South Africa, just
as a Scotchman became King of England.
Yet there is a very considerable unsatisfied
party still in evidence. Ireland has not
been given up as a bad job like Normandy
and the United States, or been mollified
by the present of a kingship as the Scotch
were; or by a Prime Ministership and the
establishment of the administrative capital
and Government officers and the ruler’s
palace in its territory as happened to the
Boers; or by local self-government as
happened to the province of Quebec, where
an overwhelming French Catholic majority—•
a conquered majority—rules over a Pro-
testant minority. It hasn’t even the polite
compliment of having its own Sultan after
the fashion of Egypt. In fact, it is a
conquered country without frills, except that
it has a representation in the British Parlia-
ment, which is as powerless for the purpose
Ireland desires as were the representatives
of Prussian Poland and Alsace-Lorraine in
the German Parliament. The large Greek
population in Turkey also has representation
in the Turkish Parliament when that inter-
mittent organisation is on its job, and in
the days of Turkish domination they almost
ran the empire at times, being employed in
all manner of public billets for which the
thick-headed Ottoman was unsuited. Still,
the Greek question didn’t settle any more
than the Irish one does. The Irish are like
the Jews —scattered folk with a minority
at headquarters and a great majority abroad.
In each case—speaking of Judah as it was
till last year—headquarters are ruled by
a nation that isn’t their own, and which
gives them much the same kind of govern-
ment that it gives to its own people. In
each case they want to own headquarters;
the Jew in London and the Irishman in
London agree on that point. So the island,
in which Britain is trying to do what it
never really managed to do anywhere else,
is a problem. It is such an interesting
problem that England would be a homeless,
bereaved sort of place without it. And the
Sinn Feiners propose to. put a new aspect
on it so that life may not become stagnant.
AFTER ALL, IS ANARCHY SO BAD?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No country lias yet of its own sober deter-
mination chosen anarchy as its mode of
misgovernment. (It is possible to make
this statement because the Northern Terri-
tory is not strictly a country, has never
yet known sober determination, and because
the thing imposed upon it from without
looks quite as anarchistic as anything
bursting up from within.) It is true that
Israel, in moderately early times, had a
theocracy, with the general government
vested in the occasional person who at odd
times might rise up in some unlooked for
spot and claim the divine right to be boss
as far as his list could travel or his voice
penetrate. But anarchy, the great mis-
understood light of which democracy is the
pale reflection, has never yet got beyond
the first experimental stage of a badly-aimed
bomb and newspaper abuse.
Naturally newspapers abuse anarchy. I
a country' where it was firmly established
there could be no politics, no crime, no
resistance of law and order, no talk of
reform, no men in the public eye—in fact,
no food at all for the daily press. The
latest poem and the price of potatoes would
be about the only subjects in which the
public would take an intelligent interest;
and as potatoes and poetry are only a
moderate basis for journalism, the press
keeps up a steady yell of “Gon save the
King”—whether the king be a right-diviner
or a mere person who loses the letters in-
trusted to him in the post office.
Nevertheless, anarchy is an excellent
newspaper heading, and none the less useful
bcause the right-thinking shudder at it, the
general person doesn’t know what it means
and the newspapers themselves resolutely
refuse to say what it means.
In any case, their silence doesn’t matter
much, for any regular definition of anarchy
can be couched in such terms that its beauty
is wholly hidden and its value depreciated.
This is due to our habit of setting an alto-
gether unjustifiable market price upon
people who merely obstruct the human
traffic. Governments are always quoted at
the top of the list as the most precious
of human possessions; and, as the anarchist
professes to have no use for Governments,
it follows that he is a depraved person
without the inner light. But is he?
Every man who loves a Government
tries to make excuses fv;' it by point-
ing out that, as the animals go, it
is no worse than its fellows. What would
be thought, though, of any man who, in
train, tram, church or pub, declared without
apology or hedging that any of the herd of
Governments now grazing on Australia was
really good? What would be thought of a
newspaper that said it without equivocation?
The mere fact that such questions can be
stated with no doubt as to the answer is
proof that Governments are bad things.
Try it another way. If all the Governments
that Australia has ever known were put in
one heap and offered in exchange for a single
bag of well-grown potatoes, who would be
willing to give the potatoes for them?
Clearly, then,.,Governments are bad; and the
public mind, as voiced by the public mouth,
is quite agreed about it.
This gets us down to the position taken
up by the anarchist. He maintains that
Governments are bad, and that he doesn’t
want them. The general person, on the other
hand, admits that they are bad, but thinks he
does want them. On the face of it, the people
who want bad things would seem inferior in
intelligence to those who don’t want them. So
the anarchist, dissociated for the moment
from the bomb that he can’t throw straight,
would seem to have at least one point in his
favor.
But the intelligent anarchist goes much
further than a mere denunciation of Govern-
ments. He takes the positive ground that
man has of recent years grown big enough to
look after himself without being governed at
all. He points to the force of public opinion
as an upholder of any number of decent laws
that need never be written ; he observes old
lady Grundy seated in her parlor in every
township, exercising by right of her pointed
remarks the power of a score of large police-
men.. Man, he claims, is a pretty good citi-
zen if you leave him alone ; and almost any
man thus left alone makes a better showing
than any Government, however closely
watched by press and people. True, there
is crime, but the crime is always most fre-
quent where the policeman’s foot is most
constantly heard ; and the policeman is the
power of the. Government in uniform. The
criminal is, in fact, a pretty fixed quantity
in the community, in spite of the Govern-
ment ; and the anarchist would ask the
policeman to stand aside and allow the pub-
lic to catch the prey he so regularly misses.
Philosophically the anarchist can make out
a good case if he gets a hearing; but when
it comes to practical revolution he always
mishandles things. Once the bomb really
goes straight and extinguishes the Govern-
ment a vacuum is created ; and as nature
abhors a vacuum, something rushes in to fill
it. That something is usually another Gov-
ernment worse than the first; and the whole
thing has to be begun over again. Consid-
ering what a bad shot the philosophic anar-
chist generally is, this makes progress rather
slow; and so anarchy and reform keep about
level running, with only a snail sprint now
and again to hearten the onlookers.
What this old world needs is some kind
of practical compromise. Anarchy has
so good an end in the argument and so bad
an end in the actual carrying of it out
that it might be well just to accept as much
of its propaganda as the people who are
only half-anarchists could carry out without
creating a vacuum that the Devil would
rush in to fill. Australia has seven Govern-
ments in full bearing, as ■well as a whole
forest of other things remarkably like weedy
growths of the same tree, that carry all
manner of deadly sins to partial fruition.
Why not use the anarchist’s axe as far as
it is practicable and safe? If the argument
for any government is tolerably w T eak. the
defence to be put up for over-government
must be calamitous in its futility. Our
present system is a perpetual temptation to


the real whole-hogging anarchist who can so
readily create the real bomb. No sane
jury in Australia would convict a patriot
for destroying a Government or two, or grub-
bing out with explosives a few brace of over-
lapping municipalities. If the half-anarchist
doesn’t do his work with moderation and
judgment the whole-anarchist will do it
without discretion to the music of massed
bands.
P.O.M.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Honors.”
The cabled intimation that many hundreds
of 0.8.E.’s have been granted recently
draws attention again to the demented state
of the “honors” market. Every colonel and
major of a certain seniority has a recognised
right to a D.S.O. If he fails to aet it there
is an implication that he is either a dud or
a drunk. On the other hand —and here is
where the outrageous unfairness of the sys-
tem comes in—it may merely be that he is
unpopular with his superiors ; or that he is
less popular than another and no better man ;
or that someone else has more “pull” than he
has. Military Crosses are dealt out on a
somewhat similar basis. After the adjutant,
the M.O. and one or two others at Brigade
or Battalion H.Q. have been provided for,
there are a certain number available for deeds
of gallantry and good work generally. But
not many. The astonishing explanation is
that 80 per cent, of all officer decorations
have, for years past, gone to the Staff. The
Prince of Wales is an M.C. Through no
fault of his own he never had a chance to
earn it in the line. The military baker at
Boulogne is, or was, an M.C. When our ex-
peditionaries come back we shall gaze admir-
ingly at colonels and majors who are D.S.O.’s
or M.C.’s, or both, but who have not, in their
whole four years of service, been nearer the
firing-line than a motor-transport billet or a
base camp. We shall gaze also upon many
hundreds of undecorated officers. A large pro-
portion of these men are undecorated for no
other reason than that non-combatant officers
were in a position to snavel the distinctions
that the combatants should have had. Here
are two cases:—
“H” enlisted in 1914, won his commission on the
Peninsula, lost his left eye, and was invalided back
to Australia. Returning to the Front, he was
wounded in the arm at Bullecourt. lie was badly
wounded in the leg in August, 1918 —being at the
time a captain—and boarded for Australia. No
decoration.
“M” left Sydney as a gunner in 1915, lost his right
eye in France; came back again and was wounded
in the hand ; came back again and was gassed ; came
back again and did good work as acting-battery cap-
tain. No decoration.
Yet all the adjutants in the Reserve or
training-camps in England werj 0.8.E.’d,
or “mentioned,” or both, about six mouths
ago. With few exceptions they ware men
who had seen a little service and then, de-
ciding that the life was too thrilling, devel-
oped old-standing football kr.ees or sprains
or what not and got back to England. There
are cases of 0.8.E.’s with six weeks’ undis-
tinguished service in or near the line, and
three years’ comfortable adjutanting at a
minimum distance of 50 miles from it. An
order appeared three months ago that in
future 0.5.0.’s were only to be awarded for
acts of gallantry; and at about the same
time 0.8. E. began to be awarded to com-
batants. The effect was to inestimably in-
crease the value of the thousands of D.S.O.’s
which had already been earned in comfort-
able and perfectly safe Base jobs, and to
give a signification to the 0.8. E. decoration
which is ridiculously inappropriate. The
“ration” system of decorations applies, to a
limited extent, to -the honors that belong to
the rank and file —that is to say, so many
M.M.’s and D.C.M.’s are periodically allotted
to brigades. But, since staff-officers cannot
wear these decorations, an M.M. or a D.C.M.
is worth, on the average, about 50 times
more, as a guarantee of the possession of
soldierly virtues, than an M.C. or a D.S.O.
And yet an M.C. or a D.S.O. genuinely
earned (meaning earned outside an office) is
one of the world’s proudest distinctions. It
is a muddled and—from another aspect—a
sordid business. The cumulative effect of
all of it is to put military “honors” in the
same low-comedy category as social ones.
There are only, two distinctions in the Brit-
ish Empire which are not under suspicion—
the V.C. and the O.M.
The Defence Department in Blighty.
Down to a very few months ago—that
is to say, for three years after there had
been a big Australian army in Europe—
there was not a general Australian hospital
in England. There were only several of
the variety known as auxiliary. Away back
in the beginning of things a very exalted
person is understood to have desired that,
“for the good of the Empire,” the Aus-
tralian sick and wounded should be scattered
among the other sick and wounded. The
intention, of course, was plain; these Aus-
tralians, who were suspected of being rather
too much Australian and not sufficiently
Imperial, were to be put into an environ-
ment which it was thought might change
their spots. High Australian authority
accepted this view—it would be interesting
to discover exactly who was responsible, for
in the last resort one man always is—and
thereafter the sick or wounded Digger sent
over from France was entrained to the north
of England, or the west of England, or the
south of England, or the east of England—
to any one of a hundred hospitals, most of
them formerly private homes not built for
hospitals and not used exclusively by Aus-
tralians. Though 50 Australians might come
over the Channel together, it might happen
that not two of them would go to the same
ward. Now, it is bad enough that sick and
wounded Diggers should be separated from
the men of their own army, the men of
their own land. Even if it were decided
that it was advisable to mix up Tommies
and Canadians and Diggers and Maori-
landers. the experiment should not be made
while they are sick, and least capable of
bearing separation from their own proper
“cobbers.” But there is another considera-
tion which ought to have long ago led to
the abandonment of the foolish and un-Aus-
tralian if not anti-Australian experiment.
These mixed hospitals to which Australian
soldiers have been sent were not only for
the most part not built for hospitals,' but
they are largely staffed by V.A.D.’s and
other untrained women; whereas the Aus-
tralian auxiliarv hospitals, at which the
Australian soldier arrived as a rule only
after he was more or less convalescent, are
staffed by trained Australian nurses. Surely
nothing could be either more utterly absurd
or more unfair both to Australian nurses
and to Australian soldiers, as well as to the
Australian doctors who manned these auxil-
iary hospitals, than such a system as this.
To urge that for some occult irtilitary reason
it has been unavoidable is in this case at
least no good, because, as already indicated,
Maoriland has all along had a general
hospital in England to which all or
practically all its sick and wounded have
been at once entrained, and where they have
had the _ benefits, not only of association
with their mates, but also of the care and
attention of the trained nurses of their own
country. Australia knows pretty well, by
this time, what Defence administration at
home has been like. When it learns more
fully what Australian Defence administra-
tion in Blighty has been like, it won’t be
inclined to alter any unflattering opinion it
has already formed.
Soldiers’ Grievances.
The point of view of a good-Australian
officer: —•
The soldiers have a tremendous number of griev-
ances—far more, really, than most of them even
dimly suspect. But it is impossible to live amongst
them for any time without finding that the earliest
and loudest complainants are invariably the second-
rate men—the lead-swingers, the loafers and the
illiterates. They concentrate, naturally, on fancied
wrongs, or on wrongs that don’t matter so much, to
the exclusion of the deeper evils. We had about the
average quota on the carrier in which I returned.
The food and accommodation were fair—as such
things go on troopships. But owing to the in-
fluenza, no one was allowed ashore between Liverpool
and Port Phillip; and on arriving there we were
ordered into quarantine at Portsea. The agitators
saw a chance to exercise their voices, practise stump
oratory, and gain advertisement. They persuaded
the others,, for a while, to refuse to land and go
through the inhalation process. On ether ships at
about the same time men went on shore against the
0.0.’s orders and those of the Quarantine authorities.
The influenza scare has died down now, and so
there is no harm in mentioning the matter; but if,
six weeks ago, it had been widely advertised that
soldiers were either jibbing at prophylactic treat-
ment, or, still worse, were going ashore without
being passed “clean” by the medical authorities—-
well, the tendency would have been to make the
returned man the reverse of popular in his native
land.
There is a general allegation that the Sardinia
was a very bad ship. What were the stated
grievances? That there was no fish. The writer
has been on seven troopships—including transports,
hospital ships and carriers—and about four-fifths
of the fish provided in all of them was either heaved
overboard or left on the men’s plates. It is no
deprivation to be without ship-board fish. The
bread on the Sardinia, it is said, was bad. So is all
English bread nowadays, The food was badly cooked,
and there was a shortage of fresh meat and fresh
vegetables. At the time of writing the finding of the
Court of Inquiry which is dealing with the Sardinia
case has not been published. But this is certain:
whichever way the Court finds, every , soldier will
know that the allegations were all true —that, in
fact, they only represented a fraction of the truth.
Give -all that in, and I still say that it is bad
policy, from the standpoint of the A.1.F., for the
soldiers to squeal on that sort of grievance.
Rightly or wrongly (for my part I am sure
rightly) the public has formed an ideal of the Aus-
tralian Expeditionary. It visualises him as a big
man—strong, good-humored, patient and philosophic.
The people who have stayed at home remember,
when they think of him, the privations of Gallipoli,
and the horrors of the front-line trenches in the
Ypres sector and on the Somme. The hero returns
(radiantly healthy in most cases) and the first
direct news his admirers get of him is that he is
kicking up a row about the food he has been having
during the previous 10 weeks. It is not in the pic-
ture. As regards the soldiers’ friends it comes
as a painful anti-climax; to his enemies it is a
looked-for opportunity.
What is needed is a rigid economy in the stating
of grievances. To get that the professor of “uplift”
requires to be discouraged. He is often a well-
meaning, earnest lad, no doubt; but he lacks
balance, knowledge, a standard of comparison—
almost everj'thing which a leader should possess.
He thinks that he is serving his comrades’ interests,
at the same time as he is enjoying an unwonted
bathe in the limelight. In reality he is doing them
more harm than those Queenslanders who hoot sol-
diers in the streets. If the papers are going to in-
discriminately print details of all soldiers’ grievances,
the men should take particular care that only the big
ones are voiced. It will be no good to those who
are due to return in the coming months to find that,
while more fish or less bully-beef is on their menus,
yet, as an outcome of it, they have lost in popularity
and respect in the community.

Marrying Maud Blithers.
The rumor is persistent that the Prince of
Wales may marry a United jStates girl—
possibly Maud Blithers, daughter of Wil-
liam W. Blithers, of Chicago. Presum-
ably he will marry somebody, it being a
habit, though the British royal family is so
numerous that it wouldn’t die out even if
one prospective king remained single. Also
marriage is a duty among the wearers of
thrones. The first business of an hereditary
monarchy is to be hereditary, and it can’t
be legally hereditary unless it marries. The
last unmarried sovereign that England pos-
sesses was a bachelor girl, and the experiment
isn’t likely to be repeated. (In this connection
it is curious to note that though there were
only five Tudor sovereigns, and two of them
were females and two died single, they had
seven wives among them. Yet the direct
line died out.) The old British habit of
marrying commoners ended with Henry
VIII., who would marry almost everybody,
but at one time it was fairly common all
over Europe. In later years, however, king-
ship has been almost a close corporation,
and through much intermarriage some mon-
archs have been growing weedy and some
have grown mad. They didn’t get back to
the old Egyptian habit of brother marrying
sister when outside royalties were scarce,
but they did get to marriages between uncle
and niece, which was almost as bad. Very
rarely a Bernadotte or a KArageorgevitch
brought new blood into the circle. Now
there is a feeling that royalties shouldn’t
become any smaller or more closely related
than they are. Most of the tribe have fallen
into disrepute through keeping too far from
the firing-line. And with Germany, Austria,
Kussia and Bulgaria barred as rabbit war-
rens of marriageable princes and princesses,
and Holland at a discount as half-German,
and Portugal gone out of the royalty busi-
ness, there are hardly any mates of the old
order available. Either the Prince of Wales
or somebody like him will be driven to marry
Maud Blithers, of the United States. She
has this advantage over Maud Possum, of
Australia—she isn’t a subject. Probably it
will take some time before the idea of a
king marrying his own subject is assimi-
lated. And Maud Blithers isn’t a com-
moner, for where there is no peerage nobody
is commoner than anybody else. Maud, in
fact, is regal in a way, like Carmen, of San-
tiago, or Dolores, of Buenos Ayres, for old
man Blithers is a presumptive ruler, while
old man Possum isn’t. So the republics
may suddenly take a top seat in the mar-
riage mart. They are full of possible mon-
archs.

Sheepvvash. . . .Free.
“Sheepwash... .free."’— Customary tariff item.
A lengthy correspondence about sheep-dip
is interesting as showing the complete unim-
portance of Australian industry in the
opinion of Hughes, Watt, Millfn, Jensen
and Co. The correspondence began the year
before last, when an Australian firm was
approached with the information that the
export of dip from England would almost
certainly be prohibited owing to shortage of
supplies and lack of shipping space. There-
fore the firm — Leggo’s, of Melbourne —was
asked if it could undertake to meet the Aus-
tralian demand, which involved the expendi-
ture of many thousands in new plant. There
was no offer of a duty to ensure that the
demand would last and keep the new plant
employed after the war was over; in fact,
there was no offer of any kind, and no assur-
ance save that there would be an opening for
business while the war lasted. Indeed, some-
one seems to have had the amazing effrontery
to suggest that the firm, being a mere Aus-
tralian manufacturing concern and thus plain
dirt in the view of a Foreign-trade Ministry,
should enter into a bond with a cash forfeit
attached to supply, at much expense in plant,
the demand which might stop any day; but
this idea was dropped. No one dreamed of
telling the English manufacturer that his
goods would be shut out unless he entered
into a bond to keep up regular supplies at a
fixed price instead of using this market as
a convenience. The importing interest isn’t
treated that way. The rest of the story,
spread over a multitude of documents,
amounts to this. The Australian firm spent
money by request to meet the demand which
it was assured the war had created ; but its
informants were either ignorant and incompe-
tent or plain humorists, for imported dip came
in as usual. The Imperial Government didn’t
stop exports, and shipping space was found
somewhere and somehow. Ministers and
Government officials of various sorts were
artlessly surprised at the curious situation,
but mild astonishment was all they
contributed to the problem, and the Austra-
lian firm found that astonishment wasn’t
saleable. There was long notice that large
shipments—apparently even larger than usual
—were coming from England, so that a
costly plant put up by invitation to supply
all Australia was bound to be at least half
useless. A cablegram announcing that a
solid duty was going to be imposed promptly
would have put the situation right, but it
wasn’t sent by the . importing Government,
and no duty was imposed. The position
looks like a particularly bad joke played on
Australian industry to show that it can’t
expect to be more than a warming-pan for
the importer. In fact, the affair went
beyond that and took on the aspect of an
elaborate hoax—which might be amusing
to the perpetrators but didn’t appeal to the
sufferers. The firm, however, was partly
to blame. It should have sold its works
to the Government as the Shaw Wireless
did.
10,000 Tons of Sugar.
It is mentioned that the Australian Gov-
ernment has made a special purchase of
10,000 tons of sugar from abroad, to sup-
plement the ordinary private-enterprise pur-
chases, which in some years run to 100,000
tons and more. This seems a curious circum-
stance for a country which prides itself on
being, above all things, a producer of raw
material. Only for one year in its long
record did Australia raise enough of this
simple, indispensable vegetable to supply the
local market and leave an imperceptible frag-
ment for export. Possibly this is because
sugar can’t be dumped on board ship in a
natural state like wool or wheat or carcases
of beasts. It requires a certain amount of
manufacturing which, so the importation
and loan crowd teaches, is a thing to be
°i^A C this country paid away
±l.l l u.uut) for imported sugar. In 191(1 it
the amount was
tl.bl i ,UOU. rhere were years when *he posi-
tion was better, but it was always bad In
addition there were imports of molasses and
syrup. The country suitable for sugar-cane
isn t nearly all occupied, but the industrv
appears to be petrified. As for the growth
ot beet-sugar, it is practically extinct, if it
can be said to have ever lived. Yet beet
raising is a white man’s work in all the
countries where it is done—France. Ger-
many, Austria, Russia and Italy for ex-
amples—and it is now the chief source of
the world s sugar supply. i n n. g
the growth of sugar-beet was started in 1874
but it was ‘chucked” in disgust because the
hist roots grown yielded only 7 per cent.
of sugar. When the same vegetable was
tried in Germany the yield was a mere 5
per cent., but the patient Hun worked on.
and with better cultivation and hotter
machinery raised the yield to 1-1 per cent.,
which was remarkably profitable. Excited
by this news the Victorian Government made
experiments and got over 18 per cent, and
some private experiments ran to 22 and 2-1
per cent., at which rate the industry seemed
to have a fortune in it. But the Australian
farmer, or rather dragger of simple old roots
out of the earth, never took to the new-
fangled crop. He has such a dread of new-
fangled crops that he hasn’t struck anything
really new since the convict days. Coffee
once got so far as to cover 547 acres.
Then it faded till, when last heard of, it
represented 91. acres. Victoria grew 1758
acres of hops in 1884; in 1916 the whole
Commonwealth raised 1515 acres and £50.000
was paid for imported hops. Tobacco stood
for 6641 acres in 1889 and was down to
1906 acres in 1916. Victoria was the one
State which experimented seriously with
sugar-beet. It got as far as to grow 1093
acres of the vegetable, and then slumped to
461 acres. Arrowroot never ran to more
than a few hundred acres. Cotton started
before 1828, for there was a small export
that year, and between 1867 and 1S T 4 some
5000 tons of the stuff was sent abroad. But
as there were no mills to use the article it
practically wandered off the map. A recent
record showed about 70 acres under cotton.
Yet the country uses cotton —uses <.,me in
its woollen mills —and imports most of it.
It also imports millet, flax and hemp
(£575,000 worth in 1918) ; bark (even wattle
bark), jute, straw, sago, mustard and sun-
dries. The patient way in which the Aus-
tralian farmer stodges along, growing the
few simple old things that his ancestors
learned about in the convict time when the
kangaroo was still plentiful in Collins-
street, _ avoiding new-fangled or “fancy”
industries (a Victorian Minister recently
warned returned soldiers against sugar-beet
as a “fancy” and rash and unorthodox de-
parture), dodging wild experiments and
being true to the faith of his bucolic fore-
fathers, is amazing.

Bolshevism Arrives.
The News (25/3/1929.)
The State Government has been overthrown. The
Balmain Soviet, led by Comrade Fritz Goreski, pro-
ceeded to the Sydney Town Hall at about 3 p.m.
yesterday. After some desultory firing an entrance
was effected. The Lord Mayor was severely wounded.
Amongst the killed were Mr. Wooster, 0.8. E.,
Town Clerk; Biffkins, the doorkeeper; and 14
policemen.

Sydney Doesn’t Want It.
After inspection :—•
Because Sydney thinks it could spare the Quaran-
tine Station at North Head and the terrors it occa-
sionally holds, a party of politicians and pressmen
went exploring last week and re-discovered Jervis
Bay, where the wool ships used to load for Lon-
don long ago, before N. S. Wales built its railway
system and ran all the trade of the State to Sydney.
The water of the bay is beautifully deep and blue,
and is fringed with gleaming white beaches. There
are no steamers on the water, no people visible on
the sands, and no clusters of roofs among the endless
trees. Huskisson, the little holiday and fishing town-
ship, is the local Circular Quay or Port Melbourne.
Save for Huskisson, the red-roofed Naval College far
away to the south and the white pillar of the light-
house on Point Perpendicular at the entrance are the
only signs of settlement.
It is the ideal place to throw off clothes and for-
get how to work ; but that sort of thing can be done
just as well up shallow creeks and on open ocean
beaches. There are in Jervis 24,320 acres of water
with a greater depth than 24 feet. Sydney has
14,285 acres. Sydney has 35 feet at the entrance.
Jervis has a lordly 90 feet on its rocky ocean door-
step, and the liner of the future will be able to
steam two miles up the bay before the water shoals
to less than 66 feet. At present it is a deserted
parlor of trade upholstered in blue, and its light-
house is a mere coastal lamp-post. Occasionally in
stormy weather it gets a line when “S.S. Tinkettle
sheltering at Jervis Bay” comforts the anxious
readers of the shipping notes. Its trade statistics
could be crowded on to a stamp.
But, because Sydney doesn’t want it, starved Jer-
vis is welcome to the Quarantine Station ; wherefore
the little Captain Cook, after doing a 10-mile run
across, sent its people ashore for a brief three-
quarters of an hour on the North Head to get what
idea they could of the 8000 acres of State territory
available. Then the vessel’s siren v ailed twice,
the explorers hurried aboard, and the vessel steamed
slowly across to the southern side, where, with a
fresh north-easter blowing across many miles of open
water, it rolled in front of the alternative
site —a strip of Federal territory. If the station
should eventually be placed there, optimists may
reckon that the Federal capital (135 miles inland
and still unconnected by rail with its strictly-limited
seaport of 28 square miles) has made some sort of
a start towards destiny.
After a long look from seaward, at that virgin
shore where the natural phenomenon known as the
Hole in the Wall looks like the muzzle of a giant
gun aimed at the Future, some of the excursionists,
seasick and tired of looking at the blank face of
the forest, urged the captain to go full speed for
Huskisson, on the way to Sydney, where the sky-
scrapers reach towards the stars and every foot of
the harbor-front is used.
Jervis is not fit for a quarantine station. It was
designed by Providence to be a great port, and by
now should be in the position of having a quarantine
station'of its own to give away. Perhaps a later gen-
eration will have more enterprise and imagination.
If you look hard enough you can see the home roofs
clustering behind the blue water and white beaches,
and hear the whistle of the trains along the hills.

According to Dr. Walter Broavn tubercular
disease is still on the increase in South Aus.
In referring to the reports of the Ralyra
Sanatorium for Consumptives, he states that
in his report for 1914 he pointed out that
the percentage number of notifications of
pulmonary t.b., in the State had been
fairly constant from year to year; if any-
thing, there had been an increase. Now, four
years later, there has been a decided in-
crease. The average number of notifications
per 100,000 for the 14 years ending 1913
was 103 ; the average for the past four years
was .117; the figure for 1917 was 140. The
deaths per .100,000 have also taken an up-
ward turn, rising gradually from 67 in 1914
to 84 in 1917. Apparently all that has been
done in the fight with consumption is that
there has been a lengthening of the average
life of a consumptive by increased efficiency
in treatment. As far as science knows at
present the disease can be eradicated from
the community only by prevention, and it
is in this direction that the national effort
should be thrown.
Senator Millen has had a suggestion laid
on his doormat by Horace Richardson,
M.L.C. for the South-Western Province
(Vic.). Richardson's idea, which has
occurred to lots of other people, and no
doubt to the named Millen himself, is that
the average sum likely to be paid to returned
soldiers for their re-establishment should be
taken as the basis for forming various
“pools," the money thus set apart to be used
for building up a line of co-operative indus-
tries. Suppose that the average was taken
at £5O a head, the capital available to start
100 men in a joint undertaking would be
£5OOO. The industry that occurs to Rich-
ardson as most suitable is the pioneer-
timber trade —dealing with the raw material
in the forest, cutting building sizes and other
marketable timbers and using all the waste
for by-products, such as wood alcohol, acetic
acid, eucalyptus oil, potash, charcoal and so
on. Richardson suggests that the sawdust
should be used for firekindlers, and no doubt
it should be possible to start a small plant
for making clothes-pegs. Australia really
will hare to start this peg enterprise some
time. Numerous private firms are making
a do of the forest-timber business in Victoria
and other States; and it should be a reason-
ably easy job, considering the existing Gov-
ernment facilities in roads, railways, season-
ing-kilns and so forth, to get a lot of re-
turned man on the move at something
better than wages—replanting the cleared
lands with young trees from the State nur-
series as they go through the timber, and
thus showing Private Enterprise that it
really is possible to improve on present
devastating methods.

By Robert Harper’s death Australia loses
a solid, useful citizen. As a commercial
man he built up one of the Commonwealth’s
biggest manufacturing houses, and had the
courage to spend enough money to make the
Australian goods familiarly known all over
the country. As financier and earnest
Federalist he did great service—none the
less great because so far it has not borne
fruit—in connection with the Federalising
of Australia’s public debts. In politics
one Premier and Prime Minister after
another, in Harper’s 20 years or more of
Parliamentary life, paid him the compliment
of not asking him to join the Cabinet.
Apart from the damning circumstance that
he didn’t have the genial temperament,
Robert Harper was much too independent
a thinker to be satisfactory to any machine;
and that he had a rocky road tven in his
electorates was more to his credit than
otherwise.
Some people are fretful over the Defence
Department’s action in banning fireworks in
metropolitan areas during the Peace celebra-
tions. Sydney Telegraph has adopted the
“harmless cracker’’ line of argument. It is
a poor one. A plaything which was, to all
appearances, an ordinary “hunger” could
be, in reality, an incendiary bomb capable of
doing an astonishing amount of damage to
the new Telegraph offices. Mills bombs
are smaller than rockets. Ask any returned
infantryman what a Mills bomb can dp.
As a matter of fact, the world is facing the
“harmless cracker” proposition from a new
angle. Sabotage, which may be anything
from slowing down on a job to blowing up
a Government office, is in the air. For four
years millions of men have been trained to
make bombs of every sort. A cracker is
merely a diluted bomb. Why encourage the
promiscuous handling of articles which may
be mere foolishness designed to make the
jumpy jump, but which may equally be
camouflaged engines of death and destruc-
tion? Australia has been remarkably casual
in all such matters: the individual with
the peculiar manner and the loaded gun has
been allowed to wander at large till the
tragedy occurred; while the state of the
different -Poisons Acts has done much for
the suicide records, and more for such
happenings as the recent poisoning of a
dairy herd at Faulconbridge (N.S.W.).
