Paderewski, the musician, succeeds Ponia-
towski, the courtier, painter, dancer, ama-
teur soldier and clothier’s advertisement in
the troubled job of head of the Polish re-
public. The interval is 123 years. Pader-
ewski’s ability in his own line is un-
rivalled ; his patriotism is unquestioned;
his hair is uncut; his courage may probably
be taken for granted. But it is difficult
to imagine any country save dreamy, im-
practical Poland selecting this spoiled child
of melody to do battle with Huns and Bol-
sheviks ; to argue patiently and plausibly
with diplomatists about boundaries and get
the last possible ounce of concession out
of them, and to struggle with a Treasury
that has been in difficulties since the year
800 or thereabouts. Pad. has lived in an
atmosphere of assured victory. His finances
have shown an easy surplus most of his
life. He has had a Divine Right at the
piano, and he has been accustomed to send
the stage-manager or the orchestra into
exile just like a Romanoff. He compelled
silence with his eye, and once in Sydney he
struck in the middle of a performance be-
cause a window creaked. There is hardly
a sound window left in Poland, which may
upset his nerves to begin with, and local
politics have always been inharmonious.
Bagpipes aren’t a circumstance to them.
Paderewski’s enthusiasm lifted him to the
Doge’s chair. His reddish mane has
crackled with electric fire all through the
war, and his efforts to rouse the world on
behalf of his country were heroic. But now
he has got a job which calls for an inspired
bricklayer, pawnbroker, farmer and sanitary
engineer and a lieaven-born alderman. He
may surprise the world, but at the age of
59 a man is almost too old to learn even a
new style of hairdressing.
By “Henry IIorsecollar” :
A certain “Ulsterman,” whom I regard as a rotten
historian, says (B. 9/l/’l9) that the Scots
moved from Ireland to Scotland, and gave that
country its name, so that when some of them went
to Ulster, then “almost completely depopulated,
partly through wars between different clans, but
chiefly through wars with the English,” they were
really going home. I would call this sheer idiocy,
only such language would cause the editor to
reject this paragraph and then I wouldn’t be
paid for it. The Scots, who spoke a Gaelic
language similar to the Irish, certainly moved
into the Highlands (Argyllshire was their first
stronghold) and either absorbed the old Painted
People or shoved them into parts unknown. They
gave their name to Scotland, and they had some
sort of throne in the Highlands, and 47 alleged
kings with names like Congardus and Dougallus
and Amberkeletus were of their race. But in
some way, about which history isn’t very clear, the
capital shifted from the Highlands, which were
Scot, to the Lowlands, which were mostly Saxon
and partly British, British being pre-Scot and
pre-Saxon and also pre-Roman. The kings, who
began to be called by Greek names like Alexander,
and Norman ones like William and Robert, instead
of Dougallus, took the name of the country with
them, just as another set of sovereigns moved from
Rome to the conquered territory of Macedonia, and
took the Roman name and empire with them.
Saxon Edinburgh, calling itself Scottish, began to
send bumptious orders to the old Scottish capital
and the real Scottish people, just as Constantinople
began to send orders to Rome. The all-per-
vading Norman soaked in, as he did everywhere.
Robert Bruce was a Norman, and while he and
all his descendants called themselves Scots, they
looked on the real Scots as slush. A time came
when a king who was Norman-French and called
himself Scot sent a colony of Saxons or mostly
Saxons who called themselves Scots to Ireland.
They were going home, just as a Greek colony
would be if it settled in Yorkshire (England) be-
cause York was a Roman capital before the English
existed, and the Greeks took over Rome as a
going concern. Of course the visitors at Belfast
have a right to be as much Irish as the other
mixed races in Ireland, most races being mixed.
The trouble is that they won’t be Irish —-only
Ulster. When an alien race keeps on celebrating
for 227 years a petty victory which it partly
gained in a bog under a Dutch general and with
the aid of Dutch mercenary troops over the Irish
population, it evidently means to be alien, not
Irish. And the friction they keep alive by letting
off crackers to celebrate that Dutch victory in a
bog makes the biggest problem the British Empire
is up against.
Tasmanian Parliament meets in special
session this week to select a successor to
Senator Long, who shook the dust from his
wings the other day with considerable
indignation. This special session was the
only way out of a “grave responsibility” for
the Lee Ministry, which otherwise would
have had the job of appointing somebody
to the vacancy at the great risk of offending
many friends. Both Houses of Parliament,
sitting together, will now shoulder the
burden. Ex-Senator Mulcai-iy, junior mem-
ber for Wilmot Division, has been mentioned
most persistently; but when the 48 legis-
lators (37 Liberals or “Nationalists” and
11 Laborites) have looked at the matter,
it is not quite certain at the moment
of writing what will happen. The
latest amendment of the Tas. Electoral
Act, which provided, among other things,
that a State vacancy shall be filled by
the next on the list at the last general
election is one nuisance. For instance, if
Mulcahy gets the Senate position, it is
pretty certain that his successor in the State
House would be Norman Cameron, and
Cameron is just about the last man the
Lee crowd desires to crown with favors of
any sort. So there is a chance of Mulcahy
missing the Senate nomination after all.
And as something almost as uncomfortable
for the Lee party seems likely to happen if
a sitting supporter is translated from Bass,
Darwin, Franklin and Denison divisions—in
two at least of these divisions the railway
pass and the six months’ salary would
probably have to be handed over to a Labor
man—the appointment of any Assembly
follower to the Senate looks like something
that Premier Lee ought to want to avoid.
Lloyd George is where he is because he
is the embodiment of what the best type of
Britisher has been feeling since 191 b. He
has been almost alone in the capacity to put
aside all his old prejudices —Freetrade, Home
Buie, dis-establishment, and the jargon of
pacificism—in order to get on with the war.
There is no one that he has hesitated to
collaborate with, honestly and generously,
to that end. He has thus been associated
variously with men like Carson, Lansdowne
and Long, and always, at a pinch, he showed
out as reasonable and patient and single-
minded in the prosecution of his huge job.
On the platform he is the greatest revivalist
or enthuser of the day, and privately the
charm of his personality is great. Above
all he is an adventurer, one of the band to
which Disraeli, Rhodes and Chamberlain
belonged. Nothing has ever daunted him, nor
ever will. The British like to be led by
that sort of man in their dark and doubtful
hours.
It was the late Robert Harper’s air of
self-righteousness, rather than his intense
respectability, that hurt his fellow-members
of the House. When he took the daughter
of dour Rev. Cairns to wife he practically re-
nounced all human right to appear unholy
in this world, and thenceforth he cultivated
a saintly cast of countenance in all save his
full, bold eyes. They continued to explain
his long-ago title of “fighting Bob” ; for in
business and financial enterprise and politi-
cal effort Robert Harper was ever combative
and resourceful. Probably he never scored
a point without considering himself justi-
fied. But one remembers what Labouchere
said about Gladstone after the G.O.M. had
euchred him on some political issue. “I
wouldn’t mind the old fox always having an
ace up his sleeve,” said Labby, “if he didn’t
invariably pretend that God Almighty put
it there.” Where he could afford to unbend
the deceased elder was quite a good sort.
When upon his perch of virtuous dignity he
was too darned good altogether.
At Auckland (M.L.) the other day there
came to light a craft with a dark shadow
on her past. About 38 years ago the
Jeanette, brigantine, was an auxiliary vessel
in an American expedition that set forth in
the belief that it could circumnavigate the
globe by way of the Arctic Sea. Above
Siberia' the Jeanette was frozen in and
squeezed on to the ice surface. The position
of her complement was desperate, and with
food and fuel running short the men had
the choice of certainly perishing where they
were or probably perishing in an attempt
to go down to the boundaries of mankind.
Only four of them survived the unspeakable
privations. Two or three years later an
expedition looking for other possible sur-
vivors came upon the Jeanette. She was
salved, did some whaling, and for some time
has been trading in the South Seas.
Cecil McVilly, one-time amateur sculling
champion of Australia and winner of the
Diamond Sculls, was one of the 400 officers
picked for the Baku expedition. They went
through Persia and across the Caspian to
organise the local Armenians against the
Turks. Things looked promising for a time
and they had 40,000 Armenians well armed
and on the job. But in the end the poor
truck turned dog on them and ran, while a
Bolshevik section made a valiant effort to
sell the Allied officers to the Turks, and it
was only by the skin of their teeth that any
of them got out of Baku. McVilly was
among the last to leave.
Brigadier-General Brand, the new Vic.
Commandant, went away in the First Fleet
as Brigade-Major of the Third (Inf.) Bri-
gade. He got his Battalion on the Peninsula
and a Brigade in France. He was a perma-
nent soldier before the war, and back of that
a school-teacher in Queensland. He is in
the forties, a C. 8., C.M.G. and D. 5.0., and
a first-rate man, whether as a fighter or as
an administrator. If he had escaped the bad
luck to be drafted for furlough just before
the Armistice he would have had an excel-
lent chance of a Division on the preferment
of General Hobbs.
Arthur Balfour, Secretary of State for
Foreign Affairs in Lloyd-George’s just-
reconstructed Ministry, is the truest extant
type of that baffling institution, the English
Gentleman. No one has ever been able to
satisfactorily define an English gentleman,
but it is universally agreed that, anyhow,
Balfour is It —despite the fact that he is
Scotch. _ Intellectually Balfour stands high
above his colleagues. On this plane Lloyd-
George, for instance, who has been described
as the possessor of “a kind of sublime illit-
eracy,” is infinitely below him. It was a
subtle stroke of British diplomacy to send
Balfour to America in 1917. He beat Wilson
out of sight at the latter’s own game of cul-
tivated generalisations on the ethics of states-
manship. _ Balfour has held almost every
high British office from the Premiership
downwards; is the author of a theological
classic (“The Foundations of Belief”) ; is 70
and looks about 50; and is easijy the worst-
dressed man, from the standpoint of the
Tailor and Cutter, in either House.

The gorgeous and crowded “Hello, Every-
body !” is still flourishing its 600 legs in 17
scenes, and changing costumes with extra-
ordinary speed, at Her Majesty’s (Sydney).
Scene No. S, “The Patent Office,” is about
the best comedy effort. George Welch as
the mousetrap crank grows madder in a con-
fidential way every night, and the lunatic
with the gun that counts the rabbits as it
shoots them rushes in and out with his cash-
register artillery until he is shoved into the
office furnace or cast down the lift-well.
“Oh, Oh, Delphine!” finishes at the Royal
(Sydney) on Friday night. Next evening
the Royal Comics start a revival of “Katin-
ka.” It may be expected to start the “Rac-
kety Coo” epidemic again.
The long wrangle of “Friendly Enemies”
ends on Friday night at the Criterion (Syd-
ney). On Saturday, the co. headed by Adams
and Waters begins its farewell with a brief
revival of “Business Before Pleasure.” M.
B. Figman, consigned to the Cri., is already
in sight. He brings “Nothing but the
Truth.”
* * *
Emelie Polini, in “De Luxe Annie,” keeps
the Palace (Sydney) busy these summer
nights. One lure of the fast and clever play
is the shivering fugitives’ entrance to the
closed house with the chill of winter and
desertion heavy upon it. That scene goes
like a soothing draught from the ice-chest.
“Everybody’s Doing it” (second edition)
still rattles its clogs at the Tivoli (Sydney).
In the new distribution of songs, young Jean
Keith drew something entitled’ “Just a
Baby’s Prayer at Twilight.’’ The title is
enough to make. strong men steal out of their
seats and step lightly for the open air. How-
ever, the tender wail is not as distressing as
its title; or perhaps it is Jean’s art that
saves it.

of looking for “The Babes in the Wood.”

“Goody Two Shoes” is still travelling on
a thousand twinkling feet at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.). The amount of “Goody” in the
show is little more than the title, so the
Demon, who is supposed to deprive her of
her peace of mind, is an unemployed person
trying to establish his deviltry with a heavy
scowl and a superfluous gloat. All round,
though, the panto is a marvel of economy in
the opportunities allowed to the minor char-
acters. The Mayor of Blossomville, for
instance, is hardly more than a phantom.
Then there are a youth named Ronnie and
a damsel called Zoe, who endeavor to rejoice
in their infrequency with an anxious Wizard
who achieves his greatest publicity in the
line-up at the end. These characters are
practically spectators who are paid to be
present, though in the cases of the Mayor
and the magician they should get a bit more
to stay away.
The double bill of “Cavalleria” and “Pag-
liacci,” which commences the Rigo season at
the Princess’s (Melb.) next Saturday, will
be made interesting by mixed Italian and
Australian casts. So far as local knowledge
goes, practically all the artists are new to
their jobs, only Balboni and Lois repeating
the tenor roles which they did formerly in
Melbourne for the old Gonsalez co. Count
Filippini in “Cavalleria” and Cacialli in
“Pagliacci” will be the novelties in the bari-
tone parts, formerly embellished with healthy
realism by the much-missed Scamuzzi. The
leading sopranos will be Leah Meyers (San-
tuzza) and Nellie Leach (Nedda).
The vast fabric of veracity erected by
M. B. Figman in “Nothing But the Truth”
is still a matter for moderate wonderment at
the Royal (Melb.). Here and there Figman
continues to play his part twice by illustrating
the spoken word with an effusive gesture, but
composure is rapidly settling upon him and
will soon make him a comparatively immo-
bile citizen with both arms and most of his
countenance on the unemployed list. Fig-
man naturally makes friends by his inherent
geniality, and the show would be more cheer-
ful if the same could be said of two of the
other conspirators, young Pirie Bush and
Mac Barnes.
“Jack and Jill,” at the King’s (Melb.), is
still a scramble of irrelevant events in which
Carl, the Hunchback, gets few chances to
arouse public interest in the Magic Crown.
All the same, Carl attains a special signifi-
cance by uttering deep, throaty threats from

“Bubbly,” at the Tivoli (Melb.), continues
to talk of any old thing that suits the whim
ot its many producers. The subjects range
irom a bit of O. Henry, stolen from the dull-
est page of “The Four Million,” to a jest
about a wishbone and a remarkable deal in
clothes, where the infinitesimal Rego is mis-
fitted with a slop suit. The incidents that
get the loudest laughs generally have the
little man bobbing up in the middle, and he
ever scores the fullest approval because his
original line of quaintness refuses to grow
old. Beatrice Holloway is now out of the
cast, and Ethel Bennetto and Clarice Hard-
wicke are sharing her duties.

The proposal that members of the Sea-
men’s Union should refuse to man any ves-
sel _ that carries Senator Pearce on his
ridiculous and expensive demobilisation job
is highly interesting. There is precedent of
one sort in the case of the British Seamen’s
Union, which refused to let any of its men
help m the transportation of certain pro-Hun
politicians.. And there is precedent of an-
other sort in an earlier case when the vessel
—a small sailing craft-had actually started
before toe union made up its mind that the
passenger was obnoxious, in consequence of
which decision the union threw him over-
board. In the present instance the matter
has been taken in time, so if the decision is
against Pearce it won’t be necessary to cast
him into the deep or leave him on an island.
-Lhe feeamen’s Union has very large powers
in places like Britain and Australia, if it
likes to use them. But it can’t control
Malay proas; wherefore The Bulletin sug-
gests to Pearce to get in early and hire a
sailing-boat from Malacca to pick
him up somewhere on the North Queensland
coast.

In Sydney three young women have been
charged with being “idle and disorderly
persons, having insufficient means of sup-
port. The first part of the charge was
proved that is to say, constables swore to
disoiderliness observed through keyholes
and other apertures. The ladies then stated
the case for the defence, which was, simply,
that they all had £lO or more in banks
As soon as this was established the magis-
trate had to discharge them. The astound-
ing law is, roughly, that if a woman is a
peisistent and voracious Rahab, with money
m the bank, she is more or less unassailable,
whereas,, if she is only an occasional and
pecunious practitioner, she goes down.
Nothing. succeeds like success—even in
prostitution.

He was settled at Brundah before the Wed-
dm diggings (now Grenfell) broke out On
his retirement his son took up farming on

A sort of definition has been put to the
Government’s attitude re air-navigation. Air-
ships are a useful device for spying out a
country’s fortifications, camps, arsenals,
munition-factories, naval depots, shipbuild-
mg yards, fleets, etc. And though Australia
isn t exactly teeming with these things, it
is suggested that no foreign air-machine or
privately-owned Australian machine should
be allowed to fly—in fact, that every air-
vessel should be under Government control.
A crowd which plays at little tin soldiers in
this earnest fashion may end by putting all
buggies under Government control. The sus-
picious way in which buggies are liable to
linng around Australia’s big-cannon foun-
dries is alarming.
Premier Ryan (Q.) is going to make
Cheap Food plank No. 1 of his platform
when he enters FederaJ politics. Yet in his
own headquarters at Brisbane potatoes were
dearer the other day than they had been for
. y ear s, and many other things were
similar. Ryan has cheap meat to his credit
(it happened that the State had a super-
abundance of beef) ; but when it comes to
cheap anything else he seems much the same
as the rest.
In a voice half-choked by suppressed emo-
tion the Akgus complains that “the wages
claimed under the Australian Tramway Em-
ployees’ log show a decided increase on those
at present prevailing, which are from 50
per cent, to 100 per cent, higher than those
paid 1.0 years ago.” Possibly; but what
tnen.- 1 It is not necessarily any more out-
rageous for a man to ask £2 where he for-
merly got £1 than it is for a child to require
twice as much cloth for a suit when he
grows to be a man. It isn’t what a man
gets, but what he can buy with what he gets,
that matters. Incidentally, it matters just
as much to the employer as to the employed.

The post-mortemist who cuts into the
National Mutual’s latest report unearths a
series of pleasant surprises. To begin with,
the Association got in a record lot of new
business during the year—ll,BBs policies
assuring £4,320,512, which was £073,000
more than in the previous best year. The
National Mutual wrote its first policy in
1869, and, as the table shows, 40 years had
elapsed before the gross income had reached
the million mark. Now, after a further
nine years, the income is within coo-ee of
two millions. During the past 10 years
the management has practically doubled the
income which had resulted from the pre-
vious 39 years’ work. This rapid progress
has been made wholly with Ordinary
business; for the old martinet who founded
the concern refused to have anything to
do with “Industrial stuff,” though “Industrial
stuff” helps to feed many an Ordinary
branch. If there came a time when the
management felt sorry it had not gone into
the new line, it was probably then too
late to give chase to other concerns. How-
ever, the National Mutual did get a move
on and set out to establish itself in other
countries. Besides the Commonwealth and
Maoriland it is now operating in a big way
in S’Africa and Great Britain and Ireland,
while it also hangs out its sign in India,
Ceylon and the Straits Settlements.
Whether the big business figures it gets
that way are worth as much as the much
smaller figures it doubtless would have had
if it had stayed at home is a matter of
opinion. One thing is certain: the Aus-
tralian mortality rate is so favorable that
an Australian office’s experience isn’t
likely to be improved by taking in people
outside Australia. Still, admittedly that
doesn’t settle the matter. There are other
considerations.
The income for the past year has been
considerably helped by increased interest
earnings. In 1914 the revenue from this
source, after paying £13,624 in taxes, was
£404,702; for the past year net income was
£562,888, taxes taking £34,330. The loss
rate on account of war claims has, of course,
grown: admitted claims equalled 40 p.c.
of the income in 1914; 45.6 p.c. for the
past year. Of the £876,989 of claims met
during the 12 months, no less than £207,088
represented direct war losses. Bill of
Berlin has been responsible for £561,308 of
losses since hostilities began, and the
Association has not seen the last of the
trouble yet. These war claims represent
a heavy loss, for most of the policy-holders
were, young men who had paid little in
premiums, while the majority of the policies,
having been in force prior to 1934, were
free of war loading.
Though the expense rate was heavier
than in the previous two years, it is con-
siderably better than it used to be :

But in a year like the last, when a record
lot of new business was got in, the cost of
writing new policies is an important factor.
One method of figuring is to reckon
that the old, or renewal, business cost 7 p.c.
and the single premium business 3 p.c.,
and charge the balance of the exes, to
the new business. On this basis last year’s
new business expense rate works out at 69.9
p.c. Possibly the old business is being handled
for less than it used to be—standing
charges, for instance, have not advanced at
the same rate as business—and consequently
the cost of recent npw business is a little
more than is shown here. Even so, it
is clear from the following comparison that
last year’s new business was written more
cheaply than any previous lot:

When the war started the directors had
three-fifths of the funds in mortgages—
principally loans to producers—\bhile the
holding of gilt-edge stock was miserably weak.
However, at Sept.' 30 last two millions had
been put into War Loan, and further
parcels have since been taken up, though
almost half of the assets still consist of
mortgages. But things go well. With
the _ bulk of the war losses met, with a
diminishing expense rate and improved
interest earnings, bonus prospects are
brighter than thov ever were before.

The Langridge is the only Melbourne
building society within The Bulletin’s
knowledge which came out of the big
smash of the ’nineties without having
to reconstruct or c l »ny depositors their
full 20s. in the £. at the due date.
Of course it did not escape trouble
altogether. In 1893 there were reserves of
£21,269, which were transferred to a con-
tingency account; and a lot of this old
stored-up profit disappeared as the manage-
ment d.pped to cover losses on realisations.
But though shareholders’ capital remained
intact they had a very dry time while the
broom was being used. For some years no
div. was paid at all; then a poor little
20 p.c. was handed out; and by 1915
the rate had gradually increased to
8 p.c. Meantime, the Society had been ex-
tending its operations again, and in the 10
years to 1914 had doubled its advances.
Since the war started the management has
been feeling its way cautiously

So in the four years deposits have been
reduced by £11,28.1 and advances by almost
£16,000. That has had this result: while
the management had to lean against au
overdraft in 1914, it . now has £5083
in War Loan and £2858 in cash.
Interest received from advances has
naturally fallen—from £9466 in 1914
to £8744 for the past year. Undoubtedly
the management did the right thing, for
until a month or so ago nobody knew
exactly what was going to happen—and
nobody knows now. So far as the Lang-
ridge is concerned it has got itself in a
comfortable position to meet any trouble.
With a paid-up capital of £68.940 and onlv
£14,060 in deposits, the creditors are weil
secured. And though profits have been cur-
tailed slightly shareholders have the satis-
faction of knowing that the Society is in
a nice position to reach out for business
when conditions become settled again.

Twenty-six years old now. and Westralians
think so well of it that nearly 2{ millions
are at present in its care ; and almost half
ot the value now in hand represents the
progress of the past 10 years. The co. should
go on expanding, for so far it has hardly
more than scratched the big field in which
it is working. It offers many advantages
over even the best type of old-friend-of the-
family trustee: (1) it does not die or go
fishing; (2) the co.’s officers are specially
trained for their job; (3) its future de-
pends solely on whether it provides efficient
service; and (4) it puts up a guarantee
that it won't try to pick winners at the races
with the assistance of its trusts. This Wes-
tralian co. has £37,514 of assets to fill any
holes if somebody on the premises should
take the wrong turning, and, if it came to
the worst, there’s a further £33,000 callable.
Still the risk is not one that would keep
shareholders awake at nights. As the table
shows, they have done very well. Ten years
ago there were 20,000 50s. shares, each paid
to 10s. (£10,000). Since then another 75..
has been got in by paying bonuses and sim-
ultaneously making calls. The paid-up capi-
tal is now £17,000, while £10,580 of reserves:
have been accumulated. These funds are:
well placed: premises, £13,077, War Loan
and public securities, £14,350; mortgages,
£4974; cash and odds and ends, £5113.. The
co. did not owe a penny at October 31.

Of the revenue for the half-year to the end
of May, 1914, as shown in the accounts, the
profit was equal to 28.6 p.c.; for the past
half-year it was 15.8 p.c. And the decline is
all the more striking in view of the fact
that the price of copper has been double
what it was immediately before the war.
But we have no right to assume that the
normal pre-war price of the metal will
be the normal post-war price. Principally
owing to the enormous increase of paper
money, and because nations struggling for
their very life were prepared to. pay (in
borrowed money) almost any price for
their requirements, there has ' been a tre-
mendous increase in values. Mt. Morgan
itself shows what it regarded as the highest
safe price at which copper could be valued
prior to the war: it still estimates metal
on hand at the old figure of £55 per ton.
Even though the market had already
fallen heavily, the London quotation for
electrolytic a week ago was £ll7. The
week before the Great Crime was launched
it was £6l. No man knows how much
of that difference of £56 will be wiped
out during the next two or three years.
If paper money had its old backing of
gold it might be possible to make some
sort of a guess. But the world—and not
Australia only—-has a vast quantity of
paper which Governments have decreed
shall take the place of gold; and the in-
evitable result of that is an increase in
the market value of commodities. The
discovery of two or three full-fledged Rands
and Kalgoorlies would have just the same
effect. The sudden stoppage of war ex-
penditures will assuredly smash the prices
of many commodities—certain metals in-
cluded, perhaps—but there is not going
to be any general return to old price-levels
while the currency is inflated.
But in considering the comparisons made
above, one fact has to be kept in mind.
Six months ago there were 2681 tons of
copper in process of refining: of this 838
tons were valued in the accounts at £lOB
per ton and the balance (1843 tons) at
£55 per ton. In the present accounts,
although it was expected that 680 tons would
be available for sale under the old contract
(£.lOB at Sydney), all “the copper in process
of refining, etc., at the close of the period.
2230 tons, has been valued at £55 per- ton.”
This is, of course, to the disadvantage of
the present accounts, just as the plan
adopted in the previous accounts was to the
advantage of the profit- figures for that term.
But we can’t look forward to the present
carry-over being quitted at £lOB Syd-
the P as t half-year’s revenue about
£9/,000 came from the surplus from the
® ale °f the previous half-year’s production.
If the 2230 tons in hand average only £lOO,
then the co. will receive £100,350 more than
it has taken credit for in its accounts. So
the past half-year’s production not realised

£.jS 10s. —to make the half-year’s actual

what costs amount to. The co. would make
a profit, as shown above, of £86,000-odd if

have been drawn on lately the reserves would

ever, governed by the price of the metal.
A bad slump in the copper market would

* * * *
The publication of the stocks of lead,
copper, zinc and tin in the hands ot the
Allied powers partly accounts for the slump
in metal prices. Also the disorder from
the Rhine to Petrograd and the Dardanelles
occasions the uneasy feeling that there won t
be the anticipated early call for metals. Aus-
tralia’s most vital interest in the slump is
that working costs, freights and mine sup-
plies eat up so much of the revenue that
there is hardly a mine that can make a
penny of profit with copper at £BO. It looks
in any case as though the cos. will have to
stock up their product for six months, as J.
Bull intends to load only food and clothing
stuffs on any of the ships released for the
Australian trade. Mt. Morgan promises to
pull along until funds or credit give out,
while Hampden, Mt. Elliott and Mt. Cuth-
bert don’t intend to smelt until they can
get lower labor costs.
* * * *
Trunkey, the old N. S. Wales field which
turned out something like six tons of gold,
provides a striking example of the weakness
of a mining law which does not insist on
mine-owners sending regular reports and
plans of work done for filing in the Mines
Dept. - A Bulletin man had a look at
Trunkey Ridge the other day. On every
hand is evidence of the usual waste
which accompanies a boom. Afterwards
tributors got out all the rich stuff they
could lay hands on down to the water level
and then packed up and left. There is
a local s£ory that values were maintained
right to the water. But the Mines Dept,
can tell you nothing of any value. There
are people who still have faith in the old
field, and now and then they back their
faith with their labor and cash. Even now
a little syndicate is making a start at
pushing ahead a tunnel abandoned in the
long ago. A complete official record of
work previously done would be of great
value to anybody who might be tempted
to have a go at some of the old shows.
* * * *
“Certainly the gold values are going down,
and whilst the multiplicity of reefs entarls
considerable prospecting work it at the same
time increases the prospects of obtaining
bonanzas.” That is the last word L. F.
Harper, of the Mines Department, lias to
sav after his recent exhaustive examination
of the old Hill End field (N.S.W.). So
Hawkins Hill Deep Bevels, Hawkins
Hill Central and the Marshall’s Mines cos.
are preparing to join forces for the systematic
and co-ordinated development of the field.
Getting down to definite recommendations,
Harper advises development work to inter-
sect as many veins as possible in the neigh-
borhood of the frequent cross-courses or
faults. He says there are indications that
the new bedded veins are developing at a
depth, and that these should pay investiga-
tion by winzing and cross-cutting below the
Amalgamated tunnel level. The gold occurs
in distinct chutes, the richest proved up to
the present having a height of about 80ft.
over a length of 2000 ft., pitching to the
north, and carroting out underfoot. As fol-
lowed down, the chute had a tendency to
pass to the west into adjoining veins. He
regards the prospects of obtaining further
good makes of payable ore as favorable.
However, the future of the field, he points
out, is more dependent -on rich patches than
on the output of a big tonnage of milling
ore.
» * * *
Even the oldest inhabitant cannot recall
such a dry time as the country around Trun-
key (N.S.W.) is experiencing. Mt. Dudley
still has a little water in its dam, but man-
ager Martin is anxiously awaiting re-inforce-
ments from the big catchment area which
feeds his water-supply. At present he is do-
ing a lot of timbering, while the work of
sinking the main shaft a further 60ft. and
opening up north at 145 ft. has already been
commenced. A
Prospecting work at Archie McKellar s
two finds, the Mastodon and the
four miles north-west of Mt. Dudley, is also
being made difficult owing to the drought, ahe
Mastodon shows a boldly-defined outcrop from
2ft. to Bft. wide, running in kindly country
for half a mile. Samples right along the
line give between one and two ounces to
the ton, and even better. Whether these val-
ues go down is still unknown, as, apart from
a little costeening, practically no work has
been done. However, the Federal Treasurer
has granted permission for the formation ot
a syndicate of 15 shares of £lOO each £lOO
and four shares going to the vendor, leaving
£lOOO to test the property.
Mount Booralong*, near Armidale (N.S.W.),
which shows a lot of low-grade molybdenite
in an open cut, is getting its plant together
and hopes to start concentrating at an early
date. Chairman Rankin is optimistic con-
cerning market prices for the metal, and
since there are 150,000 shares which have to
be fed from low-grade stuff practically every-
thing will depend on the market.
Last year Westralia produced 876,5700 z.
fine gold, or about 100,000 oz. less than in
1917,' and over 1,100,0000 z. short of the out-
put for 1004, the State’s record year. Decern-
bor’s figures were the lowest of any month
in 1918, yet in the Governor’s speech read
at the opening of the last session of Parlia-
ment the industry was declared to be fit and
well. It could scarcely be more out of sorts.
.*.***
The Westralian tin producer is in a bad
way. He has practically no chance now
that the Inter-Allied executive has gone out
of the business and the TJ.S.A. Government
refuses to grant any more export licenses.
Shipping space to England was promised by
the Federal authorities, but there has been
none available, so the producer has been
asked to bear up and be content with advances
for the time being. He refuses to be con-
soled in this way. To make matters worse,
his operations at Greenbushes, where nearly
all the tin is at present won, have been
hampered lately by a forestry regulation
which prohibits the cutting of green timber,
exceot some which no man can split.
* * * *
As Edna May (W.A.) now has only a
little more than 20.000 tons of ore in sight,
and little prospect of adding much to this
reserve, it seems probable that the co.’s re-
duction plant will shut down for want of
supplies some time this year. The Edna
May has been a good mine to the. State,
as, apart from the £300,000 cut up in divi-
dends on a paid-up capital of £25,000, its
advent caused a badly needed revival.
Vigorous prospecting in new ground is
promised by the Board, and while that
portion of the Edna May lode in this co.’s
ground is almost worked out,, the co. has
not yet exhausted its possibilities. There is
an area of gneissic rock still unexplored.
For some time before the war started Boulder
Perseverance (W.A.), which was apparently
eouipped with an efficient plant, was more
tlian covering exes, by treating low-grade
stone. But owing to dearer mining supplies
and labor troubles expenditure rapidly out-
distanced receipts, and ultimately the co.
went into liquidation. Tributors then took
a hand and began to break stone, which has
since crushed as high as 4oz. per ton. Some
tributors are said to have simply shovelled
up ore lying on the surface and milled it at
a good profit, while others did so well that
they installed an electric hoist in order to
raise more ore. Briefly, the position is that
this mine, which was alleged to be too low-
grade for a co. to work under war conditions,
is proving highly profitable to a few working
parties, and, obviously, as things are at pre-
sent, the show is not being worked to the
best advantage from the point of view of the
State.
* * * *
South Ivalgurli (W.A.) is reckoned to
have improved a dwt. or so during the year,
and the manager opines that it can be kept
up to this mark for some time. The im-
provement is attributed to developments on
the No. 3 east lode from the 10th to the 15th
level, which are stated to have been con-
sistently satisfactory. The mine bucked
up just in time, as operations for the last
financial year showed a loss of nearly £lOOO
compared with a surplus of £4OOO for the
previous 12 months.
Speaking at the South Kalgurli meeting in
London a couple of months ago, chairman
Marker reported a considerable change for
the better in the mine since the manager’s
annual report had been written. In fact,
without being too optimistic, he thought
the co.’s prospects had improved to such an
extent that the Board would be able to de-
clare another div. at the end of the current
financial year. The cash assets stood at
over £32,000, plus about £.11,000 stores in
hand.

Mick Williams, manager of the Edna May
(W.A.), recently had a look at the Turn of
fide, at Kumanalling, on behalf of some
>urne people. Williams found a shaft
200 ft., and. a short high-grade lens ot
itli poor ends. He says there is practi-

values will decrease in depth. Nevertheless
he thinks that other makes of stone will be
met with, and advises development. To date
300 tons have been treated by the vendors

* * * *
The belated tidings have just reached
local shareholders that in 1917 the North
Kalgurli (W.A.) showed a loss on working
account of £582. When the books closed
the co. owed £23, and had £12,859 in liquid
assets. For some time past the Board has
been looking for additional capital to further
prospect and develop the co.’s Kalgoorlie
holding, or, alternatively, it is prepared to
effect a sale. A cash offer for the show was
made a few months ago, -but just when share-
holders were about to be consulted on the mat-
ter word was received from sometody on the
spot advising caution, as a good strike had
been made in adjoining ground. _ This intel-
ligence caused the directors to prick up their
ears. They are still awaiting developments.
* * * *
When Richard Hamilton turned down
Sloan and Scotchman’s, Stawell (Vic.), the
locals didn’t lose heart. They had 30hd. of
stamps, two or three shafts from which to
poke abour, and formations big enough for
Broken Hill to prod into. The old shoot,
from which divs. came 30 years back, was
put out of court, even as a low-grade coun-
ter, by Hamilton’s drills. Another 2dwt.
would have made all the difference, but the
gold wasn’t there. The best bores got were
in the old Extended ground, south. Poking
about in the neighborhood of one of these
over the 1200 ft. level has revealed a fresh
make of stone. At the start it gave sdwt.
dirt within walls 4ft. apart; but now r , after
40ft. of driving, the shoot has widened to
20ft. worth quite lOdwt. On the strength
of this most of the loose shares were picked
up before Christmas, and with them pocketed
the market is nicely under control if jobbers
are out for prey. The co. owes £lO,OOO to
the bank in addition to £BOOO to the fatmen
v/ho stood by the mine in bad times. So a lot
more dirt will have to be proved to get
finances in trim and to do the work needed
to ascertain whether the shoot goes under-
foot and overhead. The latter should be an
easy job, as levels are open at 1000 ft. and
1300 ft. Nevertheless, manager Couchman
will need to sprint hard to get the market to
believe that Hamilton’s drills dodged a big
shoot in this part of the mine.
* # * *
Vic. Mines Dept, lent £16,000 to 212 co-
operative parties in three years to mine for
gold. Exactly one-half got nothing; the
other won £35,000 worth of gold. Of the
100 lucky parties 10 paid back £615; the
others paid nothing. Forty-four mining cos.
were advanced £44,386, and 25 of them were
successful in winning £143,480 worth of
gold. Twenty-four cos. paid back £10,320;
the balance of £133,000-odd was annexed.
These results don’t encourage State or Com-
monwealth to help gold-mining.
* * * *
The pegging out of more ground at Lang-
ford’s old Central Briseis (Tas.) doesn’t
mean that the claim is any better, but it
will satisfy some to have it settled whether
the old Morning Star runs of wash have all
been worked out. Surveyor Fraser, is the
mainstay, and he argues that, with the
watjr power he will make available from his
new dam site, he will show that the disaster
to the old co. would have been averted if
a good water power had been available.
It is never too late to mend, and as Fraser’s
survey work at the Briseis in the early
days was of the best, he ought to know if
he can bring in the water necessary.
* * * *
Mt. Cuthbert, Cloncurry (Q.), is obtain-
ing temporary financial assistance in order
to continue development. A circular to
shareholders states that further capital is
required, and meetings will be held in Mel-
bourne on February 13 and 14, when
authority will be sought to increase the capi-
tal from 20s. to 30s. per share. There are
at present 279,596 shares issued, with 20,404
in reserve. It is expected that calls amount-
ing to 3s. per share (£41,929) will place
Cuthbert in a position to resume smelting.
As the co.’s future is dependent on the devel-
opment of the Kalkadoon and Orphan, if
shareholders want to go on they have no
choice but to put up the money.
* * *
Herberton (N.Q.) is the most important
lode tin-field in the Commonwealth, and
its multitude of small claims will be heavily
hit by the slump. During November no less
than seven little batteries were at work.
Altogether 2026 tons were crushed for 74
tons of mill tin worth £12,112. Some small
parcels were very rich, averaging from £2O
to £3O per ton. The old Gt. Northern
crushed 101 tons for 17 tons concentrates
worth £2562. Contrast with which the
one-time triumphant Vulcan, which sent 119
tons to the mill for £360.
* * * *
A start has been made with the job of
turning the Tipper Ouse into the Great Lake,
in order to increase the water supply for the
Tasmanian hydro-electric scheme. This will
mean making a channel four or five miles,
but it is reckoned that the extra water will
guarantee another 25,000 or 30,000 h.p. at the
Waddamana station, perhaps more.
Years ago someone tried to develop a
deposit of red ochre at the Carlton River
(Tas.) and to work up a business in mak-
ing paint. As there was plenty of imported
stuff going cheap no one would bother, and
the deposit has been idle except when a
few local sheep-farmers dug up bits to mark
their sheep. Now that branding-oil, for in-
stance, has soared to 10s. or 11s. a gallon, in-,
terest in the thing has revived and another
attempt may be made to get business going.

ITEMS
S. D. Porteus, formerly a Vic. State-
school teacher, and latterly head of the
School for Feeble-minded Children at Bell-
street, Fitzroy, under the Education Depart-
ment, is beginning to receive international
recognition. For the last two years he hast
been associated with Professor It. J. A.
Berry, of Melb. University, in research work
in regard to brain development. Now lie
has left Sydney for America, where he will
carry on for a year at the Vineland Re-
search Laboratories.

There isn’t a man who, to all appearances,
knows less what fear is than Wilkins, the
ollicial photographer who is making records
for the Commonwealth Government. Wher-
ever the Aussies cared to go this particular
camera-fiend went with them, and was up
and over in a long succession of escapades, a
hundred times blandly operating his machine
with bullets plugging the atmosphere all
about him. Yet he was only once perfoi’ated,
and that time not seriously.

Home-grown Dr. J.C.Verco, after practising
in Adelaide for. between 40 and 50 years, has
decided to retire this month. The solid but
sprightly old man’s opinion has been looked
upon as the last word wjfli the S. An?. medi-
cal profession, and his retirement will be a
big loss to the State. He will now be able
to give more attention to his beloved shells,
of which he has one of the largest collec-
tions anywhere.
There is only one thing to be said in favor
of appointing Cohen, M.L.A., to the N. S.
Wales Bench —that similar scandals are
fairly common in the country from which
we get our legal traditions. Ridley and
Darling, for example, were political appoint-
merits. Neither had had much more
technical experience than Cohen.
The Bar was scandalised over both
these cases, and said so vehemently.
Ridley proved as bad a Judge as his
bitterest critic expected. Darling,
on the other hand, is one of the
soundest men on the Bench. His
one weakness is his habit of mak-
ing jokes-—and even they sound
100 per cent, better than they read.
Ansell Robin, who through indif-
ferent health lately gave up head-
mastering at the Ballarat C. of E.
Grammar School, has been suc-
ceeded there by Ernest Villiers-
Butler, nephew of that kindly
Anglican prelate, Arthur Green.
Bishop Green saw to the establish-
ment of the school during his hard-
working years in charge of the Bal-
larat diocese, and latterly the
establishment has had a good push
ahead, owing to the increasing fat-
ness of the Western District. Vil-
liers-Butler, some years ago, was
favorably known as boss of the C.
of E. Grammar School at Camper-
down, the region of the mammoth
cow and the “elephant” sheep.
“Bertie” Lloyd, D. 5.0., the sth
Artillery Division’s new General, is
one of the successes of the war. He
was a subaltern in 1914, a staff-
captain after the Evacuation, and
in France he got the Fifth Brigade.
Wherever Lloyd went brass but-
tons and white lanyards blazed.
And yet he was liked. Talking once
to a Battery on the subject of his
abhorrence—the dirty, ill-dressed,
Gprblimey type of soldier—he said
with dramatic gloom: “He is not
ornamental and lie is not useful;
he is discreditable and he is un-
trustworthy; and you will find if
you watch him that he rarely dies unless by
disease or accident.” A great phrase-maker
is Lloyd, with a vast repertory of stories,
drawing-room and otherwise.
When Paderewski, now almost President
of Poland, was in Australia, Bill Dvson was
a caricaturist still in the bud, but
most daring in _ pursuit of victims. He
wanted the hairy _ piano-smasher, went
for him with premeditated audacity, and at
length was permitted to sketch while Pad.
practised. Dyson worked up nearer and
nearer, and at length the pianist’s eye fell
upon the drawing. He sprang aloft in a
sort of ecstasy. “How!” he cried. “You
haft know ze mother of me—what! Ach,
yes! Bet iss ze poor mother.” He kissed the
drawing. “Ze poor, poor lee-dle mother!”
He kissed the picture twice. “Her.-!” he
cried, wheeling on the artist. “I giff you
von pounds for eet —two pounds for eet —
tree!” Dyson closed promptly.
“Groper” : Gone over the hill in Westralia
Frank McMillan, one of the best drivers that
Australia has romanced about. But there’s no
romance about this. As the outcome‘of a
wrangle in Coolgardie £5O was put up to
bet that Mac could pilot two dozen horses
and a coach up Bailey-street, turn them, and
navigate the outfit back to the starting
point. He did it easily.
Old man Coldham, father of the Coranga-
mite (Vic.) Parliamentary candidate, left
a unique mark on the surface of Victoria.
In the early days he owned Grassdale estate
in the Wannon district, and instead of
fences erected huge earthen walls 6ft.
high. Though battered and worn, many
still remain, and as rabbit flats are dinkum.
Why Coldham went to this expense when
everyone else ran up log fences is a mystery.
“Good breaks for fires started by a blazing
vocabulary,” some neighbors suggest.
The Australian Captain Ross Smith, who
piloted the Handley Page ’bus from Egypt
to India, received a compliment from the
admiring Hun flying men in Palestine, One
morning a Boche machine hovered over the
A.F.G. camp and dropped a parcel addressed
to Captain Smith. It contained a box of
excellent cigarettes. By the way, the day
following the big battle at GaZa on April 19,
1917, a Hun flier hovered over the Camel
Corps Headquarters and dropped a list of
the Cameliers they had killed and taken
prisoners. Another ’plane followed later
with a bundle of letters from the Captured
men to their relatives.
Gritty W'estraila has now 10 winners of
the V.C., the latest being James Parkes
Woods—-although South Aus. may Claim
him, as he originally Came from Gawier. He
gave a remarkable exhibition of valor in
France. Although there isn’t much of him
(he is so short that with neck craned he
could only just reach the minimum height
requirement of the A.1.F.), he and a mate,
who had become isolated, took a dangerous
Hun possie, which otherwise would have
taken them, and captured seven machines
guns. Then the amazing bantam held the
fort while his mate went back and brought
up reinforcements.

MAJOR ARTHUR
MORRISBY, of
Tasmania, soldier
and politician.
“Mustard Gold” puts an old mate to bed :
Jack Hoolan’s death recalls the rough, pioneering
times of the Labor Party in Bananaland. Hoolan,
the party’s lieutenant, always truculent, voluble
with much real eloquence, and fearless, commanded
more deference from the Ministerial phalanx than
any of his colleagues. He was a man of deep
emotion, and I have seen him in tears in the
midst of a speech—he was appealing for some
underdog and had noted the stony indifference
of a Government sure of the vast herds of sheep
and cattle behind it. Hoolan’s return to Croydon
in recess was the occasion of wild congratulation
for political services rendered to his miner con-
stituents. A man of medium height, much be-
whiskered, with a witty tongue and a genial laugh
—that was John Plumper Hoolar. In the Mundic
Miner (Iloolan’s own) his bizarre personal column —
“Old Bill Smith has just returned from the
metropolis after a month’s heavy shicker,” and
similarly flattering testimonials—together with the
editorial diatribes launched at the reptile con-
temporary, the Etheridge Mining Journal, stand
for ever in proof of his journalistic virility.
Usually his home was at Little River, normally
a small, tortuous stream down which flood-waters
sometimes rushed in torrential deluge, and fre-
quently lapped the doorway of the little pub his
sister kept on the bank.
East Greta (N.S.W.) coal-miners felt
good when the cable mentioned that Lieut.
Joseph Maxwell had added the V.C. to his
M.C. and D.C.M. Maxwell was an East
Greta coaiie before he enlisted, and “hard
places” didn’t worry him on the other job.
He got the D.C.M. in November, 1917, and
the M.C. in March, 1918. The Y.C. was
earned at Estrees, on October 3, for the
usual daredevil stunt in which a nest of
Hun guns was the party of the other part.
Follen Bishop, the English water-color
artist who came to Adelaide shortly before
the war and has stayed there ever since, is
about to return home. His landscapes of
local scenery have found a sale at good
prices, but critics object that his atmosphere
is rather that of rural England than the
hard clear air of South Australia. His
wife, who exhibits as Florence Fitzgerald,
is also an accomplished painter.
Cabled that Frank Anstey, the M.H.R.
whose tongue Billy Hughes fears most, is
to attend the International Labor Con-
ference which is to meet in Lausanne
while the Peace Conference is meeting in
Paris. Anstey’s last visit to France was
made with the Australian press delegates:
He represented the Labor papers. The
other apostles picked him up in London,
whither he had travelled before the mast,
going through the Panama Canal and
spending some valuable weeks in U.S.A.
There wasn’t a better-liked member of the
press party. He would rarely talk in
Public; but just about the cleverest speech
heard during the tour was one he made in
Ireland. A dozen times in the course of
his whirlwind oratory he malevolently led
’em on to expect an outbreak of Bol-
shevism ; and just as often, when he had
taken them up to the brink, he swung off
into an impish jest.
A WAYSIDE ROMANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I see her pass when o’er the hill
The red sun lifts an eye,
And from the fence the magpies fill
The world with song, and sigh:
“Alas! that this brown queen must go
In tattered print, her true,
Rich auburn wealth unkempt, and no
Sweet gems at her white throat, a toe
Protruding from her shoe.’’
She drives a waggon full of cans
To Weir and back again,
And, watching her, a thousand plans
Are drifting in my brain.
To take her from the dairy herd,
The dreary realm of cow,
To have her gowned in silk, and furred,
And with the plumes of some rare bird
To deck her marble brow.
She’s beautiful, her smile is sweet,
Her body’s girlish grace
Cries out for loveliness. I’d seat
Her in some splendid place;
With languid pearls I’d lace her breast
And her rich tresses dew,
And have her taught all that is best
In art and truth, so to invest
Her soul with glory too.
I’d have her shine where gentle dames
In gilded halls are met,
And lead in what the city names
Its most exclusive set.
But she is proud, and makes it clear
I may not intervene.
In fact I’m knapping metal here,
I’m old, and bald, and fond of beer,
And haven’t got a bean!
VICTORIA
BII.LY T.

“J.1.M.”: When John Monash was to
be knighted on the field, a guard of honor
was formed which included only the
smartest-looking Diggers of the near-by
divisions. Staff-officers perspired in their
efforts to arrange the parade, and when
they had done fussing Billjim was supposed
to be keyed up to a pitch of reverential
hysteria. Suddenly a motor tooted, parade
“shunned,” Royal Salute was sounded, and,
followed by a flock of red tabs, George V.
hurried on to the field. All eyes were
glued on the principals while H.M. drew
a sword or riding crop (I forget which)
and touching the kneeling general said:
“Arise, Sir John,” or words to that effect.
Just then I heard a titter behind me and
turned round in time to see one Digger in
the rear rank kneeling whilst his cobber
hit him on the s ; de of the head with his
bayonet, saying “Get up, you

“F. Dressing” : “To damage to W.A A.C.’s
underclothing, £1 10s.” This rare official
entry (a fine) appears in a Digger’s pay-
book. Imagine what the wife would say if
she found it! But it is not as incriminating
as it looks. Several Aussies at an artillery
school in England, seeing the military rai-
ment of some W.A.A.C.’s laid out for inspec-
tion, decided to repair unto their pals dis-
guised. That’s how it happened.
Careful estimates set down the cost of the
permanent way for air traffic between
London and Sydney at £4O per mile, or £9O
per mile including sufficient flying stock
for a preliminary express service. That is
as compared with the £17,000 per mile
which, so far, has been the average railway
cost in Australia. The sky mail between
New York and Chicago has been run in its
early stages at a cost of
2s. 4d. per mile tra-
velled by each vessel. If
a craft carrying 50 pas-
sengers could be run at
even four times that
rate it would represent
about 2*d. per mile per
passenger, which would
make the fare hetween
Sydney and Adelaide
£0 7s. 6d., the circum-
bendibus by Melbourne
being cut out. The
heavy freight problem,
of course, won’t be set-
tled till rates are
cheaper than that, but
it will probably be
settled by the new bird
as quickly as by the old
tortoise. On the trans-
continental line at pre-
sent, goods are being
carried at less than half
the cost of carrying
them, the balance being
charged to taxes, and
on the Canberra rail-
way the situation is
even worse. So in these
cases the real discrep-
ancy between train
costs and air costs is
much less than it looks.

not to be left to pass quietly into a Con-
stantinople pawnshop.

“Syd Ney” : Nobody with any sense expects
life aboard a trooper to be all roses; but.
weary Billjim, especially if he is
returning home in a damaged con-
dition, sees no reason why it should
be all prickly-pear. With the
memory of my homeward trip still
green, I write with sympathy for
the men of the Sardinia. I hap-
pened to be in the same convoy
as the Gaika, concerning whose
voyage a military inquiry was re-
cently held in Melbourne. There
is one thing that will always
rankle. We came by the Panama
route, which entails a long spell in
the tropics. During those swel-
tering days we were regularly fed
on hot soup, roast meat, boiled
spuds (of a sort) and sometimes
boiled fruit and rice. That was
not bad tucker, if it had been
cooked decently. But to see the
stewards running along the deck
with iced water for the officers’
dining-tables, and to peep at those
birds dining like first-class passen-
gers. while we swilled, like hogs,
made many a good Aussie boil. In
hundreds of cases officers and men
came out of the same house. But
in these matters we don’t seem
able to slough the English idea that officers
and men necessarily belong to different
castes.

“C.V.E.”: This was one of the best com-
pliments ever paid to the Aussies in France.
The occasion was Jerry’s final effort to break
through. Villers Bretonneux had been
partly captured; Villers Wood was falling
into their hands and Huns were swarm-
ing along the Amiens road. A British
staff-officer was hurrying the civilians from
a hamlet beyond Villers. Two very old
women were laboriously carting out their
little odds and ends from a small estaminet
and piling them on a hand-cart. Just as
they were about to get away, one of them
shrieked "Vive Ausirdlie! Vive Australia!"
and the leading mob of Aussies, rushed up
from Armentieres, swung round the corner,
bayonets fixed —same old devil-may-care
grins, same old curses. The old women
looked up at the impatient staff-officer. “Soldat
Austrdlie ?” they queried. " Oui, Madam.'’'
“Tres tien,” said the old Frenchwomen, and
immediately started to transfer their goods
back into their homes.

Cairo guides tell all the tourists that Moses
ended his perilous voyage in the ark of bul-
rushes on its shores. A party of Diggers
visited this historic landing-place one after-
noon, and held their noses over the rank mud
and filth. The following afternoon they
returned with two Aussie sisters. The same
Gippo guide took possession of the party and
led them to a dry, clean spot about a hun-
dred yards from the place he had pointed out
the previous afternoon. When all had halted
he announced: “Dis(, Mr. Captain, where
Moses land from de ark of bulrush in de
year two tousand befo’ de Christ.” _ “But,
Hassan,” expostulated one of the Diggers,
“you took us down there yesterday and told
us that that was where Moses came ashore.”
“Yus, yus, Mr. Captain,” said Hassan quite
unembarrassed, “but too muddy for de
ladies.”
* * * *
Adelaide is having a newspaper contro-
versy', the unusual complaint of nearly all
the "writers being that capital punishment
has not been inflicted. The saving of John
Grindell from the gallows, even after the
Executive had considered his case and de-
cided against clemency, was a triumph for
the Adelaide Mail, which obtained a state-
ment from the widow of the supposedly-
murdered man that she thinks he is very
likely not dead at all. The letter-writers
in the other papers are mostly people of the
wild district where the two men lived, and
they seem to have no doubt that it was a
murder, and a particularly brutal one But
bones found in a fire are evidence on which
it is uncomfortable to go as far as hanging
a man.
* * * *
“Sea-urchin”: Somebody did future return-
ing Expeditionaries a bad tuir by producing
the fact, as evidence of lack of discipline,
that gambling went on aboard the Sardinia.
Of course it did. It is the one diversion that
makes sea-life endurable for about seven
people out of 10 even in normal times, and
on liners that are the last word in luxury.
On a transport with no space for cricket,
deck games, dancing or any of the recog-
nised ship-board sports, what- is to be done
but gamble? Remember that the libraries
on transports are usually rotten, and that
in any case they are in the saloon, where
the rank and file can’t go; that the trip lasts
10 weeks; that none but coaling-ports are
touched. Remember finally that the Digger
is, for good or evil, a persistent gambler and
you have some measure of the agony of bore-
dom that threatens that section of the A.I.F.
which has still to return. Hitherto wise
C.O.’s have winked at gambling. From now
onwards they may not dare to. The first
transport that goes in pursuit of the world’s
record of a non-gambling run from F.ngland
to Australia will land here with the fullest
clink and the most maddened lot of officers
and men in the history of expeditionary war-
fare.

Breathless idea from a flapper :
I have a gorgeous idea, and if you don’t put it
in your old paper I’ll never borrow it again. Only
fancy, Paderewski i| President of Poland —that is
where the Polar bears come from, isn’t it? and ex-
plains his wonderful hair—at last the professionals
we love are coming to their own, and of course
other countries will keep in step. America has
just one darling, dear old Charlie Chaplin—did you
see him tat macaroni with his nose, and didn’t
it make you die laughing?—and o' course they will
make him President after Wilson, and that will
be such a relief, for when Wilson is turned off,
somebody else may have a say in the newspapers.
But the perfectly lovely notion is about Australia.
Our world-known professional star is Melba,, and
she should be made Goveruor-Generaless at once.
The other old thing—what’s his name? I’ve asked
all the house and no one knows; bait you will
find it in the telephone book—can go back to
England or wherever he came from. Fancy our own
lady-President, the first in the world; and she
would just fit the part, wouldn’t she? Just fancy
how too sweet for anything being presented at
Government House to Melba; and then instead of
making long, horrid speeches, she would sing just as
Paddy will play his answer to deputations on the
piano; and Charlie will show Congress how to
slide down stairs when it’s shikkered. And Melba
would change her title to something nice—l don’t
like Dame, do you?

That remarkable woman, Kttie Rout, who
first in Egypt and afterwards in France
has been battling bravely to save the Digger
and liis Maoriland cobber from Y r .D. infec-
tion, simply accepted the armistice as a rea-
son for working harder. “Whatever you do,
go home clean!” is the burden of her song
these days; and, as hitherto, she tells them
bow to do it. Incidentally she gives them
this certificate :—-
The Australians and Maorilandevs are _ now the
cleanest troops that have ever come to Paris. Since
September 25 to date (November 14) we have had
only one M.L. soldier and one Australian soldier in-
fected and reporting sick at Paris Centre, whereas
other troops on leave in Paris at the same time have
had hundreds of infections.

military authorities; she is too intolerant

THE SLUMP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The ending of the war has left
Us lacking in excitement:
Of lots of news we are bereft
Which with that mighty fight went,
Again we note the small event,
And heed the empty babble
Of such as sit in Parliament
And gabble, gabble, gabble.
The marriage or the death of Jones,
The headache that depressed us,
We talk of in emphatic tones
As things that interest us;
Men maundered in the recent past
Of cannons, bombs and rifles,
Who, though the sky’s still overcast,
Are now concerned with trifles.
We miss the war, and that’s a fact,
We critics at a distance,
Who thrilled each time the Huns attackt
And “met a stout resistance.”
Dry news, as in the days of yore,
We’ll soon again be fed on:
And even though more cannons roar
They’ll not be Armageddon.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

“Duglio” : An admiring Blighty paper once
called us “these big, shy cousins of ours
from ‘down under.’” Most Aussies are shy
but once start us and most of us can make
the pace. Late one night we hit a French
town, the natives of which had never before
seen a Digger’s hat. At 8 o’clock next morn-
ing we were told we would be there for
perhaps a week, and would be given a few
hours’ leave each day. An hour after our
first leave opened I passed a cobber making
his way back to the billets, and hailed him
with, “Hullo, Dig', fed up with the town
already?” He grinned. “Fed up be !
I’m going to get some washing to take down
home r

“Hawk”: “We British are stupid, and
therein lies our strength. When we are in
danger we don’t realise it, because we have
no imagination. Hence we remain calm and
come out of it always with honor.” So
Andre Maurois, quoted by S. M. ' Herald.
The Herald might blazon the quotation
over its doorway as its motto, the secret
of its success; and that success, valued in
millions, is not to be sneezed at. To say of
every proposed innovation, “The time is not
ripe” is not only easy but profitable; for you
echo the sentiments of the moneyed men over
40, and of such are the advertisers. To
cherish the advertiser by respecting all that
he respects; to be, as it were, a British butler
with the wooden face of servility for any
form of authority; to reverence things as
they are, because they are; and to oppose all
that is new, because it is new, is a mental
poise leading to the political, social, jour-
nalistic fleshpots. This fact should be bet-
ter known to the young man desiring to
smoke better cigars, or to pay off the time-
WHEN THE SOUTHERLY CAME UP.
“Gee! But I'm glad I got me kimony on.”
payment furniture. Be stupid and polite,
and open swing the gates of emolument.
Doubting this, visit the heads of Govern-
ment departments, the Ministers themselves,
or the directing powers of the big newspapers.
Be smiling, wooden, and follow Red Tape ; you
are then reliable, a man of weight and ap-
preciated by your superiors, they having like
qualities. For stupidity there is no punish-
ment ; for where would it end if we gaoled
all the fools? As a Federal Minister you
build a city in a wilderness as a lair for din-
goes, squandering millions ; as a State Min-
ister, you make a child’s contract with Nor-
ton Griffith and squander millions; or you
drop an odd million in absurd abattoirs.
What is the retribution? You and all con-
cerned hold your job ; for all is pardoned to
a fathead, if only he be “respectable.”
“Streptococcus”: The influenza bacillus is
practically uon-pathogenic to animals; that
is, it can be injected under the skin, or
into a vein, without causing any ill effects.
But the very latest view is that the bacillus
■influenza is not the true virus of the disease,
the trouble being caused by an organism that
is a filter passer. The experiments were made
on volunteers with filtrated bronchial secre-
tions from two monkeys which had an attack
of influenza. One of the volunteers was
injected with the filtrate under the skin,
and had a typical attack of the disease,
which lasted 12 days. Another volunteer
had a slight attack on the 6th day. Injec-
tions of the filtrate into the veins of other
volunteers was negative, nor did the injec-
tion of 3. c.c. of blood from a bonnet monkey
suffering from influenza have any ill effects.
It is concluded, therefore, that the infective
agent is a filter-passing organism, and that
the virus does not occur in the blood of
man or animals. On the other hand, the
.bacillus of influenza has occasionally been
found in the blood.
* * * *
“Glenirig”: Something on a large scale
is about to be attempted by a Mel-
bourne firm of silk-importers. These
men are Syrians, and they have bought
3000 acres of low-grade land near
Wensleydale, with the intention of mak-
ing a start with cocoon-producing. Dave
Oman, Minister for Silkworms, has been ap-
proached by the firm, which wants him to
import enough white mulberries to plant one-
tenth of the area ; and he is alleged to have
promised to push the Ministry in the right
direction —not a particularly lavish promise,
considering that the State has for some time
been providing all and sundry with young
trees at a penny each. The Syrians’ scheme
is to be partly co-operative, since it will
involve the cutting up of the Wensleydale
acres into “family-size” blocks and the settle-
ment of the right sort of workers thereon.
Little Willie and Annie will do their bit, of
course, and no doubt the firm shoving the
scheme along will decide what the “right sort”
of worker is; so the Government should play
safe and tack to its mulberry-trees a little
list of anti-sweating provisions.. Sweated
industries are no good to Australia.
* * * *
“Frank R”: There is another outbreak
of dreadful tales about Johnny Turk’s in-
famous treatment of our troops; but my
experience of him wasn’t at all like that.
As a stretcher-bearer I several times went
over the top and brought back men who
had fallen in or near the Turkish trenches.
Generally, Abdul had dressed their wounds
and : in serious cases had carried the men,
under cover of darkness, half-way to our
trenches, where we found them the follow-
ing morning. During the fighting at “Hill
60” I was detailed to approach almost to
the verge of a Turkish trench to bring
back a wounded Maorilander. When we
reached him we found that he had
received six different dressings, and he told
us that after his wounds had been attended
to he had been given warm coffee and
later had been ulaced over the trench
where we found him. On another occasion
a Turkish shell burst. over one of our
hospital ships and injured a nurse. When
we heard of the incident we placed a
note in a jam-tin informing Abdul of the

nurse's injury. Half an hour later the tin
was hurled back, with a note apologising
for the mishap and explaining that the
shell was defective. During the Senussi
Campaign the prisoners who were captured
when the Arabs sank the gunboat Tara
stated, on being rescued by the Duke of
Westminster’s armored cars, that what
little kindness they did receive was from
the Turkish general Nuri Bey and his
troops. Certainly I know of several of
our wounded being killed in Palestine, but
I believe that the Bedouins did it —many
of them were serving with the Turkish
army.

“Solomon Malaprop”: The Italian is a
poet and artist at the top. Hence he sup-
plied the world with St. Peter’s and Milan
Cathedral and thousands of other rparvels,
and crowded his land with pictures and
statuary, and had superfluous artists for ex-
port—the men who devised tin Taj Mahal in
India and did building for the Mongol em-
pire, and otherwise scattered themselves over
the globe. But the rest of him is hard
business, he being the person who invented
pawnbrokery and algebra and modern bank-
ing and book-keeping by double entry and
the wholesale bribery of political candidates.
So President Wilson selected the wrong kind
of audience when he discoursed at Home
about “binding the nations together by
friendship” and “setting up a new inter-
national psychology” and about “th c cre-
ation of a real new atmosphere” as the sur-
est guarantee against future wars. The
brutal German wallows in hazy, dreamy,
friendly, brotherly, philanthropic, school-
masterly phrases like these. The rather
kindly Italian doesn’t. He is someth!jg like
the Hollander, who produced" the wonderful
school of Dutch painters, while he pursued
the herring-fishery with a fury that beat the
fury of the Inquisition in a canter and sold
schnapps to all the human race. A statesman
of experience who wants to do business with
the Italian addresses himself to that side
of his mind which-invented book-keep'ng by
double entry, not to the aspect which devised
the Leaning Tower of Pisa. And the hard
diplomatist who has a trade to do with Hol-
land and has any sort of a case addresses
himself to the country as the father of
Schnapps, not the father of Rembrandt.
“Centurion”: “Bill Bowyang’s” conten-
tion that a few of the Cameliers came into
action at Katia on the second day of the
Romani actions, when the Turkish retreat
had begun, does nothing to uphold his rather
extravagant claim that his corps was fore-
most in the actions of this retreat and
that it had on one occasion “got the Light
Horsemen out of a hole.” The fact remains
that the Cameliers failed badly to connect
at Bir-el-abd, and let the A.L.11. down.
This because they had not trained their
mounts to long unwatered spells, but had
followed the stable routine laid down in the
drill-book while camped on the canal. A
camel watered every day expects his daily
drink, and to get him to go for days without
water he has to be educated to it. There
were no better chaps going than the camel-
iers, but they weren’t the whole team and
the little yellow dog under the waggon, and
their “mounts” simply couldn’t compare
with Australian horses.
* * * *
Stuart Town, formerly known as Iron-
bark, near Wellington (N.S.W ). has
strengthened Australia’s gold reserve. An
ancient lady resident there died on Christ-
mas Day, which was her 86th. After the
funeral her friends and relatiyes made a
search and found .-£1350 in moneys • The
greater part was sewn up in the bed-ticking.
A canvas bag held 650 sovereigns, none of
later date than 15 years back. An old-
fashioned flounced skirt had £lOO in its
folds. The hoarder is said to have had two
bottles of gold, saved, no doubt, from old
Ironbark days. But the search party couldn’t
find" that plant.
* * * *
The five miles of tram cable which
reached Melbourne at last in the Austral-
glen was a Jonah cargo. First shipped in
the Ivyarra, it received its baptism of sea-
water when that vessel was caught by a
U-boat. Recovered, it next headed for the
South in the Barunga, which was also outed
by the enemy. Then the Boorara received
the unlucky consignment, and the crew of
that vessel blamed the long steel Jonah
for being twice torpedoed and beached. The
Boorara, by the way, was originally a
German liner, which tried to bolt to sea
at the outbreak of war; but a well-placed
shot across her bow from the Port Phillip
batteries (the first fired in Australia for
war purposes) put' a full-stop to the
manoeuvre.
THE ONLY PLACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gissiffens was a nervous coot,
A hunted look had he,
fie made a tour the bolts to shoot
Directly after tea,
And locked the doors of all the sheds,
And started peeping under beds.
Thieves were his shivering spirit’s blight,
His conversation’s theme ;
They came, whene’er-he dozed, at night,
To rob him in a dream,
And half the time he. prowled around
Investigating every sound.
He disappeared; he left no trace;
The neighbors thought it odd ;
Then we dropped on his hiding place—
Oissifens was in quod!
And straightway hied we out to see
If we could probe the mystery.
What had he done? Or who? And why?
Could nothing set him free ?
There was contentment in his eye;
He’d no desire to flee,
But, 9miling, shook his shaven head.
“I’m safe from burglars here,” he said.
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.

PRACTICAL PRESCRIPTION
AGAINST STOMACH ACIDITY.
BY A SPECIALIST.
“Nine-tenths of all cases of stomach trouble
nowadays,” says a leading specialist, “are
caused by too much acid. In the beginning
the stomach itself is not diseased, but if this
acid condition is allowed to continue the
acid is very likely to eat into the stomach
walls and produce stomach ulcer, which may
render a radical surgical operation necessary,
even to prolong life. Therefore an acid
stomach’ is really a dangerous condition
and should be treated seriously. It is
utterly useless to take pepsin and ordinary
stomach tablets. The excess acid must be
neutralised by the administration of an
efficient antacid. Foir this purpose physicians
nearly always recommend taking half a tea-
spoonful of bisurated magnesia in a little
water after each meal. Large quantities
may be used, if necessary, as it is absolutely
harmless. But be sure to get the bisurated
magnesia, as other forms of magnesia have
not the same action in the stomach as , tae
bisurated, and frequently do more harm than
good.”— Advt.
