POLITIONAL
POINTS
The N. S. Wales political machines are
getting ready for a State election in Sep-
tember.
There is a struggle going on underground
for the right to export gold without Govern-
ment sanction. The demand needs to be
looked at very hard. One thing we ought
to be particularly careful about is not to
cause a rapid slump in the general level of
prices; and we shall help to maintain prices
by hanging on to as much of our gold as pos-
sible. The maintenance of prices will be
all in our favor because we have lately bor-
rowed enormously; and every shrinkage in
prices makes the debt larger, for all practi-
cal purposes.
A PARTICULAR CUSTOMER.
“The Bolsheviks are flooding the country with
worthless paper money.”— Cable.
BOLSHEVIK, JUNIOR: “ Gimme a ha’penny-
worth of lolliesky; an’ don’t wrap them
up in one o’ your dirty banknotes.”
Monash is attending to the military side
of demobilisation in England. Meßeath
(Chairman of the Commission which raked
ithe Defence administration) is to attend to
the business side. Pearce’s part of the busi-
ness will be no side in particular—just
•‘side.”
The Bulletin was frowned upon when it
laughed at Hughes’s fantastic claim that he
had “solved the metal problem.” Now, at
the very first bit of rough road, the whole
blessed scheme has broken down. Metal
is piling up in Australia and the Mt.
Morgan Co. has announced that, though it
will struggle on as long as it can, the
position is one of the gravest anxiety. Ihe
fact, plain enough from the outset, was
that Hughes never had a scheme to ensure
buyers. All he had was one to restrict
sellers.
Australia’s Protectionist tariff is one of
the things that are always coming but never
happen. Fisher promised it both times he
was in office. Hughes promised it in two
public speeches. Watt promised it in ldll-
Even Cook promised it. But always it is
defeated by the unwritten alliance between
the importers who don’t. want Australian
industries, because industries would interfere
with imports, and the Bolshevik crow! which
doesn’t want them because they might create
an employer class which is accursed. Be-
tween these two foreign elements mere
ordinary Australia falls in.
Thus Acting-Prime Minister Watt, in his
budget speech:—
Large areas of country in all parts of the
[Northern] Territory previously held under annual
tenure have during the past year been converted
into pastoral leases of forty-two years’ duration,
with conditions as to improvements and stocking.
Twelve years ago a party of Federal poli-
ticians visited Darw’in, and wrote many
things about the Territory. One was that
the country had been locked up by the South
Aus. Government on 42-year leases, and
hands were held up in horror at the outrage.
If leases did fall in they were renewed only
year by year. Now Glynn, from South Aus.,
who is ihe Minister responsible for the Terri-
tory, has locked the lands for another 42
years. Why? If there are people wanting
the lands, shorter leases would have been
ample encouragement. In Queensland since
the Act of 1884, no less than 70,000,000
acres of pastoral country has been settled
in grazing-farms and grazing homesteads.
That is within 35 years; yet the Common-
w'ealth Government is locking country up
in vast leases for nearly half a century. Gil-
ruth reports finding sub-artesian water
along the Macarthur-Katherine route, and a
big artesian supply has been tapped near
Lake Eyre. Probably that explains the milk
in the nut.
Theoretical Bolshevism, at all events, is
now the order of the day among those who
make most noise at Labor meetings. The
French Labor Mission was practically
howled down both at Newcastle and Sydney
because it didn’t approve of the “no-Govern-
ment, no-law and no-property”
ideal. John Storey and Frank
Tudor and their kind are quite
back numbers now as Labor lead-
ers ; in fact, part of the Sydney
Trades Hall meeting which boo-
hooed the French Laborites stated
candidly that there is no hope till
John Storey is dead. Instead of
leading the revolution it looks as
if he might be hanged by the revolution for
not going fast enough—hanged from the
post-office tower as a disguised minion of
capitalism.
A recent S.M. Herald tale was about
the United States. It admitted that that
country’s exports, now mostly manufactured
goods, are 2J times what they were before
the war; that it has taken Britain’s place
as the world’s chief creditor; that it
has bought up in the last four years
£2,000,000,000 worth of securities, formerly
held in Europe, and so on. And it never
once hinted that the United States has a
mighty tariff which helps it to do
these wonderful things, while Australia, a
place about the same size, with a mighty
free list, sticks in the mud and goes deeper
in debt. The Tory press is a real Archi-
medean lever. As a leaver of things unsaid
it has no rival.
If a large section of the wheat cockies
have their way the wheat-pool system isn’t
going to vanish after the war. There are
already co-operative grain cos. in all the
wheat States, and they reckon they can run
the whole business without private agents
and shippers, not to mention speculators,
getting a cut out of the crop. The big
obstacle to climb over is finance; but it is
hoped that the Commonwealth Bank will
come to the rescue. If the thing can be
worked and is properly managed, the eco-
nomic advantages to the farmer and Aus-
tralia are obvious. The opposition that will
be forthcoming from the big wheat shippers
is also obvious.
S. Australia has been the pioheer of
many reforms, and it looks as though it
may soon have another to its credit. Any-
way, Commissioner of Insolvency Mitchell,
who knows something of business as well as
law, has started a campaign for the intro-
duction of the French system of compelling
every tradesman to keep a set of books on
a statutory plan. It ought to save the
Taxation offices a few thousands a year to
start with.
It is stated openly that the point on
which Clemenceau of France and Wilson of
the U.S. disagree is that the latter wants
Germany to be admitted at once
to the League of Nations and
the former doesn’t. There have
been meetings of the Great
Powers ever since there were
Great Powers, but the U.S. has
been consistently absent. Which
means that it hasn’t dined regu-
larly with the Hun. France and
Britain and Italy have, and
they have found the animal’s
manners awful. At the Berlin
conference of 1878, though .he
hadn’t then reached anything
like his subsequent height of
arrogance, he was frankly un-
bearable. Bismarck’s roar broke
in on everybody’s speech, and he
was an ultimatum all the time. The Ilun
would require to be licked in three more
wars before he would be pleasant company.
The Argus never looks at w’ages questions
from the employees’ point of view. It snorts
at a poor drudge whose pittance has been
raised from 15 to 30 shillings a week, com-
plaining of his enmity to the employer who
doubles his own income every few years.
Yet the difference between envious wage-
earners and intolerant wage-payers is so
marked that even a Tory journal might be
expected to notice it occasionally. The
workers never reckon on amassing great
wealth by means of their labor, whereas
their employers do. To judge from the
Argus’s tone, one would suppose that the
LYNCH, M.II.R.
( W[?]rriwa).
associated tramway drivers and conductors,
for instance, were artfully scheming to die
rich.
The low-wage and long-hour enthusiast
among the farming community, who regards
the wretched remuneration and rotten con-
ditions of the old days as the one and only
hope of the industry, should reflect on the
fact that almost all his labor-saving machin-
ery has originated in America, the pre-
eminently high-wage country. So long as
brawn is valued above brain, and muscle
has to perform what can be done by machin-
ery, so long will farming remain a dreary,
bullocking, poorly-productive sort of busi-
ness. Had America wallow T ed along in the
belief that a 12-bob-a-week Hodge was the
mainstay of the industry, Hodge would still
have been delving away with much sweat
and little result.
A fear arises that Monash may have
arranged for repatriating so many Aus-
tralian soldiers before-Pearce comes footling
along that the latter will hardly be able to
find a decent excuse for his six months’ holi-
day and his expenses bill* Monash has fixed
for 15 transports to sail in January with
16,328 soldiers and nurses and an unspeci-
fied number of dependants and munition-
workers. He hopes to have similar or even
larger February, March and April contin-
gents away and others arranged for by the
time Pearce arrives. Monash fought bis way
to the supreme Australian command amid
the fierce competition of Avar, partly by cour-
age, partly by his wonderful capacity for
organisation. Pearce was a bi dding carpen-
ter when he was younger, and has been an
inferior machine politician since he has been
older. Still he is Monash’s boss, and he has
to annex the credit of the repatriation job
lest people should judge him by the muddled
accounts of his department.
A FRIEND AT LAST.
B ESS: “You seem happy, Marjorie. Is Bobbie
paying you more attention?"
MARJORIE: “No; but he’s slopped writing
to all the other girls since Mr. Webster
put up the postage.”
Even Gunga Din is warning the world
that he isn’t going to much longer put up
with such idiocies as the growing of wool
for others to wash and weave. His par-
ticular trouble is linseed; and his Govern-
ment has told ours that if we want his oil
we must buy it and not his raw material.
State finance becomes more and more of a
labyrinth. The Ryan Government’s latest
idea is that if any deceased person held
shares in a co. which didn’t belong to
Qfieensland, but happened to have assets
there, however small, or if the co. that the
deceased was in held shares in another co.
which had assets in Queensland, then his
estate must pay separate probate duty in
Queensland on his proportion of these assets.
If the other States follow suit then it will
become a case of paying duty three times
over—once to the State where the co. was
located, once to all other States in which
the co. had assets, and once to the Com-
monwealth. A person W'ith ar interest in a
concern like the A.M.P., which has assets
everywhere, would then have an exciting
time after death in addition to what Peter
might say to him at the Gate. Probably
Ryan hopes that the other States won’t
follow suit, so that Queensland will collect,
but not be collected from. These catch-
penny devices don’t pay much and cost a
lot in stationery and clerks’ wages, but they
give small-minded politicians a feeling _of
warmth in the stomach because they think
they have got ahead of the reptile contem-
porary across the border.
The man w 7 ho took over the misfortunate
oil-mines at Newnes (N.S.W.), being struck
wuth a demand for 25 per cent, increase in
wages, reckons the place will have to shut
up again. It would probably have shut,
anyhow. The whole tragedy of Newnes is
expressed in that one line in the tariff:
“Kerosene, free.” However, Minister Millen
reckons new industries are superfluous, so
why worry?
SUPPRESSED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Mr. Webster believes that all newspapers in
Australia should be suppressed.” —Sydney Sunday
Times.
Statesmen may blunder, politicians fail,
Even a P.M.G. may mess his job.
The burglar burgles on the midnight pale
When cribs are easiest to crack and rob.
Our human nature is a doubtful thing;
The fastest train is easiest to miss;
And it may chance that lips above a ring
Of wedded bondage seem the best to kiss.
All these are simple facts; and the distress
Of all the world makes fodder for the press.
Why should the public know ? ’Twere easier far
To tread the narrow pathway or the broad
If no one even guessed the things that arc
Or dreamed of chance or change or truth or fraud.
Keep it all dark! Be silent as the grave!
It is not good the public eye should see
The way its own paid servitors behave,
Or trace the antics of a P.M.G.
Dinkum reform and permanent redress
Must come by the suppression of the press.
Thus should it be if Webster had his way:
A smiling land devoid of wrath or rows
Wherein, with ignorance to match the day,
The people wandered to and frp as cows.
Who then would rage for letters gone amiss
Or telegrams that wait delivery still,
While, tootling-in an ecstasy of bliss,
The gentle statesman sang a stomach pill?
The Webster-song would get a chance to bless
Its hearers, but for mockery of the press!
And yet, and yet—without the printed word,
Without the inky blast that counts for fame
The pride of statecraft might be held absurd
And few would even know great Webster’s name.
Without those columns under headlines fair
What chance had P.M.G. of public praise
When his report against a land’s despair
Convinced the crowd that losing letters pays?
Ah, Webster, spare one tootle or caress
For that which makes and mocks —the people's
press !
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

The TOP-NOTCHScotch.
ABORGINAUTIES
MADE IN AUSTRAKIA
BARNES.LINSEED OIL
“Jacko”: Jimmy Susu, up here in Torres
Strait, has been pondering the question of just who
is “Big Guv’ment.” If things didn’t go quite right,
he was given lurid pictures of what “Big Guv’ment”
would do when it came on its periodical visit. At
first he looked with a certain amount of awe at the
little auxiliary schooner which brought round the
administrative officials from Thursday Island, and
he sang Gorsave with much fervor. But later on
another “Big Guv’ment” came along in a steamer
from down south; and Jimmy, who hadn’t been
down south, enlarged his ideas somewhat and added
“double forte” to the Anthem. Then he was told
to buck up and get the drawing-room dusted, and
put an extra shine on himself, because this time the
Big Guv’ment was coming 1 This Guv’ment was
certainly bigger, so far as numbers went; yet Jimmy
was disappointed—this Big Government didn’t wear
as much red braid and gold lace as decorated even
the village policeman’s uniform! Jimmy then tried
to gauge the status by the amount of free sweets
and calico and tobacco that each Big Guv’ment
brought; but he found this wasn’t always a reliable
guide. Some little time back Jimmy took a trip
down south himself, and has come back with a tale
that there everybody seems to be Big Guv’ment, and
that anyway there’s nothing to be frightened about,
because he himself heard “plenty white man say
dey no care along plurry Guv’ment! ” So now, when
Jimmy, squatting in the shade of a cocoanut, is
informed that “Big Guv’ment come,” he simply
turns a languid eye upward, calls to the missus to
get a few shells and curios on hand, and replies,
“Ah, we know dat one!”
“Potjostler Junior”: Tommy Atkins
doesn’t score all the points. Just after we took Bray
I had to go in for canteen stores. We used the
battalion officer’s mess cart, and, as it was raining
hard, the roads were execrable. On our way home
our old moke knocked up and it looked as though
we’d have to sleep in the cart that night. Just as
we had given up hope we saw ahead a mob of horses,
belonging to a Tommy regiment, feeding in a field
alongside the road. A gleam of joy appeared on my
driver’s dial. He coaxed the old crock along until
he got abreast of the other horses. Then pulling off
til? road, he unharnessed our stiff moke and turned
him adrift. The horse wandered over to the mob and
commenced feeding, while Billjim filled his pipe,
crawled into the mess cart and had a smoke. Half
an hour later he picked up the bridle, went into the
mob and brought out a big brown horse, harnessed
him up and off we trotted home. The prad we
drove away was a young brown ; the one we turned
loose was an old bay. But the Tommies never sus-
pected anything.
“Monyah”: “Frank It.” (B. 19/12/T8)
wants to know how many miles an alligator can
travel overland. My opinion is that the reptile
never goes further than a few yards from water.
1-Ie will crawl up on the bank and there lie perfectly
still, ready to glide back into the liquid at the
sightest alarm. It is a lesson in artistic diving
to see a big fellow take the water with scarcely the
smallest ripple. Alligators are not foragers on
land. “Frank R.’s” account of their making four-
and six-mile trips overland will need further cor-
roboration before this writer believes it.
• * * *
“Dug-out”: In the cattle country of North
Bananaland I came across a lonely grave by the road-
side, at the foot of a giant gum. About foun square
feet of bark had been stripped, and roughly cut into
the wood were these words: —,
Bill Harrigan lies here.
He didn’t know it was loaded.
“O’P.”: Gordon and I were camped in a
hut on the side of Mt,. Heemskirk (Tas.). For a
water supply we .depended on a little pool we had
dug, which caught the soakage from the
About 4000 frogs took a lease of this pool, and
their night music was worse than a brass band. One
night, exhausted by the vain effort to sleep, Gordon
took out a plug of gelignite and blew the pool
heavenward. After about a quarter of an hour’s ex-
quisite silence the blanky croaking started again over
our heads. Some of the frogs had landed on the roof.
* * * *
“Werrimee” : N.S.W. Chief See. Fuller, in
response to the almost daily reports of disastrous
bush fires, has issued a special appeal to all who
use phosphorus baits to exercise the greatest possible
vigilance. I have for years been urgently warning
the man outback against the lunacy of dropping
phosphorus about the landscape. It doesn’t get rid
of rabbits even with a severe drought doing its
best to help, but it kills our useful birds, poisons
fish in the streams (through the baits being washed
in during rain), besides endangering pastures, crops
and stock. I had thought Fuller was quite aware
of these risks, because his Department compels
landowners to use phosphorus. The same Department
is supposed also to administer the Birds Protection
Act. When the law aims at protecting birds with
one Act and causes their wholesale destruction by
another Act, it is more asinine than usual.

“Jaymac” : Tell me honestly, what use as
tucker is the quandong? I’ve sampled the red dis-
appointment in various places and always found
the fruit bitter and unappetising. Did Binghi have
any use for the vegetable as food, or did he merely
use the stones as buttons for his fancy waistcoat ?
* * * *
“Wealsman”: According to Clive Lord,
secretary of Tas. Royal Society, there are only
three kinds of land snakes officially recorded in the
Speck. These are the tiger, the copper-headed or
superb, and the white-lipped whip-snake. The copper-
head, often erroneously called the “diamond snake”
—the true diamond-snake is a member of the python
family, and is not found in Tas. —is less common
than the tiger, the deadliest of all Australian ser-
pents. The whip is probably about as numerous as
the copperhead, and is found at all altitudes, from
sea-level up to 4500 ft. Lord
says that the bite of the
white-lipped crawler is seldom
if ever fatal. (Krefft de-
clared it non-venomous, but
bushmen whose dogs have
died suddenly after wrestling
with the whip speak disre-
spectfully of these ancient
authorities.) The species
may be white, dark brown,
red, green, or any intermedi-
ate shade, but always has
white markings on the lip,
sometimes extending back be-
yond the ;eyes. The tiger-
snake is found in three shades,
bush folk generally referring
to the dark specimens as black
snakes and to the lighter
samples as carpet-snakes.
They are wrong in both in-
stances, for neither the true
black nor the carpet-snake is
found in Tas. Lord says in
effect that color cannot be
regarded in the classification
of the species. If a snake
above the size of a whip-snake
is met with in the Tas. bush,
the odds are six to four on it
being a tiger. If cornered or
disturbed when mating, the
tiger shows fight readily. Like
its near relative, the cobra of
India, it distends its neck
laterally until the dark skin
shows beneath the scales,
raises itself on its propeller
and goes full speed for the
foe. The latter’s best plan then is to retire as
suddenly as he knows how, unless he is armed with
a handy stick with which he can get a clean hori-
zontal swipe at the advancing serpent. A stick is
better than a gun and infinitely better than a hobble-
chain or a stirrup-iron or an axe, or even a razor.
“Dook” : I can back up “Wandi’s” state-
ment (B. 28/11 /’ 18) about fig-trees growing in the
forks of other trees. In a public reserve in Jam-
beroo (N.S.W.) there’s a fig which started some
100 feet up, and has completely strangled its host,
rooting all round the original tree and forming a
new trunk. It is imposible to say what the original
tree was, for it has almost completely disappeared,
leaving the fig quite hollow for some 100 feet or so
up. The latter is Oft. through at the base and
looking up from the inside of the trunk faint traoes
of the old timber can be seen.
“G.A.” : Something new in real Australian
industry has been brought to Adelaide by Mathew
Kropinyerie, the educated three-quarter abo. who
takes a great interest in Binghi’S history and lan-
guage. (Mathew looks a full-blooded abo. himself,
but his mother was a half-caste. His father was
“the abo. evangelist.’’) Recently he returned from
the S.A. West Coast and brought with him examples
of native skill thereabouts in textile making—two
tam-o’-shanter caps of wombat hair, and the imple-
ments used in their manufacture. The spinning-
wheel consists of a saltbush stick about a quarter of
an inch thick and two feet in length, with two shorter
pieces pierced through and tied in the form of a
cross and fixed near one end of the main stem and at
right angles to it. The gins keep this simple piece
of mechanism in motion by rubbing the main stick
between the hand and knee, while with the other
hand they twist the wombnt hair and wind it upon
the cross pieces. The result is a tough woolly twine
which is made up by means of a crochet hook fash-
ioned from the bone of a ’roo.
* * * #
“E.S.S.”: An instance of wild waterfowl
being tamed in their native state was supplied by
a flock of pelicans at Tliylungra, West Queensland.
The homestead stands on the bank of a big lagoon,
and the pelicans, about 80 of them, had been quiet-
ened by constant feeding from the kitchen. The
bank of the lagoon drops sheer to the water a few
feet from the back door, and the birds came there
regularly at meal-times to feed on the scraps thrown
out from the kitchen. Though never very wild
when not interfered with, the pelican is usually a
lover of quiet places. From the cook’s point of
view it as valueless as the shag, but with its
enormous yellow bill and huge black and white
body it is the most conspicuous and interesting fea-
ture in the waterscape.

“Mopoke”: “Lantana’s” “snake” with
“fins” (B. 5/12/’18) is not a rare freak. I have heard
of it on one or two occasions and had one bottled
for a considerable time. Whenever arguments arose
about snakes with legs I bided my time and at the
right moment produced the specimen. It generally
meant drinks all round at somebody else’s expense.
I dug my crawler out of the ground one winter, and
shortly after contact with the spirits he shed his
outer garment, and it was then that the fin-legs
became very noticeable. They were scarcely visible
before.

THE WATER.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
When Allenby’s army smashed the Turk,
Who was the bloke who did the work?
The Aussie knows, and he’ll tell you straight
That most of the job was done by his mate,
The wonderful Aussie waler.
It was umpty-nine in the shade each day,
And the wells were spoiled in the Turkish way ;
But with nothing to eat and plenty to do
The heart of the waler carried him through,
The wonderful, wonderful waler!
For ten long weeks through the desert hot
He plugged along, and all that he got
Was a drink—-or not a drink—a day.
But did the stamina once give way
Of the wonderful Aussie waler?
Was he the one to desert his mate?
Just watch him coming up the straight,
With twenty stone of harness and man!
No wonder the Turk was an also-ran
With the wonderful, wonderful waler!
When drink was not and feeds were few,
There still was his harness that he could chew;
With a nibble or two at another’s mane
He plucked up heart to march again,
The wonderful Aussie waler!
And when everything eatable seemed to fail,
A hair or two from a neighbor’s tail
Made a pleasant meal; and there’s no doubt
They took it turn and turn about,
The wonderful, wonderful waler!
A white Australian through and through,
There’s a good time coming, old horse, for you.
There’s a paddock green, with grass to your knees,
And there you shall roll at your lordly ease,
My wonderful Aussie waler!
With a gallop or two to keep you fit —
And won’t it bring back the thrill of it!
There’s no more hardship and little work
For the cobber who broke the heathen Turk,
My wonderful, wonderful waler!
But what is this that the orders tell?
This mate of mine they’re going to sell!
To the old home paddock you’ll never come back;
They are selling you to a dirty black,
My wonderful Aussie waler!.
The times together that we’ve been through,
When all that I had in the world was you!
Out there! Out there, in a world of men
You were more than wife or sweetheart then,
My wonderful, wonderful waler!
There was trust and mateship in your eyes.
A horse has no soul! All lies, all lies!
And more than a kiss or soft lips that speak
Was your nuzzling nose against my cheek,
You wonderful Aussie waler!
A life-long slavery is your fate ?
Not while a mate can still shoot straight!
Your eyes....l need a steady hand....
Good bye, old chap....you understand,
You wonderful, wonderful waler!
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

“OUR REPRESENTATIVE.”
CALLER: “Is the manager in?”
YOUNG LADY : “He’s on active service.”
CALLER: “What about the acting-manager, then?”
YOUNG LADY: “He’s on holiday.”
CALLER: “Well, the accountant?”
YOUNG LADY: “He’s inspecting the country branch.”
CALLER: “What’s become of the business?”
YOUNG LADY (with dignity) : “I am here.”
“Nitro”: The snake with fins recorded
by “Lantana” (B. 5/12/’18) is right. I met this
queer exhibit eight summers ago at Tweed Heads
(N.S.W.). He was within 6ft. of my tent, making
for the doorway when I finished him. Didn’t know
he possessed the fins, or whatever they may be, until
I audited the corpse. It started many snake yarns
when exhibited among the campers, but nobody could
name the specimen. He was about 2ft. long and
reddish brown in color —no spots.
* * * #
“Yarrum”: Worst night I ever spent was
up a leaning tree, in the moonlight, with a very
wild cow pawing madly at my shadow. Between
paws the mad thing gazed up at me murderously
with fiery eyes. It made me feel sick, for I’d been
treed a long time and was only about eight feet
from grass. After seven hours of it the cow lunatic
knocked off to give sustenance to her day-old calf
a couple of chains away. Then I dropped and tried
to run, but couldn’t. The beast started in pursuit;
but she was as exhausted as I was and fell on her
head. After a hit I crawled to the nearest fence —-
luckily a strong one —and dragged myself under the
bottom rail, where I lay exhausted and slept. When
I awoke the relentless beast was breathing hard on
my face.. I can smell the clover yet.
* * * *
“Gouger”: The day had been fearfully
hot. The old hatter, sighing heavily as he lifted
the billy from the fire, was vaguely puzzled. It
did not seem tea-time, somehow, yet the sun was
' surely failing in its light. The hatter (he told
me the yarn himself) glanced at the tangle of
tropical scrub a hundred yards in front of the hut.
Ten minutes before a few glaring sunrays had
struck down through the dense foliage. Now it was
one mass of shadow. Not a.leaf moved; not one of
the scrub birds sang. Even on the open forest
country at the back of the hut was a deathly still-
ness. “Damn me if I don’t think we’re goin’ to
have a cyclone, an’ a wopper, too. You’d better
coil up in your holler log ter-night, Bluey. . No
good campin’ in ther fireplace. Yer’ll catch yer
death er cold.” The old man was speaking to the
big fat yellow-striped gohanna that steadily watched
his friend for any bits of corned beef he might
throw away. The hatter finished his tea, stood in
the doorway of the hut and looked towards the
west. There was no sun visible now, but a dense
veil of cloud was rapidly approaching. “Best get
ter bunk,"” he mumbled to the lonely watcher. “This
is goin’ to be a real snorter.” With all his clothes
on, he climbed on to the sapling bunk, pulled the
bag blanket over him, and lay still. The gohanna,
seeing nothing more doing, waddled off to his hol-
low log. The little brown fly-catching lizard ran
swiftly across the floor of the now darkened hut, and
coiled up in his home under the old man’s bunk.
Not a sound. Soon, from far away across the moun-
tain tops, came a low hum. It increased rapidly
to a muffled roar, the heated air suddenly grew cool,
and then, as the furious wind struck the scrub,
there was a rending, crashing roar of stricken trees.
The wind leaped from the scrub’s edge and the frail
bark but left its moorings. A hundred yards fur-
ther on it met a giant granite boulder against which
it was held by the force of the screaming wind.
Then came the deluge of rain. Frightened and
breathless the hatter scrambled out and made his
way across the flattened grass until he found the
charred mouth of a very large hollow log. Putting
his head and shoulders into the opening the old
man wormed his way until the narrowing tunnel
told him he could go no further. A few feet in-
ward two spots of light glittered; it was the
gohanna, looking towards the human as still and
silent- as ■ the Sphinx. “I’ve come to camp with
you fer th’ night, Bluey!” shouted the old man.
“Th’ old humpy’s blown ter Hell an’ I reckon
there’s no more uv the world left.”
“Nor’-West”: Please whisper to “Simon
II.” (B. 21/11/’18) that there is only one species
of cod inhabiting the western rivers of NY S. Wales,
and that is the so-called Murray cod, which is
•really a perch. The “rock cod” is a bushman’s
myth. The short thick fish caught by “Simon” was
evidently a ma-fish. I have fished in the Severn, the
Gwydir, Mole, Sovereign (or Dumaresq), Namoi and
Condamine, and the cod in all these rivers are iden-
tical except for trifling differences in' conformation
and markings, due to environment. Cod of any
size usually go in pairs. Two fish I caught one
night, weighing over 10 pounds each, had; divided a
rabbit between them. Of a pair one is always
short and thick, and in the spawning season will
be found full of roe. The longer, handsome, well-
proportioned fish is dad. Color and markings are not
of importance, as the cod, like the golden perch and
bream, is able to take on color to suit the state of
the water. When the rivers are low the cod is very
dark and distinctly spotted with dark sea-green,
being difficult to see from above. The camouflage is
altered when a fresh occurs and the fish takes on a
yellowish color. The “dew-fish” referred to is evi-
dently the fresh-water cat-fish, commonly called by
western anglers “jew-fish.” The “yellow-belly” is
the golden perch, the finest edible fresh-water fish
that swims. It is difficult to account for the dis-
parity of the different species of fish in the Severn
and Macintyre. So far as I know perch or cat-fish
are not to be caught in the Severn, although it is
alleged that a few of the latter can be caught at
Strathbogies, dear Emmaville.
* * * *
“D.F.”: Tasmanian black brother, now
extinct, seems to have had a fancy for dying on re-
mote and almost inaccessible islands. The Hobart
Museum has a skull, apparently very old, from
Maatsuyker Inland, a heap of rocks and scrub off
the stormy south coast of the Speck. This confirms
the opinion of Flinders and Bass that it was visited
by natives, who had to cross three miles of wild
sea to reach it. Tasman Island, off Cape Pillar,
consists mainly of cliffs and rises to nearly 1000
feet; yet it also has yielded a skeleton. What Binghi
wanted and how he got there it is hard to guess.

“Snell”: “After all, cows is very human,”
said Old wisely; “very human cows is. There’s
nothin’ in the way of ridic-lousness a human woman
has got what a cow hasn’t got more or less. Cows
is. all sorts, same ez women. There’s vain cows, ’n’
haughty cows, ’u’ plain, homely cows, excitable
cows what’s alwuz a-friskin’ their heels all over the
lan’scape, ’n’ sober, self-possessed cows that you
couldn’t shake up with a ’underd-ton gun. Cushin’s
cow Selina was p’raps the most conceited cow—for
a cow—what I ever met. She wasn’t bad lookin’ ez
cows go, bein’ a strawb’ry blonde, with neat horns,
’n' rather fine eyes, but the way that cow fancied
herself wasn’t outdone fer wicked vanity be any
young lady in the church. She was alwiz lickin’
herself, ’n’ polishin’ up her complexion over some
pool in Pill Creek, ’n’ a-twistin’ of herself most
awkward to get a better view of her figger, ’n’ thin
she’d sort of lead herself round ’n’ up ’n’ down afore
the other cows, fillin’ ’em with envy, she thought.
She was skittish, too, Cushin’s Selina was. The way
that cow ’ud kick up her heels ’n’ sorter prance
round made you think of a giddy old maid bein’
tickled surreptitious. Well, one mornin’ Cushin’s
Selina went missin’. She’d once won first prize fer
her size ’n’ kind et the Stawell Show, which maybe
accounts fer the conceit ’n’ pride of her, seein’ she
was no mortal good for enythin’ ever after, but
Cushin’ set store by . her, ’n’ went huntin’ her all
up ’n’ down the countryside; but nobody hadn’t
seen, her, so Cushin put up a notice offerin’ a reward
of two quid for Selina, ’n’ next day one of the
young Colters comes to pushin, ’n’ claims to have
found Selina. He takes Cushin over to the yard
of the old, deserted house Andrews used to live in,
’n' there in the tumble-down shed was Selina more’n
arf dead from hunger ’n’ thirst, but perfectly ’appy.
It seems that Andrews had left a hunk of ole broken
mirror in that outhouse, leaned agin the wall, ’n’
Selina in her travels had found it ’n’ there she was,
gazin’ at herself in the glass, dyin’ of starvation, but
perfectly satisfied. To get her ’ome Cushin had to
carry the mirror in front of her, ’n’ to keep her
’ome he was compelled to fix it on a stump in his
’cme paddock, V there Selina would stand by the
hour, lickin’, polishin’, ’n’ tittivatin’ herself, V
admirin’ of her fine eyes. You know the old savin’,
‘Pride goeth afore a bust up’ ? One evenin’ Selina was
.found lyin’ in front of her mirror dead ’n’ stone cold.
It was seen at once that some kids had got to her
with a pot of brandin’ mixture, ’n’ had messed her
all over with it. The vet. said it was the first time
in all his experience he’d known a cow to die of a
bioken heart.”

“Nitram”: Haven’t noticed any Abo.-scribe
mention how fish are submarined in the Macintyre
and Severn rivers on the Bananaland —N. S. Wales
border. Procure as many sealed empty syrup-tins as
possible, attach a short length of line and hook
by a couple of half hitches around the middle, bait
and throw into a waterhole where it will float with
current or breeze. When the fish bite they are
doomed to be hooked, as the empty tins are surer
than the most expert angler and when caught they
always make for the side of the river where it is
an easy matter to capture your submarine with fish
attached. Have caught 101 b. cod with small 21b. sub.
Have also left submarines in water-hole all night
apd had as many as nine fish with 10 tins. Anyone
going fishing up in those parts without a supply of
convenient tins is looked on as being of weak intel-
lect.

“Melos”: We've hit it right sometimes
with our nird aliases. “Farmer’s friend” fits the
straw-necked ibis; and “flute-bird” is good for the
black-backed magpie. So are “coachwhip” (golden-
breasted whistler), “squeaker” (noisy mina), “post-
sitter” (brown lly-catnher), “willie wagtail” (restless
flycatcher), “spinethil” (Australian bee-eater), “cock-
tail” (superb warbler), “alarm-bird” (spurwinged
plover), and “cobbler’s awl” (spinebill). “Curra-
wang” (picd-bell magpie) expresses as nearly as
possible the bird’s call. “Woodpecker” (tree-creeper)
is only technically incorrect. We have no true
woodpecker. Incidentally this and the vulture are
th<= only widely distributed families unrepresented in
Australia.

“D.F.”: The word “lubra,” or “luherer,”
was used by the Tasmanian aborigines, if the evidence
of G. A. Robinson, the “black protector,” is correct.
In his diary for May 18, 1829, he describes a native
on Bruni Island as using the word “luberer” for his
wife. On the other hand, if Dove, who lived with the
natives on Flinders Island at a later date, is right,
“lubra” is a borrowed word, and had displaced the
original term “lurga” ini the language of the eastern
and southern tribes. Milligan describes these tribes
as using quite different, words for woman—lowalla,
lowanna and nienate. i

“HEARNE’S”
person, and Aga Khan takes more than
pains; he almost takes agony. He and ins
kind are eminently respectable, like the men
who wrote the New Testament, and the re-
semblance becomes closer the more it is
studied. The New Testament men were
accustomed, like the Indian, to foreign domi-
nation. They had the cosmopolitan outlook
of the cultured Indian. Like him they
walked barefoot to avoid waking Caesar. In
a. time of bitter oppression (which somewhat
spoils the resemblance because the Indian
isn’t oppressed) they managed to write a
book without speaking unkindly of one
Roman, not even of Pilate, the crucifier;
though they cast or hinted certain reflections
against the Pharisees who were hard Coven-
anting patriots; and the Ilerods, w’ho were
Idumeans; and Barabbas, who was, local
raw material; and Judas, Ananias, Sapphira
and similar obscure trash. Aga Khan has
travelled widely and gathered up a mass of
interesting and valuable information. Armed
with this ammunition, he gives the impres-
sion that he would like to shoot, but is too
polite. Very courteously he suggests that
there are many ways in which the manage-
ment of India could be improved, and he
haves an impression that his ideas are good.
He indicates tilings that should be done, but
he uses not one expression that a curate
could object to about those who leave them
undone. 11.11. the Aga Khan should have
known Alfred Deakin in that polite gentle-
man’s best days, though he might have re-
garded the Affable one as too violent. (Pub-
lished by Philip Lee Warner, London.)
Keeling Letters
This is a tome of middle-sized beer, 330
pages strong. It is produced by a small
corporation of admirers, including his family
and H. G. Wells at his worst. The reason
why it isn’t a tome of small beer is that the
writer of the letters had enough inherited
money to travel —not in the large Australian
or United States way, but at least as far as
Italy—and beer grows bigger by removal.
The letters are fairly good as letters, but
thousands of ill-paid newspaper men write
as good stuff every day. Keeling was a
socialist and reformer of the condescending
and dress-suit variety. Also his death proves
conclusively that prigs are not bullet proof.
Pictorially he is shown in three attitudes:
(1) as a barefaced youth of 20 with an up-
ward look in his eyes, his chin resting on
his hand and his forefinger pointed at his
ear; (2) as a stagey and bearded person of
22 with dark, disordered hair, a cafefully-
cultivated yearn in his eyes, and an aspect
of suppressed verse; (3) as the most beauti-
fully barbered and shampooed soldier imagin-
able. His story reveals that his sense of
fairness and justice and his large cosmopoli-
tan outlook and his superiority to the local
clamor of the herd made him hope the Huns
would carry everything before them —on
land anyhow. Up to the beginning of the
war at all events he regarded the French as
a menace to Europe, a disturbing element
and a race of poor fighting capacity. He
considered Russia the sole cause of the war;
regarded the execution of Nurse Cavell,
despite the method of her trial, as a justifi-
able act; and fell into profane language at
the infamy of any trade boycott against
Germany. Also he grew pale with rage at
the people who dismissed their Hun ser-
vants in' England. Wells, in his preface,
insists on Keeling’s originality. So do two
of his schoolmasters, one of whom relates a
pointless anecdote about how he got out of
a window, and another about how he refused
to be grateful to the nurses who attended
him in an illness, saying with the philosophy
of Dickens’s Smallweed family: “Well,
they’re paid for it.” In reality he seems a
father stereotyped kind of local bounder, and
he deserves a little notice because he was so
true to pattern. He was the perfect sample,
like the gentleman in the barber’s window or
the milliner’s lady figure with the new corset
on. (Geo. Allen and Unwin, London.)
A Satchel of Books
Innocent Amusements, by Barry Pain (T. Werner
Laurie). Light as the themes are, there is always
a hint of humor, a twist in the tail or a bit of
shrewd observation to make these stories worth
while.
Destruction, by Elinor Glyn (Duckworth and Co.).
This war booklet is written in short, explosive barks
and paints slightly but forcefully a series of pic-
tures in keeping with the title.
Robin of the Round House, by Isabel Maud
Peacocke (Ward, Lock!. A Maoriiand yarn,
pleasantly told, of two business girls who, on com-
ing into a legacy, set up as farmers. The Round
House is a place built by an ancient mariner in the
style of a ship. Robin strolls into the yarn from
an orphanage and the garden blossoms. Better
and more readable work than most fiction of its
class.
The Keys of Heaven, by Marjorie Weatherly (Aus-
tralian Authors’ Agency, Melbourne). The daughter
of an earl is sold as a slave in the open markets of
New Orleans. There is a preface to explain that
such things could occur, and a couple of hundred
pages to tell how after it happened everything came
light. It doesn’t seem to matter much after this
lapse of time; but the yarn goes along with some
show of probability.
’Twixt Eagle and Dove, by E. V. Lucas (Methuen).
Short essays which for the most part run thin.
There is no depth of thought, little skill in construc-
tion and a total lack of brilliancy; but the book
is very harmless and eminently suited for the Sun-
day reading of not-too-devout maiden aunts.
At Petunia, sketches by S. Elizabeth Jackson (G.
Hassell and Son, Adelaide). Excellently written
little stories of life in an Australian townlet. The
small work is full of quiet humor and good observa-
tion. Nothing much happens; but the 80 pages
glide pleasantly along and leave the impression that
the authoress is capable of much bigger things.
Personalities, by Paul Selver (George Allen and
Unwin). A book of verse, mostly with a topical
flavor. Much of the work is excellently done ; and
even where the movement is rather heavy there are
flashes that redeem it. Sample (from “Ten Epi-
grams”) : <
This ancient saw on which I dwell
Must make our artists vain,
For they conceal their art so well
It can’t be found again.
Many pages are about as good as that.
Business, Robbery, etc.
Of the common things of every-minute use
none have been so hard to get in England as
matches. “No matches” has been a sign up
on half the tobacconists’ windows of London ;
and when you have seen an expectant queue
of men you have known that the shop-
keeper has achieved a supply somehow. Two
men out of three and nine Yankee soldiers
out of 10 have carried flint-spark lighters
with them; but the best don’t satisfy the
pipe-smoker and the worst produce only
curses. Nobody worth mentioning has struck
a match without looking round first to see
whether he couldn’t borrow a light; and
perfect strangers have pushed their faces
into yours and lit their fags at your stump.

“F.C.”: Something has gone wrong with
the blessed law of supply and demand again;
at least, with the retail part of it, so to,
speak. I notice that Minister Oman has
been advising Vic. meat-producers—sheep-
growers—to hold their jumbucks back for a
few weeks, as at the last few markets in
December they have been overdoing the
supply and getting at least Bs. per head
from the exporter less than he could well
afford to pay under the Imperial meat con-
tract. That is “supply and demand” in
correct form, all right; but I also notice
that on December 14 the meat price-fixing
regulation was cancelled or abolished, where-
upon the local retail prices of mutton, which
were already higher than the Imperial con-
tract rates, unaccountably jumped up Id.
and id. per lb., notwithstanding that mut-
tons were cheaper than they had been these
last two years. I am a methodical, orderly
individual, and there is too much unaccount-
ableness about the meat business to suit me.

“R.S.”: The nerve of the professional
debt-collecting firms is colossal. A friend
of mine got a request for payment of the
balance of an account within seven days.
The amount was sent by P. 0.0. to the
creditor (not the debt-collecting robber). A
day or two later there came a note from the
latter saying that. 15s. 6d. expenses had been
incurred, on receipt of which the summons
would be withdrawn. My friend smiled, but
the summons was issued all right 14 days
after the account had been paid. The mad-
dened recipient handed the documents to a
solicitor, and the client of the dun had to
part out more than his original full account
was worth.

“Bill D.’s” tale of sin
Bob Downs had been working on Gaffer’s Hill
for a year when one evening after he had come off
day shift he announced his intention of making a
move, and went round the camps to bid the fellows
good-bye. Now, on Gaffer’s Hill we paid once a
month, and as the locality wasn’t the safest in the
world, being nine miles from anywhere, we never
kept money to pay odd amounts between pay-days.
If a man wanted to leave suddenly he gave some
friend on the mine an order to collect his wages,
in exchange for which the friend gave him the cash
in advance. Bob left on a Wednesday night, two
days before pay-day, with 24 shifts to his credit.
The pay-clerk took it as a matter of course, there-
fore, when the first man who lined up for his pay—
it happened to be “Uncle” Wilson —presented a piece
of piper, which read, “Please pay U. Wilson amount
due to me 24 shifts at 14s. = £l6 16s. R. Downs.”
But when lanky, loose-kneed Mulga Ralph, who was
the second man to line up, presented a replica, he
said shortly, “Here, wot the ’ell yer givin’ us! I
jest paid that to Uncle Wilson.”
Ralph shoved the paper into his pocket and strode
out, murder in his heart. “My Gawd,” he was say-
ing, “if ever I git my hands on the blarsted swine”
—when he noticed that little Micky, the next man,
was carrying a piece of paper in his hand as he
entered. Ralph waited, and presently out came
Micky, waving the paper, his face crimson with
rage, wild Australian words bubbling from his lips.
By this time the main body of men were rolling up
to the pay-office, and the comedy commenced in
earnest. As each one came out, his face black with
hate, or pale at the thought of £l6 16s. worth of
beer lost, he was cheered till the faint echoes crept
back from the low cliffs on the other side of the
salt lake. There were a few exceptions, men who had
been “short,” or who shared a camp with someone
else, in which latter case Bob could only “touch”
one of the partners; but nearly every man on the
show had suffered.
When the last man was paid, and the last cheer
had died away, a puff of dust showed out on the salt
plain, and in a few moments two carts were visible.
As they came closer they proved to be the two store-
keepers from the township. “Funny,” said one; “they
don’t usually come out till the Saturday.” As they
pulled up in front of the office Ralph said to the first,
“Hello, Mr. Gunn, day earlier than usual this time!”
“Yes,” was the reply. “Bob Downs wanted to get
away to Perth in a hurry, so I took an order on his
tvages for the bill, and gave him the change.”
The other storekeeper’s face was a picture. He
scented something of the situation, sprang from his
cart in such a hurry that he landed in a heap, pulled
himself together, and bolted for the office door, closely
pursued by his rival Gunn and the profane, riotoul
laughter of Gaffer’s Creek.
* * *
A new industry is interesting Melbourne.
It consists of converting stringy-bark in its
natural state into a strong and durable floor-
covering. The idea was originated by Mrs.
Frank Walker, of Ringwood, who began by
plaiting the shredded outside bark and
fashioning these plaits into decorative baskets.
Realising that stringy-bark possesses an un-
common amount of durability and strength,
Mrs. Walker conceived the notion of making
the plaited bark into floor-mats. From these
small beginnings apparently there is a great
future for a material which up to now has
been regarded' as a waste product. Big quan-
tities of raw material are available, and all
that is wanted is suitable machinery. At
preseht the industry is a handicraft which
though effective is costly. Experts think
that with efficient machinery floor-coverings
better than Japanese can be turned out
at a small cost. The industry would employ
hundreds of returned soldiers. If local pro-
duction could not compete with the Jap in
price perhaps someone might think of a Pro-
tective tariff.
* * * *
“Gleniris” ; For years the Victorian bush-
man’s building rule in mountain-asli dis-
tricts has been: “Ash above-ground and red-
gum below.” Red-gum is alleged by Melb.
University tests to be five times as valuable
as the more commonly used jarrah; and
mountain-ash is a proved and sterling hard-
wood, suitable for many industrial purposes.
Owing to the expensiveness of imported
materials, ash is now being used as a substi-
tute for pine in case-making and for oak in
casks, and it has also been “discovered” as
a sound roofing. Plenty of country dwell-
ings have of late years been made almost
entirely of ash, roofs included. The most
up-to-date roofing of the kind consists of Gin.
boards, laid lengthwise along the roofs and
“checked” underneath to take the top edge
of the board below. For fire-proofing the
standard application for woods is silicate of
soda, so it would probably be the correct
thing for ash, too. Early application for a
fire-insurance policy is also advisable.
* * * *
“Spec”: Sad news has come to Adelaide
from one of the leading distillers in Scot-
land regarding the whisky market., Aus-
tralia appears to take a wrong view of the
general trade position as affecting Scotch
and Irish whisky, and concludes that, to-
gether with most other commodities, after
the war the spirit will gradually fall in
price; but this is not so. The principal fac-
tor against a decrease in price is the three
years' age-limit imposed by law in the
United Kingdom, in May, 1915. At that
date there had been practically no advance
in spirits, but after the age-limit was im-
posed certain kinds of Scotch and Irish
whisky increased in price by 200 per cent.
Added to the age-limit gradually came all
the other restrictions, of which, of course,
the greatest was stoppage of distillation. The
age-limit, however, is the main trouble.
There is not the slightest cliancfi o'f the
British Government abolishing that. When
distilling is recommenced there will not be a
large production on account of the high cost
of material. The opinions of the largest dis-
tillers in Scotland and Ireland are that
Scotch whisky will not decrease in cost for
a long time ahead, and that normal condi-
tions cannot be restored for at least four
years from the date they commence to distil.
It would be practically impossible to-day to
buy 500 quarter-casks of Scotch whisky in
Scotland even if 30s. a gallon were offered in
gold.

* * * *
“G.”: Prices of all foodstuffs have rushed
skywards this last few years, particularly
finned goods, which Australians still think
it necessary to import. Tinned fish have
increased from 100 to 200 per cent, since the
war. Australia seems to be successful in
canning fruits, rabbits, etc. (the finest bun-
nies in the world, Billjim says), so why not
fish? Many years ago a canning factory was
started at Kingscote, Kangaroo Island, and
the salmon treated there was considered bet-
ter quality than the imported; but, owing
to the cheapness of imported fish at that
time, the Australian industry had to close
down. During November and December
each year the salmon pass along the west
coast of Kangaroo Island in large shoals,
some of which are estimated to contain 20
tons. The local fishermen then make catches
of from 40 to 60 baskets, the fish being sold
as low as 2d. a lb. Salmon not being a good
keeper, and facilities not being available to
market it promptly, large quantities are lost
each year. With modern preserving methods
these fish could be distributed at a price
which would be a godsend to the public.
Snook are also caught in large quantities,
only a small percentage of which reaches the
market for consumption. If the Australian
is too shiftless to catch and can the fish that
swim past his front door he deserves to pay
high for his imports.
“WATCHES REPAIRED.”
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
Folks ne’er heed the sands are failin’
Through the waisted glass of Time;
All they know is dinner’s callin’
When the mid-hour’s due to chime.
But in Dad’s repairing crib
Clocks that tick an’ clocks that jib
Hang with faces all asmile
At their owners’ daily guile 1
All atickin’ an’ atockin’
Through the hours long they go;
Seemingly they’re often mockin’
At our daily drips of woe!
Pendulums aglint are swingin',
Gongs hn’ loud alarms are ringin’,
Marking moments gay an’ grave
For the coward an’ the brave!
Here’s a face illuminated
Showin’ how the mir fly;
Others there with dials I
Tell how months crav. . lowly by.
Tickin’ ! Tickin’! on they go .
Sometimes fast, an’ sometimes slow,
Moments drip unceasingly
To the tub—Eternity 1
Here’s a watch —with pride I tell it 1 —
That “went bung” at Lonesome Pine;
O’er its wearer’s heart a pellet
Struck him from the Turkish line,
But the metal took the knock,
Nothin’ happened but the shock.
Now it starts again to tick
Like its owner —in good nick 1
There’s a golden wristlet beauty.
Just a little gift he gave,
When he heard the call of duty
Ere he fought and found a grave.
And I know a sad-eyed lass
Sorrow might have deigned to pass;
Now his watch will count her tears
Through the long and hallowed years!
All day long the ticks are countin’
In a warning sort of way,
And Time’s tide is surely mountin’
To the count of Judgment Day.
Notwithstanding poet’s paeans
Moments fly to build the aeons,
An’ Dad’s “tickers” keep their trusi
Tickin’ on because they must.
VICTORIA.
RITA SUNYASEE.

ARE YOU A REXONA GIRL?
MISS M ON A FERGUSON (from a Monte Luke, photo).
Dear Sirs,—
Having- been a constant user of Rexona Soap for
some years, I think you may be interested in hearing
my opinion of it—which is, that it stands alone as a
skin and hair beautifler. AH other soaps that I have
used have a tendency to enlarge the pores of the com-
plexion. liexona Soap seems to make the skin closer,
finer and smoother every day it is used.
With regard to Rexona “anointment” (as a little child
I know calls it), I must tell you that I use it in winter
to prevent the chapping that would otherwise ensue
from bitter winds; and after surfing in summer I always
anoint (the word is useful, isn’t it?) my face, neck,
arms and hands with it, first rubbing a little of it well
in, and then quickly wiping the remainder away with
a clean linen towel. It gives me pleasure to write. I
hope it will give you pleasure to hear.
Yours truly, (Sgd.) 3IONA FERGUSON.

GREGORY NORTH RABBIT BOARD.
Applications for Superintendent of
Works.
APPLICATIONS are invited until Tues-
day, 17th March, 1919, for the posi-
tion of Superintendent of Works.
Applicants to state age and general experi-
ence in the construction and maintenance of
rabbit-proof fencing and rabbit destruction.
Salary four hundred pounds (£400) per
annum. Camel outfit provided in dry coun-
try, applicant to find necessary horses and
plant for the other portions of the district.
Applications, together with copies of testi-
monials, to be addressed to the Chairman,
Gregory North Rabbit Board, Boulia,
Queensland, and endorsed “Application for
Superintendent of Works.”
For any other particulars apply to the
Clerk of the Gregory North Rabbit Board,
Boulia, Queensland.
E. S. SCHOLEFIELD,
Chairman.
Boulia, 3rd December, 1918.

Specimen Section of a
“COLO” Tyre,
Eight Points of the “COLO "Tyre
(1) TEE AD.—Corrugated, presenting small sur-
face to the road, thereby saving friction.
(2) TEE AD.—A great, thickness of rubber, which
is practically cut- and chip-proof.
(3) BEEAKEE STEIP, which, being made of
loosely-woven, heavy fabric, binds the tread
to the carcase of the tyre.
(4) PUEE EUBBEE.—Pure Eubber is placed
under Breaker Strip.
(o) CAECASE. Composed of five plies of heavy,
long-fibre canvas, heavily impregnated with
rubber.
(6) FULL AIR SPACE.
(7) SIDE WALLS. —Realising the severe work
tyres are called upon to do in the country,
we have placed a great thickness of rubber on
the side walls, preventing water from entering,
as often happens in other makes of tyres after
a few months’ service. Other makes have
very little thickness of rubber at this point.
(8) BEADS are heavily reinforced and skin-
coated with rubber, to prevent rust.
MARCUS CLARK & CO. LTD.,

POVERTY
POINT
“Snell”: My aged parent recalls a season
of egg-throwing at the Charlie Napier
Theatre, Ballarat, in the old gold days. The
actor, one Will Smith, appears to have been
quite a bearable sort of comedian, but he
had got himself into ill favor, putting up a
fist fight on behalf of an official. Eggs,
brought from Ireland, packed in salt and saw-,
dust, were 4d. or (id. apiece at the time, but
or. the night after the fight there were plenty
for Smith gratis. My progenitor does not
recollect the play, but Act 1 of it was mostly
eggs. Coming on in Act 2, the victim
A SURPRISE.
THE ARTIST : “So you liked my little picture
ih the exhibition?”
THE MODEL : “I was never so astonished in
my life as when I saw your name on it.”
casually carried in a large brown bag, and
when eggs started coming his way again
Smith got back at the. enemy with the same
weapon. The actor man proved to be re-
markably deft: whereas the audience often
missed him, he never missed the audience.
Every egg Smith threw fetched someone, and
every time an egg was thrown at Smith,
Smith replied in kind. Those men whom
Smith hit, and who were not in the crusade
against him, being unable to get at Smith
directly, got to the egg-throwers in the audi-
torium who were indirectly responsible, and
in the melee that followed the poor player’s
enemies had very much the worse doing.
Smith solemnly brought on his brown bag
in Act 1 next night, but eggs were off. Smith
had triumphed. At the end of the season a
complimentary banquet was tendered to the
comedian. The menu was all eggs. Even
the brandy was served in egg-shells.
“O.K. ’: Frank Thornton was essentially
a Tubbs in his commercial outlook. No
actor-manager ever had a keener eye for
gross receipts and net profits It was said
of him in Australia that when he was run-
ning the show on his own, or on sharing
terms, he would count the heads in the d.c.
whilst playing his part. Then when the
night’s returns came along he would check
the d.c. figures and remark upon the number
of deadheads. If the doorkeeper had omitted
to enter one of the non-productive visitors
in his book Thornton would call him to
account. His spendthrift Dick Phenyll in
“Sweet Lavender” was good because it was
the dead ring of Edward Terry’s creation.
His own Private Secretary, Lord Chancel-
lor (“Iolantlie”) and John Wellington Wells
(“The Seorcerer”), not to mention Charley’s
Aunt, were persons of a distinctly niggardly
type. They looked like haggling over a
thrum.
Even before the armistice, there were two
Maoriland teams of entertainers in the
theatrical field. The Kiwis were showing
at the Alhambra in Paris, and the Pierrots
at the Victoria in London. Both companies
were advertised as “direct from the Front.”
Ibsen is to be the Taits’ third Repertory
programme at the Palace (Sydney). “John
Gabriel Borkman” is the play selected —a
gloomy thing with less comedy in it than an
addled egg.
“L.L.W.”: I was at the first performance
of Paderewski’s Polish symphony in London
eight years ago. The music described the
revolution of 1563 in his country. I haven’t
forgotten yet the clash and clamor of the
brass sarrusophones and the thunderings of
the tritruones, his own invention. We all
yelled for the composer and I expected to
.see a tall, pale, serious-looking man with a
wild shock of untamed hair. Instead, a
little man in evening-dress, with a mane
combed smoothly back like a neat wig,
popped in front of the orchestra an I began
bowing in all directions. By the way. many
years ago Pad.’s opera “Manru” was banned
from Berlin, just bn the eve of its produc-
tion, because the composer gave a large sub.
to the funds of a Polish committee at Posen
formed to prevent Poland from being Ger-
manised.
“Smike” : Comparing the two big
Melbourne pantos., the advantage
in scenic effectiveness is with the
Firm’s. In “Jack and Jill” Wil-
liam Itowell has certainly made
two happy successes of the Bagdad
and Spanish Ballet scenes, but his
performance hardly counterbal-
ances weaknesses elsewhere. Clint
in particular has not painted up
to his usual form, and the lighting
hardly mitigates the rawness of his
cloths, notably his principal effu-
sion, the Floral Ballet. In “Goody
Two Shoes” the conspiracy be-
tween more experienced artists and
limelight men is a constant beguile-
ment. Note the seductive pretti-
ness of the Kirchner Girl scene
and the delicate fancy of the Bub-
bles set. These pictures are the
work of Coleman. This veteran’s
interiors have that suavity of de-
sign rarely achieved except after
years of effort, and his outdoor
scenes, like the Village of Rlossom-
ville and the Fringe of the Forest,
possess the highest artistic attrac-
tion.
‘O. Blin”: Barry Lupino. who is most
of the agitation in “Jack and Jill” at Mel-
bourne King’s, has a great regard for
decayed theatrical relics. Any small object
that has been associated with some old-time
celebrity is a valuable passport to his favor,
and he will listen to its history with a big
babe’s eye for the most trivial details. At
the present time he is attached to an aged
dolman or cape which was a permanent
feature of the late Dan Leno’s panto outfit.
This antique guarded Leno from misfortune
for many years, and to Lupino it is almost
a second wife. He speaks of it like a poet
of his poem or a punter of a popular fancy.
“Yarra Ben” ; The Tait brethren like
being particularly busy showmen during the
next few months. In addition to their general
theatrical ventures they will be piloting Harry
Lander round Australia for a second time
and filling in the odd moments by showing
a big film they have just acquired in Eng-
land. This depicts the surrender of the
Hun battleships and other ironmongery, and
if that “movie” isn’t destined to add a Tew
kopecks to the Baits’ banking account I’ll
take to work for a living.
“BABES IN THE WOOD,” AT SYDNEY
GRAND OPERA HOUSE.
Horatio, the Hamfat (Dan Dunbar ), and
the Bad Baron (Horace Mann).
The idea of this new film (there is to be
about 3000 ft. of it) originated in Adelaide.
Liquor arguments are being prepared by the
organisations controlling the industry, and
the prohibitionists’ case (or barrel) is being
presented by the South Aus. Temperance
Alliance. The liquor trade intends to show
the industries which will be destroyed by
prohibition; the Alliance will attempt to
demonstrate how other industries will be
developed by the reform. “King Alcohol —
Shall He Live or Die?” is to be the title.
“Scuffler”: On New Year’s Day I saw
above the sleepy, smoky city an old familiar
gasbag blowing inland and wobbling lazily
towards the floor of Heaven, and I guessed
that an old friend was risking his neck once
more. True-—-next day’s paper had the news
that airman Taylor had gone up from Coogee
and landed safely in a suburban road. Tay-
lor must have parachuted about 300 miles in
the last 10 years, and, so far as I know, in
all his coastal ascents, his flying umbrella
has never once landed him far out in the
Pacific. Somehow, his smoke-bag always
seems to drift inland and snake down into
somebody’s back-yard, so that he has got quite
used to interrupting an afternoon tea-drink-
ing with, “Pardon, but may I get my balloon?
It has just fallen on top of your woodshed.”
“Valentine Day”: Some Dickens produc-
tions that I remember (for “O.K.’s” benefit).
Besides the Tyrone Power version of “The
Only Way,” an American serving of
Dickens’s “Tale of Two Cities” was offered
bv Katie Putnam at Sydney Gaiety in 1889,
and was followed by “The Old Curiosity
Shop.” J. L. Toole made the hit of his Aus-
tralasian tour as Caleb Plummer in “Dot,”
in which, however, he was overshadowed by
the Peerybingle of John Bellington and the
Tilly Sloeboy of Eliza Johnstone. Toole also
staged scenes from “Pickwick” here; and
played Sergeant Buzfuz. “0.K.” has also
overlooked dramatisations of “Bleak House.”
The first in my memory was presented by
Madame .Tanauschel —imported in the late
’7o’s _by Harwood, Stewart, Hennings and
Coppin to rival Madame Ristori, shown by W.
S. Lyster across Bourke-street at the Opera
House. As Lady Dedlock the German failed to
draw. Georgie Smithson, I think, got in first
with Jo, at Sydney Queen’s, early in the ’Bo’s.
Jennie Dee first showed us how the part
should be played in 1884, or thereabouts.
She revisited Australia with “The Crossing
Sweeper” as her strong suit during 1891-92
and 1899-1900.
nv" ®i'.”" .' Tooth for a Tooth,” at Melbourne
Tivoli, i s an ingenious- trifle, to me more
interesting than anything I have seen on the
Australian stage in a year. It opens with
an amorous couple in quaint guise in a futur-
ist setting under a swollen moon. The lovers
embrace and dance down stage in a mystic
atmosphere. Suddenly footsteps are heard.
The girl simulates fear. Enter a man in
black, his back to the audience. The girl
incites her lover to stab the stranger. As the
figure falls it turns, and we see that it has
no face —just a white fungoid blank
occupying the site. Again footsteps. The
lovers drag the dead thing to a seat at the
back of the stage, and propping it between
them pretend to hold it in conversation.
Enter a police-inspector. There is by-play
between the inspector and the girl. The in-
spector i s suspicious ; he commands the youth
to rise. The youth obeys, and the body left
to itself sways, then falls. The inspector
goes to the body. The youth in horror shoots
the girl, and the curtain falls on her con-
tinued screams, and rises almost instantly,
disclosing the interior of a dentist’s-room,
with the girl in the chair, yelling, fresh from
the gas, and the dentist holding up the ex-
tracted tooth. It is the smartest sketch the
revue craze has brought to Australia, and is
well done, although the Aubrey Beardsley-
esque atmosphere and fashion of the charac-
ters should be more strongly emphasised.
Couldn’t a few Australians do smart, quaint
things like this, and infuse some gumption
into the blatant revues?
SUPPORTS.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
The big old actor booms all morning;
We hear him like a minute-gun.
All interruptions blandly scorning,
He tolls his virtues one by one —
The praise he had from critics hoary;
The transcendental parts he “took”:
This climax to each wondrous story—
“ And I supported G. V. Brooke!”
“I’ve shone in comic play and tragic
For over half a century,
And men have spoken of the magic
Ot my fine personality.
By- mumming youngsters I am classed a
Back number, maybe; but, gadzook !
At any work I’m still their master,
And I supported G. V. Brooke!
“My Hamlet was the finest ever”
(He gives us samples from the book) ;
“M.v .Borneo distinctly clever,
And I supported G. V. Brooke!”
To-day exclaimed the hash-house mother:
“1 don’t know him, but I attest
That here at one time or another
I have supported all the rest!”
VICTORIA.
STARGAZER.
A MISCHANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A man, a maid, a box of chocs,
Three at the show, a play that shocks,
A kiss, a squeeze (at crowds love mocks)..,.
Lights up—he’d kissed her mother!
A taxi home, a short-lived ride,
H'is thoughts upon his future bride;
He helped her down and then fell wide....
Lights out —he’d met her brother!

The beauty specialists have to make the
exercises attractive to hold their customers.
The latest idea to keep Mrs. Fatneck inter-
ested is to stand her with her back to a
mirror and let her examine her southern
aspect minutely from all angles.
Mrs. Pankhurst has screamed in J. Bull’s
ear that the Women’s Party must stamp
out Bolshevism. History has done a record
sprint since the suffragettes were setting nre
to houses, churches and pillar-boxes; also
window-smashing, hunger-striking, etc. But
how would Mrs. Pank. deal with a con-
victed Bolshevik who persisted in hunger-
striking? No use offering him the franchise.
He has that already.
“■B” : He is not called Palestein in the direc-
tory, but that name is as good as any
other. All the most affluent members of
our suburban Jerusalem were present at his
daughter’s wedding breakfast, and Palestein
proposed the toast of the happy pair. I
hope,” he concluded, “that their future vill
be as pright as these tiamond studs” —and
a fat, beringed hand caressed in turn the
three jewels that blazed upon his rampart
of boiled shirt.
General Sir Francis Lloyd, R.S.V.P.,
P.T.0., etc., who lately relinquished his
command of the British forces on the Lon-
don front, has probably made more speeches
than any other English officer living. His
beautifully-groomed moustache and hair and
his glittering uniform have adorned the
opening ceremonies of hundreds of soldiers
clubs, military charities, matindes, etc.,
where he has swayed in orgies of elocu-
tionary flapdqodle. it was he who issued
the order that wounded men, as well as all
other soldiers on leave in London, were to
rise to attention and swing their arms to all
officers —even if seated in a theatre, settled
in a restaurant or jambed in a public con-
veyance. This brain-wave occurred at a
time when things were looking blue, and the
lowah ordahs were showing signs of war-
weariness. Lloyd George, who was working
under a tremendous strain, was pleased.
Uncle Sam, having unearthed a little list
compiled by Bernstorff, late Hun ambassa-
dor in U.S., of Americans who could be
relied upon to be “useful to Germany,” Uncle
is now calling upon sundry citizens to ex-
plain the thusness thereof, and the air is
thunderous. Among the most noisy of the dis-
claimants is David Starr Jordan. David’s
case will call for laborious protestation, if
his American career is consistent with his
brief but Vesuvian upheaval in Australia.
Jordan came here as a. peace spouter,
preaching the utter folly of armaments, and
the preposterousness of those who thought
war possible in these enlightened times.
According to Jordan, what was folly in all
other nations was sheer maniacal high-step-
ping in Australia, cut off as it was from all
participation in any possible war. If
Australia had listened to D. S. Jordan, the
Commonwealth would have stripped itself of
offensive and defensive machinery at a time
most satisfactory to the abounding Hun.
Jordan explains to Uncle Sam that his only
dealings with Bernstorff took the form of
intercession on behalf of an Australian lady
who wanted some favor or another at the
hands of Germany. But Jordan does not
explain how it came about that out of 100
million Americans one Australian lad:
should have picked on him.
“P.X.” from the other end of the earth
There is nothing else that the world seems quite
so short of as sugar. In England and practically
every other country under arms it is forbidden to
put the old familiar sugar-bowl upon a public dining-
table. John Bull has rationed his sugar supply;
every citizen may buy half a pound per week and
no more. First, he registers in his district;
then every week he draws his book of coupons at
the registry, and he must hand over his coupon
before a tradesman may supply him —which rule
applies to meat and a few other things besides sugar.
You carry your own sugar to the public dining-
table ; incidentally, this has produced another article
of common use—the pocket sugar-case, often of
silver, about the size of an old-fashioned watch. If
you haven’t your own supply of sugar with you, you
may be able to get a grain of saccharin —a sweet
coal-tar product without any food value. You might
spend months in France without seeing a grain of
either sugar or saccharin, though you can generally
get a liquid saccharin, which is horribly unsatisfac-
tory. But the armies in France get a fair quantity
of sugar ; at any rate, they get all there is. Italy
is worse off than France; even saccharin can rarely
be got there. The U.S.A. sugar-bowl has been put
away in the cupboard, too; but a teaspoonful may
be served at a meal to every customer in a public
rating-house; and that is the rule, too, in Canada.
But Canada isn’t so strict about sugar as it is about
spirits. Save Montreal and a small slice of Quebec,
the whole Dominion is bone-dry; and there are
plenty who think it will never be moist again.
“H. 0.” : Nawman Bayles, an awf’ly decent
sort of chap, is the most persistent intel-
lectual shirker in politics. To obtain or
supply definite information on any subject is
too much of a beastly bally bore for Naw-
man, whose speeches are as vague as the
policy of the Government he supports. To-
ward the close of the session State Labor-
leader Prendergast made a speech in the Yic.
Leg. Ass. on prison reform, full of facts and
figures regarding the prisons he has visited
here and abroad, which drew’ congratulations
from both sides of the House and moved
Nawman to emulation. Nawman men-
tioned that he too had devoted some time to
investigating the subject. He had visited a
prison in Maoriland, but could not recall its
name. The prisoners were employed on
forestry work and were paid, but he could
not remember how much. He was so inter-
ested in the system that he had made it his
business to call on the Minister in charge.
The Minister’s name had slipped Nawman’s
memory, but he was “a very nice man,” and
had given Nawman a quantity of literature.
Nawman had kept that literature for years,
and had intended bringing it down to the
House that day, but had not been able to
lay his hands on it. However, he was able
to assure hon. members with absolute cer-
tainty that it was “very interesting.” Hav-
ing thus delivered himself he sat down, with
the air of a man w T ho had done his duty at
the cost of personal discomfort and mental
strain.

When a man has a good wife, a good bank account, and a
he may reckon himself “very well off.”
While everyone may not secure the
others, no one need deny himself the
pleasurable possession of the G.B.D.- —
