From an Australian oil-well driller, at
present/in the U.S.A.: —
Recent developments in this country should give
the Commonwealth additional encouragement to go
ahead in the search for oil. Until a year or so ago
a co. was considered extremely venturesome if it
expended much capital in “wild-catting ’ territory
that did not on the surface give some indication of
the presence of oil below, in the form of
a gas or oil seepage. But of late, discoveries of
oil in unsuspected places have opened up vast possi-
bilities in territory that has previously been passed
by without a thought. It had been considered that
the Los Angeles district was on the has-been list so
far as new development was concerned, with the old
Le Brea, Whittier, Fullarton, Brea CanSn fields all
well defined and settled down into “power”-pumping
propositions. But the discovery of oil at Monte-
bello, a hitherto unsuspected anticline between Los
Angeles and Whittier, by the Standard Oil Co. some
18 months ago, has focussed attention on many a
hill in the district that had given no surface
betrayal of possible hidden treasures below. And
there are many “wild cats” now being put down in
these parts that are anxiously watched by oil men,
and it is not unlikely that Los Angeles will see a
repetition of the oil-boom days.
But what portends to be the most extensive oil-
field the world has ever seen is in Ranger, Texas,
which I visited last month. This field has not been
defined yet, but it has already been proved that the
oil extends over an area of, roughly, 120 by 65
miles, and not a seepage of oil or gas anywhere. The
bore that struck oil was a test hole being put dovyn
to look for coal, as there was a coal-seam being
worked about nine miles away ; but instead of coal
they got a 3000-barrel well of 43° gravity oil.
To-day there are over 200 rigs at work in Ranger,
development being curtailed by shortage of men,
water and storage. A pipe line to the coast, 200-odd
miles away, is now being laid. I motored 54 miles
south-west from Ranger, and was not out of sight
of a drilling-well all the way. From a sleepy
village of about 400 souls to a hustling town of
7000 is the record of Ranger, and all since April
last. Among many other fields I visited the famous
Eldorado Field, 30 miles from Wichita. Here the
Gypsy Co.’s Shumway Lease (100 acres) has pro-
duced $14,000,000 worth of oil in the last 14 months,
and four years ago a well put down in the centre of
this lease owing to it being badly drilled was pro-
nounced dry! A further try was not made until
over two years later.
*•* * *
John Craze, running the Round Hill silver-
lead mine, near Sheffield (Tas.), isn’t
keeping back news from shareholders, yet
all he has to say rather confirms the idea
that the Round Ilill has not got such a
solid formation as that on which North
Farrell has done its sprints. Till lately
Craze wailed over shortage of workers, as
well as the cutting out of the old shoots
met with in No. 1 tunnel. These gave
profits, but the cash had to be put back
into the mine, and things looked blue till
a north-west crosscut oft the bottom tunnel
got on to a shoot with decent walls. Early-
birds went nap on shares, which weary
holders were ready to supply under the belief
that the formation was all poor stuff. But
the last little lot of concentrates ran 4dwt.
gold, 43 p.c. lead and 90oz. silver per ton,
and costs were low. About 100 feet has
been proved north and south on the shoot,
and as the width of the formation is 18 feet
the outlook would be cheerful if already
the presence of a slab of mullock had not
shown that Round Hill is not yet out of
the shreds and patches list. Most of the
rich stuff comes from a narrow vein in the
shoot, and the point to be settled is whether
the north-west crosscut off the No. 2 tunnel
higher up the hill will prove the same shoot
of high-grade material.
The property which includes the old
Phoenix copper-mine and the Mabel gold-
mine at Wiseman’s Creek (N.S.W.) has
been idle for many months, although a
quantity of furnace products, reverb,
bottoms, etc., were sent away from the
Phoenix early last year. Now there is a
chance of a Tasmanian crowd buying the
property. The previous history of the
Phoenix has not been particularly en-
couraging, zinc and lead in the ore having
made reverb, work unprofitable. The Mabel,
worked for a time by a Sydney co. which
vainly endeavored to cyanide the ore for
its gold contents, was originally opened to
supply gossan for fluxing purposes. Although
it probably contained about sdwt. gold, its
copper and sulphuric contents precluded
successful cyaniding, more especially as the
all-solution method was tried. Crushing
with water, and then cyaniding, might have
been more successful; but apart from the
slimy character of the gossan when crushed,
a percentage of baryta in the ore caused
mechanical trouble. At the same time there
are many propositions that carry less
values than the Phoenix and the Mabel, and
a deep shaft below the latter might unearth
a good copper lode.
North Wiseman’s, Wiseman’s Creek
(N.S.W.), has been pegged out by a Bath-
urst syndicate, though whether it is for the
sake of the old dumps or for the mine itself
is not yet known. Probably the dumps will
be the first consideration. This mine has
in the past yielded good results; during one
period of three years 3400 tons of 10.5 p.c.
ore were sent to Lithgow. Many attempts
have been made to obtain permission to
again prospect the property, but the fact
that it was a C.P. precluded any satisfac-
tory terms. Even the dumps, which should
contain some payable ore, have remained
untouched for years. Once the scene of
a good little goldfield, Wiseman’s Creek has
almost disappeared. Its greatest hope now
lies in the chance that North Wiseman’s,
thrown open by the just-amended Mining
Act, may come into prominence as a copper
producer.
* * * *
The world’s most southerly tinfield, Cox’s
Bight (Tas.), has had a wee revival lately.
For a long time there was only one miner on
it, and he had as lonely a job as could be
found in Australia; but when tin soared
prospectors began to nose about and found
that the beach sand carried tin. as high as
3 p.c. in places. There is talk of a dredge.

INCREASED STRENGTH FROM
PHOSPHATE.
SPECIALIST TELLS HOW TO
DOUBLE STRENGTH AND
ENDURANCE.
A great authority on the subject of health,
strength and endurance, in explaining the
marked increase in strength and endurance
obtained from the use of the product known
among chemists as bitr o-phosphate, claims
it is due to its recognised nerve-building
properties. He says, and it must be admitted
that his logic is unanswerable, that, inas-
much as the muscles simply transmit power
derived from the nervous system, strong
nerves and a big reserve of nervous energy
are infinitely more important than big
muscles. Bitro-phosphate, not being a drug
or a stimulant, but a nerve-builder which sup-
plies direct to the brain and nerves the re-
quired phosphoric elements, was first pre-
scribed by physicians with marked success
in the treatment of neurasthenia, insomnia,
nervousness, debility and mental depression.
It was soon noticed, however, that, in addi-
tion to permanently relieving nervous dis-
orders, it also greatly increased strength and
endurance. The amount usually prescribed
is one 5-grain tablet with each meal, and, in
view of its comparative inexpensiveness,
those who wish to increase their strength
and endurance, as well as those who suffer
from nervous disorders, are strongly advised
to give it an immediate trial. — Apvt.

AWOMANS
LETTER
Mrs. Anzac, from foreign parts, has been
coming along in pretty solid batches, and
there are a few thousand more of these war
brides (mostly English) to tcllow. As one
of the Red Cross heads who met some of
them said : “They’re a healthy, rosy-cheeked
lot, and the babies look blooming after the
long voyage. But, not to put too much var-
nish on the subject, they’re more beefy and
solid than our girls, and when it comes to
feet and thereabouts —well, Mrs. Bullzac has
a remarkably fine grip of the earth. You
can look almost in vain for the slender
ankles of the high-steppiug Sydney girl.”
Melba’s concert last week was a gor-
geous success even for the best “draw” the
Australian booking offices know—it had been
billed as the Dame’s last song in Sydney for
years to come. When the plan was full the
Mark Tupleys resolved to chance it, and re-
newed acquaintance with the Town Hall’s
exterior decorations. Some of them scored—
a few who hadn’t booked were allowed in-
side the tabernacle..
The Dame sang even better than her wont.
There was genuine emotional quality in
such encores as “Annie Laurie,” and Tosti’s
“Good-bye” was a climax of perfect musical
mechanism, with something that sounded
like a sob behind it- The sudden illness of
her old friend .Tohi) gave us Melba in her
softest mood. With a piano only as accom-
paniment the Voice rang out full and clear
like the matin-song of a thrush.
Leila. Doubleday’s fiddle was a brilliant
aside. The Victorian girl caught the flame
of enthusiasm, and answered with kindling
fires not expected from her. Pianist de
Bourguignon, an accomplished Belgian who
served in the war, and accompanist W. G.
Burrell, completed the small concert party.
The v.r. mat was well furnished. Ex-
cellency Munro Ferguson and Lady Helen
and sub-Governor Davidson and his lady
(who came from Moss Vale for the event)
were in the sacred reserve. With them Lady
Doris Blackwood, who temporarily absconded
(but only to grace the A.D C. camp, just
behind) from the front selection. Melba’s
diamonds illuminated the platform.
Miss d’Erlanger, Lady Galway’s daughter,
who has been studying at Adelaide Uni., is
due in Sydney with mother. They’re off
to the other side. But a re-shufHe of the
v.r. cards gives Governor Galway another
12 months, probably, in the.grape State.
It lqolted full of innocence as it lay
on the tray, and how was Melba to know
that in either the chicken or the glass of
water lay all the elements of an inter-
nal revolution? With an unruffled
mind she partook of the sandwich and the
water: and went to bed —it was the day
after her big. Sydney concert. Three hours
later she felt as though she was the walls
of Babylon surrounded by the battering
rams of the enemy. For four days the
struggle raged; then with Peace restored
she began wondering if that chicken was
a Bolshevik holding his last mass meeting,
whether the water and the chicken were
settling a Prohibition argument, or if the
bird was a rooster with a hatred of the
Dame’s top notes, and the whole disturb-
ance • was merely the desire of the male
to crow over the female. Anyway, the
impolite fowl has not lessened Melba’s
affection for the birds of the air, and her
sole unsatisfied desire in leaving Sydney
is that no birdman has dropped from the
•clouds with an offer to fly her to London.
Country Week tennis at Double Bay
courts, had very mixed weather to begin
with, but the contestants kept on valiantly
even when a wild wind came out of the
west and almost took control of the courts.
Dr. Dartyy, R.A.N., a keen player, is back
in Sydney; Major Selmes, D 5.0., just
released from quarantine, had a whack at the
balls last week ; and Lieut. L. M. Howell
also showed up on the lawns from France.
Many sun-browned maids and matrons, in
white linen frocks, from the country, were
on and around the green. Miss Scott
(Narromine) and her sister, Miss Dowdell
and Miss Bragg showed some stylish racquet
work. Camden was represented by Mrs.
Steele and Miss Faulder. Miss Case (a
brilliant performer, who won the country
championship), Mrs. Clifton and Miss Con-
ran came from Lithgow for the fray. Manly
kept its end up with the special assistance
of Miss Ilalley.
The Parramatta Burnside Homes, the pet
hobby of James Burns, M.L C., had a couple
of new annexes added last week. Set in
a pastoral scene which scintillates within a
wreath of golden wattle in the early Spring,
these Homes for orphans of plaidie descent
have frequently been blessed by Governor-
General Fairguson. On Saturday, while the
mercury showed a shade temperature of 104
deg., his Ex. made some complimentary and
thoughtful remarks in opening the addi-
tiqns. Dr. and Mrs. Robertson were re-
sponsible for one section which bears a long
Gaelic inscription. Lady Helen spake the
formal word over the section given by Mrs.
E. M. MeGaw. Is it the same, child’s petti-
coat of which the cables spake a few years
ago? J. J. Virgo, the Y.M.C.A auc-
tioned a little woollen garment for £6O at
the Burnside party. Queen Mary made it.
On Jan 17 sailors and soldiers’ wives will
rally round Voluntary War Workers in the
Town Hall vestibule. The idea is to say
“Thank you” to those women whose useful
toil has not been thrust conspicuously under
the limelight. Lady Davidson, whose stir-
ring address to a huge meeting of soldiers’
wives centres a few months ago in the same
place made her a comrade in the movement,
comes to town for the gathering.

their power to make

John, is Australia’s

For. one thing, he is
so entirely free from
the vanity and the irri-

many of the cult as easy to handle as pnckly-

tive. And that's not a small thing. More,
he has been the tactful buffer between the
imperious and often impulsive singer and the
general public—and that’s not easy, either.
His illness, it is hoped, is only temporary.
But in future he will have to go slow, and
take more care of himself.
Melba, accompanied by Peggy Center,
visited the Old Gold and Silver Fund last
week and bought a pair of quaint Russian
chrysoprase lace-pins and a snuff-box. Melba
has the keen eye for snuff-boxes. No use
trying to palm off one made DAY. (during
the war) for a gem used by a Louis or a
Charles.
Mrs. Gallien, who was on her way to Bor-
neo to join her husband, Captain Gordon
Gallien, was amongst the passengers on the
beached Houtman, but was one of eight who
were lucky enough to get berths _on the
Montoro and at latest was in Darwin await-
ing transhipment. Mrs. Gallien writes that
everyone feels very sorry for Captain
Guidhuis, who is described as a broken man.
Major Paul Goldenstedt, whose engage-
ment to a Westralian nurse is announced,
was doing pars. and rounds for the
Bathurst Times when _ war arrived. But
the fighting fever got him and he couldn’t
spare time for such trivialities as “copy.”
Hourly, he would take advantage of the
crow T d gathering for any of the afternoon
editions that the small sheet got into the
habit of running out, and distribute enlist-
ment forms in opposition. Inside a week
he had within an ace of 300 names. He was
probably the first man to suggest the route-
march >dea, having put it up to the clamor-
ing outside-the-offiee crowd when they wanted
to know who was going to pay fares to Syd-
ney.
Captain Bill Graham, M.C., of the
artillery, will bring home an English bride.
She’s Dorothy Ashe, of Nuneaton, where
father is the Anglican vicar.
Mrs. Mitchell-Thomson —she used to be
Madeline McEacharn and is a daughter of
the late shipping magnate Malcolm —will
have to get new visiting-cards- The death
of her father-in-law, Sir Mitchell-Thomson,
Bart (he was the first to get the title)
gives her husband the handle. The new Bart,
is a Unionist M.P. for North Down who
dabbles in literature. His travel-yarns con-
cern Manchuria, Siberia and the West In-
dies.
An Australian who found himself at
Stratford-on-Avon on Armistice Day was
Private T. G. Richmond, bellringer at St.
Mark’s, Darling Point, before the drums
began to beat. On leave, he chanced to
wander through Shakspeare’s hamlet and
was forthwith taken to Holy Trinity by
an Australian war-work?r, Mrs Galbraith,
who was helping the local Y.M.C A. at
the time. They were part of a big pro-
cession, and they chose the Aussie to ring
in peace in one of England’s most historic
villages.

to hang to the nearest bough, ’possum
fashion, by the soft silky appendages of 1919.
Lady Helen, in dawn o’ day pink satin the
other evening, had enough of the drapery on
the floor to move with a swish. The hand-
some v.r. Davidson lady, in a pearl-tinted
evening shimmer, also wore some of her
dress on the ground. Tie tail was
cut off everything but the dressmaker’s bill
for several years. Now it is in, and the
winter ballroom will entangle the male boot.
Melba had only a stray sash-like drapery
end on her Jan. 7 concert gown—just long
enough to touch the platform. It was the
end of a perfect frock—diamonds and foamy
white net with a pink glow underneath.
From Brisbane :—•
Returned Anzacs don’t seem to have satisfied
their craving for taking risks, for military weddings
are all the rage. The last Anzac on whom Cupid
scored a hit was Corporal H. W. Brereton, one
of the original 9th, who surrendered to Cissy
Browne, a well-liked Sandgate girl. May Browne
and Rita Grant were attractive maids. The
bridegroom was pounced on at the church door,
and carried shoulder-high to the waiting motor-
car by a mob of lads in uniform.
John Reid, managing director of the Brisbane
branch of the J. C. Hutton Prop., died last
Saturday. He was entering the lift at the Brisbane
Club, was taken ill, and expired in a few minutes.
Outside his business proper he had' done good
national service as a council member of the
National Agricultural Association for 30 years.
Sister Dorothy Webb, with 3-J years’ service and
a Royal Red Cross to her credit, came home last
week. She was In France during all that time
with the exception of two very short leave periods.
She’s a daughter of Dr. and Mrs. Webb.
Over £BOOO has been raised for the Queensland
War Nurses Fund since hostilities began, and the
expenditure in connection therewith amounts to
£l9O. £5OOO is in the bank at fixed deposit,
£2389 is available for the upkeep of a War
Nurses’ Home (when one is established) and the
remainder has gone in the purchase of war certifi-
cates and a donation to the Nurses’ Equipment
Fund. All the committee asks now is that some-
one will lend or donate a home where sick or
tired nurses may rest.
John Meagher, M.L.C., the pillar of so
many Hibernian operations, leaves for the
Cold Country in March via U.S. Some of
the family goes with him as a safeguard.
Denis Sheridan, of the Harbor Trust, who
has been for two years with the Army Ser-
vice, returned last week, invalided. He
arrived in Blighty about a year ago with
double pneumonia* and the effects of it have
since kept him from having a look at the
long, long Front. A few weeks after his
departure a son arrived from Storldand, and
was on hand to greet daddy.
David Barker, of the Anzac Book, now a
warrant-officer, who cfid his bit first as a
stretcher-bearer on Anzac, and later with
the Indian forces in Mesopotamia, is due
home in a week or so. For the last year or
more he has been on loan to the Egyptian
Government, attached to the Photo Section,
in Cairo and thereabouts, art-editing the
Kia-Ora Coo-ee in his spare, moments.

took a wife (Miss Cummins, well known in

The Citizens’ party to the Anzacs —how
that word creeps back, though among the
men themselves . it’s not popular!—was by
ferry to the Avenue, Lane Cove, in Satur-
day’s blaze. The Avenue, an arid flat lapped
by the cool river, has sheltering trees on
the rise behind, and therefore reasonably
lives up to its beguiling name. Sports, danc-
ing, and a tug-of-war, with Brigadier Alex.
Jobson to lend his aid, made the company
warm enough to drink flagons of soft stuff.
Lulu Boyce, one of Neutral Bay’s dainti-
est maidens, has accepted a diamond or two
from Ronald Hall. He’s a Digger who in
pre-Gallipoli days grew wool.
Baritone Filippini, of the Gonsalez Opera
Co. (deceased), is apparently taking root
in this old town. He’s teaching singing
in the Italian manner. During the holidays
he had a wedding and asked Nance McFar-
land, a local girl, to help him.
Sister Pike, who left us a couple of
years ago, returned last week as the wife
of Lieut. Laflin, whom she married while
at the St. Albans convalescent home, in
London. •
The learned Lousia Macdonald, M A., for
close on 27 years chiefrainess of the
Women’s College within the Sydney ’Var-
sity, has sent in her resignation. She’s
almost due to retii’e. After a long and
strenuous charge of the bluestockings, she
merely waits for the councJ to choose her
successor.
The present Marquess Conyngham is mar-
ried to an Australian girl, Betty Tobin. He
was for a time m the North Irish Horse,
and recently succeeded the old Marquess,
who went west with pneumonia.
Miss Fox, whose tireless : enthusiasm for
work as lion.' matron at Rose Hall will not
be forgotten by many a grateful Billjim, is
holidaying with relatives, at Moruya. When
the Red Cross brought in the edict that
only trained nurses could be inatrons of con-
valescent homes, Miss Fok and Mrs. A. J.
Hare (Woolwich Home), two of the most
able honorary workers, who ran their heal-
ing havens on velvet tyres, had to retire.
It was a heartbreak to them, and Bill lost
two mothers.

THE above illustration shows a Beale
sound-board being planed in one
operation upon a 36-inch planer. This
wonderful machine at the same time tapers
it from thick in the treble to thin in the
•bass —a necessary feature for the production
of good tone. Beale sound-boards are'made
of hand-selected, straight-grained white pine.
They are built up from small boards, carefully
joined edge to edge: "a * grain being used
in the bass,*gradually becoming finer toward the
treble end. The ribs are straight-grained hr, and
are of a special form which keeps the sounding-
board in the convex shape necessary to good
tone production. Each board is protected back
and front with several coats of our own special
varnish. The better musician you are, the more
you will appreciate the Beale.

MELBOURNE
CHATTER
Boarding-liouses of rubber, guaranteed to
expand to twice their normal size without
an explosion, will have to be adopted to
meet the congestion in holiday resorts. At
present, life in the seaside dens would make
a tinned sardine roar lustily for a Housing
Commission. The other night this chatterer
sat down in the gloom of the verandah of
her temporary residence, when the seat arose
in a blanket and hissed “Damn you, you
might let a fellow get a wink of sleep.” A
neighbor’s great hairdressing turn had made
her elbows recognise the size of the micro-
scopic room, and she was anointing her
raw angles with lanoline, though she had
nothing to quell her indignation. Meal-
times provided even less liberty of action,
and we were wedged in around the groan-
ing board uncertain that our chop was not
the chop next door. One day somebody
had the temerity to complain to the
marchioness who ran the place for a little
more room all round. She peevishly re-
plied that her house was unoccupied for
seven or eight months each year and she
had only the remaining time to “reap her
harvest.” “Reap her harvest” was good.
We were the dirt which produced the
golden grain.
Sassiety, a little brown about the face and
arms, is drifting up from the seaside and
the old tearoom-parties are beginning again.
Mrs. Pannifex and Mrs. A. Richardson roped
off an arm of the Oriental Hotel lounge on
Friday and entertained about 100 beautiful
summer hats and frocks. Sister Mrs. Teddy
Muir brought an unimpaired complexion
with her and roused the envy of the crowd
by wearing pale pink. She explained that
she had dodged sunburn by taking her dip
at five o’clock every morning.
Mrs. Billy Falkiner turned Fairholme up-
side down last week for a big farewell party
to the popular Dot Hidgeock, now Mrs. Gill.
Dorothy’s mother has stayed away from thfe
Murrumbidgee sheepery for a string of Dot’s
farewell parties. At Mrs. Falkiner’s revel
the ballroom was turned into a music-room.
The hostess, in lobelia-blue foulard, cooled
her summery guests with pink ices.
Pianist Cahill will arrive in Melbourne
from Bananaland early in February. Some
observer has discovered that Cahill owns
the smallest hands of any known adult
pianist.
Myra Lumsden has said “Yes” to Stanley
Tomkins, who is art master at the Swin-
burne Technical College, and hails from
Ballarat. Myra is one of the pretty daugh-
ters of Alf Lumsden.
The engagement is just proclaimed of Lieut.
Bolton and Eileen Fitzgerald. The fiancee
is the dainty little daughter of Tom Fitz-
gerald, the popular clubman and golfer, of
Tooradin. Bolton is an Englishman who
was known about the swaggah Melbourne
clubs before the war. He did his bit from
1914, and is back with the furloughed Anzacs.
He was wounded at Gallipoli and gained
his commission in France.
Baronet Rupert Clarke has a grand-
daughter now. The young woman arrived
at daughter Phyllis Power’s London address
a month or so back. Reggie Power, who
served in Salonika, got back to his native
Melbourne in December with a badly-injured
leg. He goes to England sometime soon to
bring the missus home.
Som# time ago a Minister who wouldn’t
be noticed in a crowd acquired a handsome
young secretary who could have played
juvenile lead in any dramatic co. The
first time the Minister went on tour the
station-master at Back o’ Sunset seized the
secretary’s bag and hurried along the plat-
form with it, while the Minister for Fire-
works staggered along behind with his own
luggage. It was a good joke at first; but
when the same thing happened at Ghost
Ridge, Jumbuek Plains and 20 other
places, the Minister lost patience. The end
came at Wild Dog Gully, where a bloke
of the township approached the Minister
with, “ ’Ere, you come down with th’
Minister ter-day, didn’t yer? I wanter see
’im.” “I am the Minister.” was the reply.
The man of Wild Dog Gully looked at him
incredulously and spat on the ground. “Ah,
rats!” he said. Shortly afterwards the
secretary was transferred to a less public
job in another department..
Major Watt, one of the new military
0.8.E.’s —please note the qualification —
married Muriel Hartley W illiams, of Mel-
bourne, a daughter of the Judge. In a re-
shuffle she espoused an English colonel
named Lawford. Lawford proved a success
as a fighting man, and he is now a profusely
decorated General and a knight. She is
looking somewhat florid these days, but still
constitutes a good advt. for those photo-
graphers who can get her to sit for them.
Large pictures of herself and the General
were features in the Knightsbridge window
of Barnett, the ex-Australian, recently.
Talking of London, it is said to be abso-
lutely creeping with moneyed Victorians.
Mrs. “Jack” Brooks has been there for a
long time now. Lieut. Jim Burns, of Parra-
matta, is a half-brother of hers, and he
found it handy when he got wounded the
other day, and ended up in Wandsworth
Hospital.
The numerous Chirnside family seems
to have shifted to the other side en bloc,
as our fascinating Allies have it. Audrey
Chirnside (as was) is there with her steeple-
chasing, Yeomanry husband Harold Pape.
Gladys Chirnside, of the large and dazzling
blue eyes, is seen about a lot, and so was the
Captain Chirnside who has been General
Birdwood’s faithful A.D.C. for so many
years. Captain Chirnside had an odd sort
of job when Birdie went visiting battalions
and batteries in the line. He bore in his
arms a mass of illustrated London papers
and dealt them out to the Diggers. Birdie
hated to visit a unit without a good stock
of literature, and the only one he really
trusted to efficiently distribute the priceless
stuff was Chirnside.
The Austral Salon
is now 29 years old
and the senior
women’s club in
Australia. It has
labored faithfully all
those years with a
cup of coffee in one
hand and a slice of
cake in the other.
The quarters in
which it lived for so
long were con-
demned, but its aims
have never been im-
pugned. So it
flourishes again in
Elizabeth-street, near
unfashionable Little
Bourke-street. All
the Salon gods were
placed carefully in the same order as in the
old rooms. Mrs. Quinnell, here pictured, has
been secretary for 14 years. She is the
widow of an Indian Army medical-officer,
Colonel It. J. Quinnell. With her has been
associated all along, as treasurer, Mrs.
Horatio Webb, and their combined efforts
have kept the coffee-urn going.
Colonel Cohen is coming home. Like most
persons named Cohen he is outrageously
affluent. For the past three years he has had
charge of the 6th (Artillery) Brigade, and,
though the authorities persistently omitted
to make him a General, he did good work
with it and repeatedly harassed the unspeak-
able Hun to the verge of frenzy. He has a
C.M.G. and a D. 5.0., and with luck he
should be entitled to write even more of the
alphabet after his name before the Pearce is
signed.
A muddled sort of cable came from N’York
on Tuesday to the effect that “a sum of
£400,000, held in trust by the Morgan Co., is
to be divided among the servants of the late
Mr. Yuill’s daughter, the Countess Port-
arlington-Lethbridge.” The Lethbridge is. a
superfluity. George Yuill’s only child, Win-
nie, married Lord Portarlington 10 years
ago, and she is still the bearer of his name.
The Earl is a stout person in the early
forties who has not seen over-much . service
in France. It was no fault of his; but
maybe he didn’t worry. “Bombs and things
ruin the shape of one’s clothes,” he re-
marked; and, anyhow, the scenery over
there is dreadful.
Artie Staughton, who went to India to
buy army remounts, has been ill in England
for weeks, but is now convalescent and will
presently start for Australia.
The Hunter Landales’ Riverina homestead
has been burnt to the ground and all the
contents tvith it. Hartwood, which originally
belonged to the late Richard Blackwood, was
one of the best humpies in the district.
Adelaide observations and discoveries: —
Amongst latest Royal Red Cross winners is Sister
Grace Burns, daughter of the quiet Scotchman who
lias filled the Register's chair while William
Sowden has been on tour. Her good work won her
a mention in despatches in the far-off days when the
wounded from tl:e Landing were being brought to
Egypt.
A necklet of diamonds set in platinum, With an
exquisite black opal pendant, has been given to the
Galway lady as a parting present. Her good-byes
were to have been said at the end of December, but
the postponement of the sailing of the Niagara gave
her a fortnight’s grace, and she and Miss d Erlanger
deferred their departure till Monday. The last few
days have been put in quietly at Marble Hill.
Chaplain G. P. Cuttriss, who has published a little
book of experiences in France with the 3rd Division,
returned on Sunday. He was in the S’African
trouble as a combatant Maorilandei.
Mrs. T. Ainslie Caterer, wife of the acting-
headmaster of St. Peter’s College, died on Sunday.
She had been ill for a long time.
Col. H. A. Powell has returned after an absence
that dates from October, 1914. He was second in
command of the No. 1 Stationary Hospital, and got
his first “mention” when the Turks attacked at Ismailia
and Serapeum in February, 1915. Two other “men-
tions” came while he was at Lemnos and in France,
and a C.M.G. followed. The No. 1 Stationary Hos-
pital subsequently became the Third Auxiliary Hos-
pital at Dartford, and then the 2nd Australian Gen-
eral Hospital near Boulogue, where the staif —doc-
tors, nurses and orderlies —were Australians. Before
he sailed for Australia . Col. Powell put in some
weeks studying the after-treatment of wounded in
England, with a view to future work here. He
brings back enthusiastic accsunts of the work done
by Lieut.-Col. H. S. Newland, also one of the
original members of the No. 1 Stationary Hospital.
Newland is at the Face Hospital at Sidcup, Kent,
where badly-wounded men are being fitted with new
noses and jawbones.
Pianist Laurance Phillip, who died from ’flu’ in
California, was a nephew of the Otto Schomburgks,
and gave a recital at their house in aid of Red Cross
Funds when he was here last winter.
The death of Mrs. Archibald will leave the Federal
member for Hindmarsh a lonely man. She took a
keen interest in his work and often journeyed to and
from Melbourne with him when the House was sit-
ting. Mrs. Archibald was one of the few women
from this State who went to London with the Parlia-
mentary party at the time of George’s coronation.
Miss Johnson has booked her passage to London in
a February boat. She is to be married there to Bob
Rees of Dalgety’s London office.
A ripple of joy was caused in the Adelaide Police
Court 'this week when a lady gave her name as
Atlantic Ocean May Jones. It’s pleasant to hear
during a heat-wave.
On Armistice Night, when all the fog-
horns and steam sirens of Hobart were
blowing off, and all the brass bands and
fife bands, and tin-can bands, and bagpipes
were noisily parading the highways, the
Salvation Army band (brass) slip'ped along
a quiet street to his Majesty’s gaol, and
close beneath the shadow of the grim wall
played with soulful plaintiveness “Are You
Coming Home To-night?” Thoughtful!
Gordon Chirnside and Mavis Thiel are. to
be made one on January 31. The wedding
will be an evening affair at Scots’ Church,
Collins-street. Bettie O’Hara and Beryl
Fitts are to be bridesmaids.
Nellie McDermott, the St. Kilda violinist,
the other day promised to play second fiddle
for life to Professor Henri Penn, the Bel-
gian pianist, who is attached to the staff of
Sydney Conservatorium. Flautist Johnny
Amadio was best man, and after the ceremony
at St. Pat.’s, there was a gay wedding-
supper at Wickliffe House.
From Blighty comes news of the marriage
of George Arthur, of the well-known
Ballarat family, with a daughter of the
late Bennett Burleigh, war correspondent.
Before the war young Arthur was with
Gordon and Gofch. In France he supervised
the organisation of the Y.M.C.A. field
service, and was given the honorary rank of
major.
Many a Billjim
has blessed the
Dental Corps. In
other wars the sol-
diers nursed their
toothache along with
other woes. Lieut.-
Colonel Hall was
organiser-in-chief for
all the military
fangs of Australia.
He was an old
militia officer, and
ranked high in his
profession. It was
found that 90 per
cent, of the soldiers
needed dental treat-
ment, and to keep
up the tooth-stand-
ard about 150 dental
officers were sent from Australia to England.
France and Egypt. They were a fine lot of
well-trained experts. In Australia, dental
officers w’ere established in every State
under a major. Major Bill Aitken looked
after Victoria. In two years 40,000 sol-
diers were made dentally, fit before embarka-
tion. This photo of the Lieut.-Colonel was
taken by Alice Mills.
Dr. R. E. Weigall, president of the Auto-
mobile Club, has put up a neat war record
of his own. With his car he has thus far
met every boat that has returned from over-
seas with soldiers aboard, an example that
has had an excellent effect in keeping other
club-members busy.
Trippers to England are advised not to
take dear Fido with them these times. Hydro-
phobia, epidemic thus early in the European
year, is regarded as certain to rage furiously
in the “dog days” of June and July. Aus-
tralia and M.L. are closed against British
bow-wows for an indefinite period, and even
though dogs from here are admissible to deah
old London, they can’t be taken for a walk
without a chain. It isn’t even safe for
Fido to frisk for joy. A policeman is always
likely to mistake doggie’s frivolities for
rabies, and rain baton blows upon his curly
head.
Major J. K. Mountain, an Australian engi-
neer, is now second-in-command at Regent s
Park Air Depot, London. He’s the only
son of Adrian Mountain, who used to be
surveyor to the Melbourne City Council, but
retired some. years ago and went to live in
his rural eyrie alongside the lovely Sher-
brooke Falls, at the back of Mount Dande-
nong.
Dr. Jack Gordon, D. 5.0., is coming home
from the wars. He practised in Fitzroy be-
fore he put on khaki. Lately he has been at
Southall, where poor old Billjim goes, as a
rule, when he has lost a few limbs. Its
quite handy to Windsor. The boys from
there often drop in on George for a fag.
When Vivian Sleep went under to ’flu’ in
Egypt the other day, Australia lost a sol-
dier who had been right through two wars—
the S’African disturbance and the one now
having its edges trimmed off. Sleep, before
joining up with the A.I.F. in 11)14, was a
grazier at Longwarry, and had a pre-war
commission in the 13th Light Horse. He
saw the war through as a private, however.
His father, now a very old citizen, is Lieut.-
Colonel John Sleep, who was pioneer 0.1 .
of the ancient Ballarat Infantry nearly four
decades ago.
An Australian officer just home tells of
London as he left it: —
A dance in London is not the ordered affair that it
used to be. In fact, it approximates closely to a
revel. Four niggers may supply the music. No one
actually dances. There isn’t room, for one thing.
And, anyhow, it. isn’t the fashion, The outcast who
starts doing tango steps, or anything of that pre-
historic description, is looked at so coldly that his
(or her) feet freeze, and the performance conse-
quently comes to an end. The dancers simply walk
round in each other’s arms, sometimes with the
music, more often against it. At intervals one of
the niggers bursts into song, or deraches himself
from his colleagues and executes a fandango on his
own. The principal course on the supper menu is
invariably champagne. There is little butter and
no eatable bread, but the habitual champagne-chewer
can indulge in his pastime all night if he likes, and
even then there will be lots over.
With Melba off to Europe and prophesy-
ing that it will be more than three years
before she returns, her Honolulu “discovery.
Peggy Center, has also taken wing and de-
parted for her home island. Peggy was a
member of the well-advertised singing class
started by Melba at the Albert-street Con-
servatorium in one of her enthusiastic
moments.
Melba invited' friends to a musicale on
a recent afternoon at the new Orehestrola
rooms in Collins-street. Two Anzacs named
Williams (not related, by the way) are
partners in the Orehestrola Co. One of them
has developed a machine devoid of rumble
and scratch; it is almost entirely Austra-
lian. and Australian woods are used to
enclose the gear. Frank Williams, the
other Anzac, an Adelaide boy, decorated the
rooms in an original way. There are big
lounges from which to gaze at painted wall
drapings. The decorator managed to get
some 400-years-old Italian wall plaques that
take some looking into. The room was
dressed in nasturtiums in flat green jade
vases. The lights are amber-shaded and the
glare of Collins-street is shut out by striped
green blinds. Dame Nellie, in a plaid suit
and a shady black hat, made an encouraging
speech.
Jimmy McDonald, who, before donning
khaki, was a popular figure in artistic
Bohemia, is back in Melbourne. He took
part in the Landing and was wounded in
the middle distance. Afterwards he served
in France as camouflage artist, until his
health broke down as the result of wounds.
John Longstaff, in London, is busy on a
portrait of General Monash Australia’s war
chief. Another Longstaff has done various
fine war records, including a picture with
tears and glory in it—a view of all there’s
left of the Church of Villers Bretonneux.
That’s the hamlet where the Australians
did probably their very biggest thing—one
of the very biggest things of the war. lon
remember how they checked the till-then
invincible army which was rushing on
Amiens? From the hour of that check the
war situation changed and the Allies never
turned back. Second Division Headquarters
chose a reproduction of Longstaff’s “Villers
Bretonneux” for a card to carry their Christ-
mas greetings home.
Julius Grant, partner of Bert Bailey, has
just lost his mother, who was in her 80th
year. She came to Australia with her father
and mother when 16, and the family settled
on the Loddon.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
An impudent monopoly was aimed at and
to a certain extent achieved, in Sydney, the other
day. After the recent yearling sales the Big
Breeders formed themselves into an Association. To
be eligible you had to have not less than five mares
pastured in N. S. Wales, and an average of not less
than seven yearlings during the past three years.
In connivance with the salesmen they ruled that
only their yearlings should be sold during race
weeks--the only time, of course, when all these who
buy yearlings are on the spot. The Small Breeders
bucked—and so violently withal that the “N.S.W.
Breeders’ Association” thought it politic to suggest a
conference. The two associations have now amalga-
mated, and they have the nerve to say that, in
future, no yearling bred outside N. S. Wales shall
be sold during race weeks. Under the original
conspiracy the breeders' of such champions as
Cetigne—one of the best up to 1} mile in Australia
—and Kennaquhair—easily “the greatest stayer we
have—would have been excluded. (Cetigne’s owner
only has one. and Kennaquhair’s two mares.) The
breeders of the following winners since last August
are still barred: Arch Marella, Gloaming, Rebus,
Aries, King Offa, Nightwateh, Irish Princess, Alms-
giver, Estland and Killowen —animals that have
already taken between them 22 big races.
* * * *
S. M. Herald (7/1/T9) remarks: “With
plenty of public form to guide him, the handi-
capper has distributed the weights of the A.J.O.
Anniversay Meeting with an even hand.” Well,
here are some samples. Arch Marella, who beat
Bee in the Summer Gup by a nose, meets the
latter on 51b. worse terms in the Anniversary.
But Wedding Day, who beat Bee ten lengths in
Tattersall’s Cup, has to give her only 61b. in
the Anniversary! For winning a Trial Handicap
at Randwick, and for just defeating Quaestor (who
was giving him 101 b.) at Rosehill, Booster is made
out to be as good as Bee. First Lesson is handi-
capped as better than Eastcourt, who won with Bst.
21b. up at Flemington.
* * * #
Greg’s 7st. 71b. in the Anniversary defeats
Comment and leaves Comparison gasping down the
course. About the soundest criticism of it is the
fact that this animal brought 1625 guineas at
auction immediately after the appearance of the
weights. Imagination staggers at the thought of
what Satin Bird would bring at auction just now.
He has an Epsom to his credit. He wdn twice
at the last Randwick meeting, carrying 181 b. above
the limit in the Welter and beating Anotto (who
won next day) ; and he followed that up by
beating Ardrossan —also a winner at this meeting.
In the Anniversary he is bracketed with Greg.
* * * *
Jumpers, like poets, drunks, etc., are born,
not made. Look at .Tack Rice for instance. As
a flat-racer he was useless, however low the weight.
Tried over the sticks he proved one of the best
hurdlers seen in Australia. He remained as in-
different as ever in the run home after the last
fence. The quality that enabled him to win
hurdle races carrying up to 13st. 121 b. was the
natural and effortless manner in which he jumped.
Graphone is of the same class, and one of the very
best in consequence. Pilot, Bay Eagle, Grafnell
and a score more won most of their races over,
as distinguished from between, the fences. In
fact, they jumped so fast and easily that it is
doubtful if they could have negotiated the journey
more quickly if the obstacles had not been there.
* * * *
“D.”: The Bulletin (9/1/T9) remarks
veraciously that “the horse of Australian parentage
is rapidly becoming a curiosity at Randwick.”
What is the explanation ? For my part I ascribe
it to fashion sheer damphoplish fashion. There
was never anything: wrong with the locally-bred
sire. For example, Trenton and Wallace —who inci-
dentally got Trafalgar, about the best horse
“imp.” or otherwise, that Australia has seen in the
last ten years. The theory that two-year old racing
has a pernicious effect on the embryo Australian
6ire doesn’t impress me so much. Carbine raced
as a two-year old. It didn’t affect him in his
subsequent career on the track, and in England he
sired Spearmint, a Derby winner and a champion
too. Mountain King, who is still getting winners,
raced as a two-year-old, and Trenton won the
Welcome Stakes in Auckland (M.L.) at that age.
Just as a number of misguided lads about town
have taken to the chewing-gum habit and the
pork-pie hat of the Bowery, so a majority of our
big owners have concentrated for years past on
imported sires, and with no more justification.
What is worse, they have concentrated on the
same sort of sire. Every recently imported horse
of note has been a St. Simon, with the result that
the country is being drenched with this particular
blood. It is a good enough strain, but Australia
already has too much of it.
* * * *
The best horseman killed in the war, pro-
bably, died only a few days before it ended —-
Fred Rickaby, the English jockey, and son of
another famous jockey of the same name. Young
Fred had a remarkable record in the Oaks. He
won it in 1913. 1915, 1916 and 1917, and,
was on the second in 1914.
* * * *
There is a dearth of good horsemen in
Australia. Across country there is no one in the
same class as the old-timers: Boyd, "of Melbourne,
and Mooney are probably the best of an indifferent
lot. On the flat there is but one front-ranker —
McLachlan. McLachlan is good over all distances.
He is keen away from the barrier in sprints. He
has judgment in long races, and he can finish. In
fact, lie is in or near the category of past masters
like Hales, O’Brien and Kelso. Some of the likeliest
boys of three and four years back went to the war,
and many of them will never return.
* * *
J. Timing's trainer, Kidman, went over-
land to Perth from Melbourne with Clever Jim, High
Tension and Dim Laddie. It was the first batch of
thoroughbreds to use the Great Western Railway.
The enterprise wasn’t a success. The horses were so
knocked out, as a matter of fact, that they didn’t
race at the Perth meeting after Derby Day.
* * * *
The trouble over the misplacing of Sydney
Damsel in the Villiers Stakes reached finality with
the complete stultification of the A.J.C. Committee.
The judge placed Panacre (who ran ninth) third and
Svdney Damsel (who ran third) nowhere.- The
tote paid over Panacre, and after long consideration
the A.J.C. decided to pay Sydney Damsel —in the
case of any backer who happened to have kept the
ticket. According to the A.J.C. rules, “the judge’s
decision is final,” and so we here have the interesting
situation of the Club’s governing body being the
first to violate the Club’s own rules. But the point
of real public interest is in the fact that the mis-
take should ever have been made. The A.J.C. pays
three stipendiary stewards to observe the racing,
and, in addition, it has three honorary _ stewards
(committeemen) on the same job. The judge has
an assistant with him in his box to observe the
placed horses, the judge’s proper concern being with
the winner alone. Yet with all this horde of watchers
the mistake was made and persisted in. The same
bevy of experts who placed a horse that bad run
ninth, under their very noses, so to speak, have the
power to disqualify an owner, trainer, jockey or
horse for something that has happened or that they
think has happened, on the other side of the
course, half a mile or more away. Surely there is
something wrong with such an arrangement. The
mistake in question, by the way, has stood the
A.J.C. in over £lBOO.
* * * *
About the slipperiest thing in diminutive
horseflesh to-day is the 14.1 pony Star Queen, by the
American sire Leonidas. This two-year-old fled out
of the barrier at such a rate at her first attempt
that the heads gasped. She then ran right off. The
following week she went mad again. Since then
her manners have had attention and she has done
well. Star Queen is in the same stable as Bronzite,
than which nothing better has been seen out on un-
registered courses for years.
*** * ,
“Bill” Hill’s number went up last week.
He died at Rancliffe, Woollahra —previously the
home of another well-known Sydney racing man,
Theo Marks —aged 80 and looking a lot younger. He
raced as “W. Carleton,” and latterly had specialised
in imported prads, which, though good, were unlucky.
Gadabout, Rushford, Irritation and Gallipoli were the
best of them. Gadabout won once at Randwick and
ran third in last Melbourne Cup to Nightwateh and
Kennaquhair.
* * * *
’S. Donogbue is head of the English win-
ning-jockeys’ list for the fifth year in succession
with 66 wins out of 270 rides. Carslake, the
Melbourne rider, is second.
* * * *
Spencer Leigh Hughes, journalist and
member of the House of Commons (if he wasn’t
defeated in the recent elections), recalls a yarn
of G. J. Bonnor, the Australian XI player in
the ’eighties, in his book “Press, Platform and
Parliament,” just out. On one occasion, when
the XT was in England, Bonnor was a guest of
the Johnson Club and was asked to make a few
observations on the doctor. “He confessed.” says
Hughes, “with the manly simplicity that befits a
giant, that until that night he had never heard
of Dr. Johnson. Someone laughed or gasped and
Bonnor added, as if in reply, ‘Yes, and what is
more, I come from a great country where you
might ride a horse 60 miles a day for three
months and never meet anyone who had. But I
have heard of him now, and if I were not Bonnor
the cricketer I would be Samuel Johnson.’ ” By
the way, Australia House, in London —the Com-
monwealth’s headquarters—is a sort of neighbor of
the doctor’s now. It stands alongside the old church
where Johnson used to make the parson very
nervous (there is a brass-plate on the spot where
the doctor used to fidget) and a Johnson effigy is
the onlv one in front of the tabernacle.
* * * *
“Slip”: N. S. Wales’s crumble in the
inter-State match v. Victoria was due to the selec-
tion committee’s omission to search out a fast
bowler. Contrariwise Vic.’s victories over both N.
S. Wales and South Aus. were chiefly due to the
hurricane efforts of E. A. McDonald. In practice
alone the inclusion of Jack Scott (Middle Harbor)
would have helped the N. S. Wa’es team. He has
skittled 20 wickets, averaging 18.25 this season.
* * + * *
Sydney grade cricket season is only half
through and yet there are 21 three-figure efforts
on the books! It emphasises the poor bowling
which is a feature of N.S.W. cricket just now.
The Gregory-Kippax partnership for Waverley
against Central Cumberland (when 183 went up for
the third wicket) was an unusually lively affair —
just the sort of thing the public pays eagerly to
see. Warren Bardsley made his third century this
season, raising his aggregate to 639 and his average
to 91.28. J. Bogie (Varsity) alone has better
figures —654 runs, averaging 93.42. Both are left-
handers.
* * * *
B. L. McCoy has come to the surface
remarkably in the past two years. With Glades-
ville Hospital last season he scored 1605 runs,
averaging 74.42. This season he has walloped
1460, averaging 91.12. He did his bit in khaki
on the other side of the globe, and therefore is
among the eligibles for the A.I.F. XI in England.
* * * *
Vic.’s 102 for the last wicket against S.
Aus. last week is no record. On a previous
occasion, v. S. Aus., Vic. had scored 261 for nine
wickets. The total reached 472 before Ellis (118)
and Hastings (106) were separated. At the Carlton’s
Club pavilion there is a photograph of them standing
defiantly before a scoring-board emblazoning their
glorious effort.
The Ranfurly Shield —the chiefest bauble
of M.L. cricket—shifted quarters the other day when
Canterbury (which won it in 1914-15 and six times
in ten years) went down to Wellington by two
wickets. ' The occurrence was due chiefly to J. S.
Iliddlestone’s fine effort of eight wickets for 59 in
the first inrings.
* * * *
A man who takes his morning header regu-
larly at 6.45 splutters this from deep water: “Don’t
talk to me about the vanity of flappers ! It’s nothing
to the prancing conceit of the athletic old ass. The
regulars dive off when they see him coming, but
there is always a stranger to pass some admiring
remarks about the old boy’s high-stepping run along
the beach or his shadow-sparring in the gym., and
that starts him off on a loud, buzzing lecture on his
muscles and the way he keeps himself fit. He isn’t
comfortable until the morning mug comes along and
bites. While he talks he keeps swiping at the atmo-
sphere or slapping his chest so hard that
the seagulls can’t sleep. This is intended
to impress the shivering questioner, who is
losing his own quiet exercise and getting a chill
while he listens. The lecturer’s, snorts, slaps and
shouts echo and re-echo across the water and every-
body has to do a long dive or listen. He says he is
59 and infers that he expects to live another 100
years. Then he arranges his towel like an acrobat’s
mat, stands squarely on it and does a back-bend till
you think he is going to bite his heels or split down
the front. As most of the bathers are neighbors
they have to be polite; but a. visiting schoolboy
obliged with the necessary acid yesterday while the
old boy was doing a bit of feeble shadow-sparring
and hoping to Heaven someone was noticing him. The
lad stopped, looked and called out, ‘Knock off, dad!
It isn’t real —you only dreamt it!’ ”
* * * *
Private George Cunha, the Camp Lewis
(U.S.) swimming instructor, did 25 yards in 10
seconds at America Lake recently, which is two-
fifths of a second off his own world’s record. Cuhha,
it will be remembered, came to Australia with
Ifahanamoku.
* * * *
The handful of patriotic N. S. Wales
schoolteachers who make an annual hobby of teach-
ing children to swim put through over 5000 . last
year, and the estimate was that 80 per cent, of
girls and 70 per cent, of boys mastered the art. The
instructors expect to do even better this season.
Walter Bethel,, who originated the scheme, has been
swimming and teaching hard for over a quarter of a
century. He was among those who “found” Barney
Kieran.
* * * *
A brace of lightweight Chows, Rud Kee
and Taggie Young—met at the Haymarket (Sydney)
Hippodrome last week to decide the Mongol
championship or something. The row was easily
the best yet seen at the Hippo., and Kee, on his
showing, would make it very sultry for the average
Caucasian of his weight. He has a devastating left.
AVith it he temporarily ruined his Young friend’s
right eye, and the weapon had much to do with
his getting the decision.
* * * *
At Sellick’s Beach (S.A.) recently E.
Ferguson, on an eight-valve machine, cut the
atmosphere at the rate of 95 miles an hour. He
reckons that with a little higher gear he can
eclipse J. Booth's world record of 103 miles.
Ferguson already holds the half-mile and mile
records of the" State, occupying 19 2-5 seconds
and 38 seconds ,for the respective distances—
electrically timed.
* * * *
Sunbrowned bdcolics tripped hundreds of
miles for the N. S. Wales country tennis carnival
at Sydney last week. And how they • did work ! They
began each day at 9.30 a.m. and twilight had set in
before the games ended—over 10 hours of tennis.
On one day A.' C. Laurie, of Maitland, played 15
sets (141 games). Always a titbit of the meeting
is the teachers’ events, and this year H. Woolley beat
11. W. Edwards (Attunga) in the singles by 6 —4,
6—2, and Woolley and Edwards won the doubles.
Edwards took the event in the two previous years.
Grey-headed G. P. Barbour, who came from Too-
woomba (Q.)—614 miles —and lasted u'ntil the
semi-final, played cricket for ’Varsity and Burwood
and Rugby Union for N. S. Wales a quarter of a cen-
tury ago. In the big event, the District Champion-
ship, Forbes just wiped out Buddabadah by 4 sets 36
games to 4 sets 30 games.
* * * *
“Ernest Low”: The news that Paderew-
ski, the lion-maned pianoforte genius, is to be
President of the Polish Republic stirs up within me
a memory of 20 years ago. Paderewski is, or was, a
great pool player and no slouch at feats of strength.
So I and (incidentally) Sandow, the strong man,
found out one memorable night. I was engaged in
bringing out Physical Culture, a magazine “edited”
by Sandow, although throughout its brief career he
never saw “his” articles except in proof, and I
had laboriously to teach him the names of some
of the muscles. I had a great “scoop” with my
first issue—nothing less than an article by Paderew-
ski on “Pianoforte Playing and Physical Culture” ;
an article for which some of the established maga-
zines would have paid a hundred or two, but which
I secured for nix. We met Paderewski at midnight
at the Eccentric Club, after he had earned a fabu-
lous fee for playing at Clievden, millionaire Astor’s
palace on the Thames. We played “live” pool till
the small hours, and after Paderewski had won
or divided every pool we trotted him off in a han-
som to Sandow’s school in St. James’s-street. There
we got him to do everything—bar play the piano.
We put him through his paces at weight-lifting and
general strong-man business; and at a game which
depends upon wrist and forearm strength he knocked
us (including the great Sandow) fairly silly! I
wrote the article from our scattered fragments of
talk and submitted it to Pad. before it appeared
under his name. He approved of it; so did the public,
for it sold out my first issue like hot cakes. And
after all these years I remember somewhat sadly
that that first issue was the most successful.
* * * *
The S.A. Lawn Tennis Assoc, wants to fix
up a Vic.-S.A. struggle in March next. The chief
obstacle is the dearth of first-class players in
Victoria. A. O’Hara Wood, J. A. Eaws, H. W.
Lemme, 0, R. Lister, J. Addison and R. M.
Gillespie have all died fighting, and Norman
Brookes, R. Lycett, R. W. Heath, 0. V. Heath, R. R.
Treloar, G. L. Patterson, and R. M. Rolland are
still O.A.S. There has been no Association tennis
of any kind in S.A. for the last four years, and
it is therefore difficult to say what first-class' players
will be available. When the Hun went mad
Ronald Thomas was the State’s best, and he is still
away. Reg Reid, another well-known figure, has
been killed.
* * * *
R. Botchers won South Aus.’s big shoot-
ing event recently at Ambleside. He hit a
2i-inch circle with 10 out of 12 shots at 200
yards—ls 3 out of a possible 156, which is a
record for the State.
* * * »
At the Redfern (Sydney) Harriers’ meet-
ing on Saturday W. W. Hunt tackled six events out
of seven and won the 220yds. (22sec.) and the 120
hurdles, was second in the 75yds.. and third in the
high jump, hop, step and jump and broad jump. It'
is the sort of thing that will ruin t.he youth’s
chances of ever being a champion at anything. Two
years ago Hunt could . run lyd. “worse” than
“evens” ; last week he could only do “evens” in a
rush for a record.
* * * *
“Riverina”: Once, when we came out of
the line —it was at a time when the western
front was a frozen horror —some of the boys dis-
covered that we were in hare country. The rifle-
men soon got busy. Bullets sang, and the whine
of ricochets resembled the song of many aeroplanes.
Our billet stopped several; and one Digger swore
dreadfully just because a bullet had ripped his
hat. Then a Frenchman’s horse got fatally punc-
tured, and the owner raised a row that ended the
sport.
* # * *
Lacrosse once was understood to be the
national game of Canada; but an Australian who
lately spent a fortnight in the country, travelling
from east to west, didn’t see the game played, nor
did he hear it mentioned. Also, he didn’t see a stick
or a ball in a shop window. Baseball has got that
country.
* * * *
“Riverina” : Journeying home on a Maori-
land transport, we came through the Panama
Canal. The Yanks —soldiers and civilians alike—
gave us a great how-do. They blew us good
band music, and showered coins, sweets, fruit and
reading matter on board. One magazine fell short.
Then the usual sudden galoot came to light. He
hopped overboard to do the retrieving act. A
shout of dismay (which rather puzzled us, as the
ship seemed stationary) went up from the shore.
We were in one of the locks, considerably above
the next level, and the water was running out
beneath us. Our man swam with a queer heavy
legged action. Then a handy rope was thrown.
He grabbed it, and to the accompaniment of much
barrack from his relieved mates, was dragged to
safety-—and arrest.- He was a good swimmer, but
confessed to being all in when the rope came.
* * * *
“Pick”: “G. 11.” (B. 9/1/T9), who ques-
tions my statement that rifle-shooters sometimes use
a 6in. bull, evidently‘•imagines that the beginning
and end of things are at the Randwick (Sydney)
range, where everything is admittedly carried on
strictly to regulations. On country ranges, how-
ever, very rarely have frames been erected to
hold the first-class targets used at 800, 900 and 1000
yards, and the riflemen who practise thereon usually
cut down the size of the bull at the shorter distances.
They find it a good lurk, too, when engaging in prize
shoots under N.R.A. rules, because the unaccustomed
larger bull then makes good shooting so much easier.
On every range, Randwick included, the shooters are
shown their hits with a 3in. spotting disc up to
700yds., and a 6in. disc thenceomvard to 1000.
* * * *
Many fishing enthusiasts spent their holi-
days at The Creel, but the sport was very
disappointing. There were hardly any trout in
the Snowy, and it was thought the cormorants had
been the cause of it. Young Bob Ashton, of Double
Bay, made the biggest catch of the season.
THE DOMAIN OPTIMIST.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
Image of Hope and spurner of Despair!
Immovable upon the rocks he stands
(A black north-easter wailing through his hair),
And angles for the fish which are not there.
His hands are redolent of long-dead bait;
A reek of berley permeates his clothes;
No one within the confines of the State
Smells higher, or knows better how to wait.
His hook is camouflaged with practised art;
Sweep cynically scrutinise the lure;
Toad-fish draw up before it with a start,
Examine it, and sneeringly depart.
Outside the Heads the lordly snapper swim,
And red and black rock-cod and nannegai;
All these, and more, lurk in the ocean dim,
But such —thrice bitter thought!—are not for him.
Dusk threatens. He can hear the strident ring
Urging the lovers to the Garden’s gates.
He hurriedly essays another fling.
He has caught nothing—not a blessed thing.
The evening southerly arrives. His dreadful hair
Now leans in the direction of the Zoo.
He does not shift—he still stays fishing there:
Image of Hope and spurner of Despair!
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

“Austred”: “J.H.” (B. 19/12/’18) mis-
conceives the attitude of the Red Cross in
one respect. It consistently set its face against
accepting the service of any applicant who,
being within the military age, was without
a reject certificate. Only the number of
representatives actually needed for work and
asked for by the society’s commissioners
were sent abi’oad ; and certainly men wish-
ing to avoid service in the A.I.F. found no
shelter under its banner.
A new Labor monthly is to be issued.
Its name will be the “0.8. U.,” and it is
to be the mouthpiece of the One Big Union
idea, concerning which other Labor papers
have lately been publishing disputatious
views. The 0.8. U., from all accounts, is
to be used to educate the proletariat in the
beauties of “industrial unionism” and to
teach “craft unionism” its futility; also,
according to an enthusiastic partisan in a
Melbourne paper, it will “train the workei-s
to own and control the industries in the
coming Republic of Labor.” It should start
its career by indicating, say, one dozen of
the industries which it intends to control,
stating in a straightforward manner exactly
how it means to get hold of them; and in
justice to the worker it should prove to
him by simple arithmetic that he will be
at least as well off in pounds, shillings and
pence under the new order of things as
he was under the old.

to the strain of working in these times, it is
astonishing that so many people allow them-
selves to get below par. The best of us are
none too strong, with the strain and anxiety
experienced and the increased demands for
work. Yet too many of us allow ourselves
to grow thin and bloodless, losing energy and
sleeping badly, suffering from indigestion,
headaches and nervousness.
Keeping up to the mark requires the blood
and nerves to be in good order. If these
are right, appetite and sleep will re-
turn, the digestion will give no trouble,!
and the nerves will grow strong and
steady—fit for any emergency. Thou-
sands of persons of both sexes have
testified to the many benefits derived from
the rich, new blood made by Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, and the resulting
improvement in nerves and digestion.

strength by beginning a course of Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills without delay. Go to
any chemist or storekeeper, and ask for Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills; then you will avoid
the cheap substitutes that are sometimes
offered.

Heart of Empire Series —
No. 11.
Victoria Embankment
From Westminster-Bridge to
Blackfriars the roadway is
100 ft. wide and 7,000 ft. long,
while thewall is of solid granite
Bft. thick and4oft. high. Built
by Sir J. Bazalgette on about
30 acres of land reclaimed
from the Thames at a cost of
£2,000,000, finished 1870. The
view shows the Cecil and
Savoy Hotels, Somerset
House (built in 1776 and now
used by the Inland Revenue,
Probate, Audit and Registrar
Generals Departments) and
Cleopatra’s Needle, an
Egyptian Obelisk erected
here after many vicissitudes
in 1878. Waterloo Bridge,
opened in 1817, is also shown,
it is beautifully pro-
portioned and cost £1,000,000.

OF ALL STATIONERS AND ARTISTS’-
MATERIAL DEALERS THROUGHOUT
AUSTRALIA AND NEW ZEALAND.
“The amount of Australian capital sunk
in the Northern Territory is nearly
£7,000,000, and the annual loss is £250,000,”
says Melbourne Age, doing its daily weep.
Well, England has about that much sunk in
the South Sea Bubble, but it doesn’t weep
over the matter. It simply worked hard and
paid the debt 198 years ago and started
afresh, with the faith in itself .that shifts
mountains.
Eyre Hutson, C.M.G., Colonial Secretary
of Fiji since 1908, who has been appointed
Governor of British Honduras, is descended
from a line of churchmen. He has had
wide experience in minor colonies since 1885
—at Barbadoes, Mauritius, British Guiana,
Jamaica and Bermuda. He represented Fiji
at the Wireless Telegraphy Conference at
Melbourne in 1909, and was Acting-Governor
of Fiji and High Commissioner for Western
Pacific in August, 1915.
When awake, Victoria’s House of Fat is
tenderly jealous of its “dignity.” It almost
tossed out the Electricity Commissioners Bill
because of a clause providing that these
radiant persons might be dismissed on a vote
of the two Houses or of the Assembly in two
consecutive sessions. The thing was charac-
terised as an “attempted outrage” upon the
Chamber of Fat, and was deleted. Members
had passed an exactly similar clause in the
Forests .Bill two days before, but were too
sleepy to notice it.
The S’African gold-mining industry lias
been badly knocked by increased costs. For
the first half of 1917, 17 Rand cos., whose
capital aggregated over 13 millions, pro-
duced gold worth £3,649,740 from 3,474,285
tons. Some mines showed a loss, and the
total net working profit of the 17 cos. was
only £55,943 —not one-half per cent, on the
paid-up capital. Ore reserves were reduced
by 8 million tons by the elimination of stone
which is no longer payable under present
conditions. Watch out —our old friend
Bimetallism is nearly due for a resurrection.
“Hawk”: Manly can’t use its garden hose
and its roses and geraniums are turning up
their toes. Manly is indignant. It may have
to abandon tennis and bowls. In the
Hawkesbury we have had one good rain in
12 months. Stock is doing a perish ; fruit
trees, bush trees are throwing in the towel.
No one bleats; we are used to being dead.
We are all broke; we are used to being broke.
By resting it a good drought recuperates the
soil; and bleached bones are a most excellent
fertiliser.
There was something characteristically
Hunnish about the story of that English
nurse in a German gaol who was undressed
once a week in the yard and hose-piped as a
substitute for a bath. Fleshy, square-faced
races who wear their eyes wide apart gener-
ally have a robust birth-rate, a notable illegi-
timacy rate—-before the war the Hun topped
the white man’s record in this respect, as
well as in unnatural offences —and a weird
tendency to hose undressed ladies in gaol
yards and elsewhere. This is usually de-
scribed as the manifestation of an artistic
tendency.
It looks as if a deadlock will be reached
in Victoria in connection with the Closer
Settlement Bill because of the refusal of
the House of Rich Landlords to accept the
clauses relating to the compulsory resump-
tion of estates. Yet the Leg. Council has
cheerfully accepted the principle of compul-
sory acquisition in the Metropolitan Tram-
ways Bill, thus granting to a municipal body
a power which it strenuously withholds
from the Government itself. It would be
most improper to suggest that the Council
was influenced by the thought that' broad
acres in the Western District are scarcely
likely to be required for tramway purposes.
Captain Lester, of the American Army
Intelligence Service, giving evidence before
a Senate Committee :
On 10th July, 1914, the German Government in-
formed German employees in Berlin that war would
soon commence. These employees had been despatched
to all parts of the world to conduct German propa-
ganda, and 31 of them had reached the United
States. German Lutheran pastors had been instructed
from Germany to preach pro-German sermons through-
out the States. Some of these pastors had joined the
army, and had been subsequently arrested for sedi-
tious utterances and sentenced to terms of imprison-
ment.
Do the Federal authorities want any
stronger argument in favor of closing Ger-
man Lutheran schools in Australia?
Died in France, a few days before Christ-
mas, Roderick Hutton, of the air force.
Hutton had been right through the war. He
was at sea, on his way to England, when the
Hun advance began, and he enlisted in the
Scots Guards on reaching England four
days later. He fought in France for two
years, and was then invalided to England
with wounds. Afterwards he returned to
France, this time with the R.A.F., and put
in another two years there. An elder bro-
ther, Oswald, went overseas long since as
a Red Cross commissioner. The late Tom
Hutton, well known in the Western Dis-
trict, was their dad. He ran sheep for
many years at the ancient Cheviot Hills
ranch, near Penshurst.
“O.B.”: Although Australia probably con-
tains the largest quantity of first-class mica
in the world, the production of that valuable
mineral is limited to the enterprise of one
small co. working handy deposits in South
Aus. With direct Tariff protection or a
bounty the infant industry would eventually
become capable of meeting all Commonwealth
requirements and supplying some of the
trade abroad. But all appeals for a little
encouragement have not moved the Govern-
ment to plug up the large hole in the Tariff
through which the cheap foreign mineral
enters the country. Presently, when the
shipping increases, Indian mica, gath-
ered by niggers averaging 6d. a day) will
be poured into the Commonwealth. The
Muscovite mica, most useful in insulation
and in many minor directions, reaches the
highest standard of quality in Australia. The
largest block ever recorded came from the
Macdonnell Ranges (N.T.), and fetched a
record price in the London market, one
parcel selling for 30s. per lb. Other
high-grade deposits are known to exist in
Queensland, Victoria and Westralia, but the
Government that is asked to help their de-
velopment replies with polite evasions.

How to
Withstand
Hot
Weather.
THE HEATED TERM.
The heated term is withstood without
a whimper by those people who have a
healthy stomach. Hot weather cannot
hurt the man or woman whose stomach
and digestive organs are perfectly
healthy. But you go into January or
February with a disordered stomach,
and see what the result will be.
SUNSTROKE.
Sunstroke attacks only the man or
woman whose stomach or digestive
organs are too feeble to protect the
strength and maintain one’s physical
power to resist the depressing effects of
the summer’s sun.
SUMMER DIET.
Summer diet, with all its vegetables
and unripe fruit, adds its unstable
influence to the depressing effects of
hot weather, causing a still greater de-
cline in one’s strength and vitality.
IF THE STOMACH IS OUT OF
ORDER.
Ninety-nine out of every hundred
people whose health and strength run
down in summer allow their stomach or
digestive organs to get out of order
That is easy enough—in fact, it is hard
to prevent without the use of some
thoroughly reliable digestant.
