Drifting Sand.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
THE riotous southerly drives it over
Yielding girl and expectant lover;
Loose sand blowing from Sydney
beaches
Silting over the pale tide reaches;
Flinging out on the moonlit night,
Squander, sifted and fine and white;
Up where the bracken climbs and flutters,
Down where the breaker rolls and mutters ;
In where the stunted bush makes cover,
Filling the hair of girl and lover;
Over the hands so closely twined—-
Sydney sands in a wet south wind!
Stars a-stvim in the frail cloud-tangle,
Seas far out on the rocks that wrangle;
Round moon galloping through the mazes,
Red grass fire (that pricks and blazes,
Making all for a moment bright
In the cuftain of tree and night),
Choking down by the barren dune
To leave us only a racing moon.
The hush of a, minute’s passionate cling-
ing —
But the sand comes scudding, and cool and
stinging,
Touches their lips and their finding fingers,
And on her bosom it falls and lingers.
Untwine your arms and unclose her hand,
Ever between you there is sand !
With drowsy flies in the long ward drum-
ming
(An old march song in his dulled brain
humming,
Like tramping feet in the desert sand),
A dream hand ever eludes his hand,
As he turns and turns on uneasy pillow
Where palm groves rustle and hot sands
billow.
Does he guess at the tryst that she is keep-
ing,
While he lies so near to eternal sleeping—
Fevered and torn by the Turkish shell,
Body and brain in a sweating Hell?
For the pain is long, and the end is slow,
And the spoils to the man who did not go!
There’s a laugh in the wind that leaps the
ranges,
A laugh for life and its merry changes.
And far away where the trams slide down
To the lilac towers of Sydney town,
There’s a laugh for those who went forth to
save us,
While we fool the trust that their leal hearts
gave us;
A laugh for those who have only scored
A straight white sheet in a long white ward 1
Would you grieve for the man who will soon
be lying,
Where the desert sands in the samiel flying
Will cover his cross with their arid piling?
Oh. seek fresh love, for bis lips are, smiling.
And the moon rides out like a highwayman,
With the gay old motto of “Keep who can 1”
Forget! Most men forget.... and woman
Is weak, and lonely, and only human;
And the wind comes up with a long caress-
ing,
And the lips that are near are warm for
pressing;
And the wild flowers spill through the dark
their sweetness,
And love is yours in its glad completeness.
Oh, the quiet ward, and the tranquil nurses!
Oh, the even moan, and the broken curses,
The fret of the flies’ eternal thrumming.
And the prayers of a Bedouin, incoming,
To mix with chaos and dying murmur !
When the grave is dug shall the sand be
firmer,
Tight-packed that no wraith may rise to
follow
By flat canal and by sea-front hollow.
Till it come to the far-off golden beaches,
Till it find the way to the Sydney reaches,
To place on the treacherous mouth and hand
Cold, dead lips, rough with desert sand?
The rollicking southerly mocks repining;
Shut out regret—with your hot arms
twining—-
There’s a lilt of life in each wild green
billow!
His face is still on the tumbled pillow,
And the woman's picture upon his breast
Has weighed it down in its pulseless rest.
And they bind his jaw, and they close his
eyes,
As safe in a new love’s arms she lies !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Preparing His Defense.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
I AM a Dutch waiter, newly arrived from
Holland. Seeing in the press numerous
suggestions from profninent citizens as
to how to hang the Kaiser, it has struck me
that Australia may bef interested in my
reminiscences of the gro/’at assassin. I was
employed, till recently, at the Herberg Het
Houten Paard in the jtown of Amerongen,
and my chief duty was to carry refreshment,
alcoholic and otherwise*, to the Kaiser and
his staff at the imperial villa, a few doors
up the Hoogstraat. <
It is the simple truth that ruin has
befallen Amerongen by the arrival of this
pack of brigands. On the Rembrandtkade,
overlooking the water, are Yon Tirpitz and
a number of royster/ing naval officers and
their servants. In the at one time peaceful
Vlamingstraat, close , to the church, lives
Hindenburg—seldom af ever sober after 10
a-m.—and a horde off swashbuckling retain-
ers who have caused a Schnapps-famine that
is felt for miles. Opposite the Kaiser, in
the Hoogstraat. is the Crown Prince. A
number of so-called “Actresses” descended on
the town simultaneously with the prince, and
many hundreds mora have arrived since.
When I left, the Municipal Authorities were
constructing an Actresses’ Quarter in the
park, partly in the* interests of public
decency, partly as a solution of the housing
problem. j
This problem has (been exacerbated by
swarms of speculative English attorneys of
the lowest class who halve come over, unasked,
to advise the Kaiser on the criminal law of
England. The Kaiser and his staff, ever
arrogant, decline to have anything to do
with them. They are, in consequence,
stranded. They spend their time threaten-
ing the burgemeester, and others in autho-
rity, with lawsuits and in striving ineffectu-
ally to force their way into the room in
which the Kaiser and his advisers hold their
conferences.
To add to the difficulty of the position
large numbers of persons are converging on
the town, from all over the world, with the
object of assassinating the Kaiser. Some
are mere popularity-hunters. Others are
duly-accredited Bolshevik officials. Many
have undertaken the enterprise to win
wagers. A young Finnish sportsman is ex-
pected early in the New Year who has
pledged himself to walk from Archangel, in
his evening-dress clothes, and kick the
Kaiser to death, or forfeit £5OOO. In the
interests of law and order, the burgemeester
has placed a cordon of police round the
town. The would-be slayers, baffled tempo-
rarily, are settling down on the outskirts, in
tents, and so forth, to the detriment of agri-
culture and the annoyance of the inhabitants.
Tirpitz struck me as a bilious pessimist,
ever eager to show that his imperial master
was doomed. Not so Hindenburg, who —
more especially when in liquor—fairly radi-
ated hope and cheerfulness. Neither
appeared to know much about English or
any other law. Ludendorff played a judicial
role ; on the one hand mitigating the abys-
mal gloom of Tirpitz, on the other checking
the insane buoyancy of the hero of Tannen-
berg. There were times, I thought, when
the Kaiser showed distrust of the capacity
of his staff. They were under no illusion
about the intentions of the British Govern-
ment. Von Stumm, an elderly diplomat,
formerly an attache in London, had in the
guise of a telephone operative abstracted a
copy of Sir F. E. Smith’s “Opinion” in the
Kaiser’s case from the Attorney-General’s
chambers. The purport of it was that a
conviction for murder could be obtained
against the Kaiser; the specific case chosen
being that of Elias Mugg, a gardener who
was killed in an aeroplane raid on the Blank-
some Hospital, foussex, in August, 1916.
The English legal authorities, in accord-
ance with custom, are placing no obstacles
in the Kaiser's way, so far as the prepara-
tion of his case is concerned. The Govern-
ment immediately acceded to Tirpitz’s
demand that every book on English law
should forthwith be delivered at Amerongen,
and the first of the ships chartered for the
purpose arrived some weeks before I left.
One case which I helped to open included
Broom’s Legal Maxims : Simon’s and Stuart’s
Reports (10 vols.) ; Vesey’s Reports (15
vols.) ; Moore’s Indian Appeal Cases (47
vols.) ; Brown, Macnamara and Neville’s
Railway and Canal Traffic Cases (35 vols.) ;
May on the Fraudulent and Voluntary Dis-
position of Property ; Emden’s Building Con-
tracts, Building Leases and Building
Statutes; Davidson’s Precedents and Forms
of Conveyancing (8 vols.) ; Coote’s Law of
Mortgages; Stephens, Gifford and Smith’s
Manual of Naval Law (the only book in
which Tirpitz seemed to have any confi-
dence) ; MacSwinney on Mines, Quarries
and Minerals; Arnould on Marine Insurance
(Tirpitz again interested) ; Attenborough on
Pawnbrokers; and Mac Queen c'n Marriage,
Divorce and Legitimacy. The last-named was
borrowed by the Crown Prince as soon as it
was unpacked and was never returned. The
episode caused considerable unpleasantness,
as Tirpitz is involved in domestic trouble and
wanted the book himself. He is not the
man to mince his words, and his remarks
about the Prince’s efforts as a collector in
the Verdun sector, and on the ethics of loot-
ing generally, were bitter.
The whole of the volumes are being trans-
lated into German as they arrive. They are
first dealt with by a staff of 1500 typistes,
who work in a temporary hutment in the
back garden of the Kaiser’s villa. The
typistes are nominally • under a feld webel.
but in point of fact they are an undisciplined
lot who obey no one. All day long they
surge about the passages in quest of tea,
face-powder, etc., and their giggling often
drowns the clatter of the machines. At the
front door are the English solicitors, clamor-
ous over their fancied wrongs. Over the
way at an open window lounges the Crown
Prince in the undress uniform of the Death’s
Head Hussars. On his knee sits the “act-
ress” _ who is, for the time being, in favor.
By his side is a bottle of champagne. Ming-
ling with the solicitors and reviling them,
the Hohenzollern family, Holland and the
world in general, are hundreds of indigent
actresses. And through the mob, waiters
from the different cafes struggle periodi-
cally with drink and food for the toilers
within. It is an animated scene, but ill-
adapted by reason of its noisiness for stren-
uous brain-work.
Hindenburg, as I have already indicated,
does not impress one as a reliable lawyer.
A tireless worker, yes. But temperamentally
over-sanguine, with the result that, after a
couple of quarts of his favorite beverage,
Ost Preussiche Bowl (champagne, stout,
rum and brandy in equal parts), he tends
to take a roseate view on every issue, re-
gardless alike of the facts and the law. As
an example I once heard him shout exult-
antly as follows: “Majesty, you are saved !
Hoch ! Hoch ! Hoch ! The good old German
god of legal battles is with us and the Eng-
landers must swallow defeat. I, the victor
of the Masurian Marshes, have! said so.
Hoch ! Hoch” He was here interrupted
somewhat tersely and asked to explain him-
self. whereupon he said that he had found
in Hale’s Pleas of the Crowm that “in case
of a riot, or rebellious assembly, the officers
endeavoring to disperse the mob are justified
in killing them.” He pointed out that “felo-
nious riot” consisted in “remaining together,
to the number of twelve, after proclamation
made to disperse.” He contended that it
would only be necessary for the Kaiser to
prove that there were twelve or more people
in the hospital and he must be acquitted.
Notice to disperse was clearly given by the
arrival of the aeroplanes. That Mugg and
the other people in the hospital were “re-
bellious” was proved by the fact that they
remained where they were, deliberately mak-

ing of themselves, so to speak, a screen be-
tween the bombers and the earth. And the
persons who released the bombs were offi-
cers. The chain was complete. The Kaiser
was impressed. Even Tirpitz admitted the
reasonableness of the proposition. At a
later stage, however, Ludendorff discovered
that the “officers” referred to meant “officers
of the law.” For a while the staff declined
to believe that even the English were cap-
able of giving canaille like policemen rights
over human life which were denied to the
military caste, but they were at the end con-
vinced.
I was waiting at lunch one day when
three or four tons of law-books were dumped
from a lorry on the pavement outside, the
passages, etc., being, at the moment, too
full to receive them. “How could I be ex-
pected to know all this law?” said the
Kaiser querulously. A roar of approval burst
from Hindenburg. “A defence, All High-
est!” he shouted, beating upon the table
with his beer tankard. “An unanswerable
defence! All Germany knows what a rotten
(vollstandig nutzloss) lawyer you are. ‘As a
soldier not to be mentioned, as a lawyer un-
mentionable’—so ran the witty phrase coined
by myself at the time of your decision in
the Furstenburg scandal.” Tirpitz admitted
that if ignorance constituted a defence there
were “ten thousand good German mouths
available to howl into the ear of craven
Britain the fact that their Kaiser was
totally ignorant of the laws of his own, much
less of other countries.” And finally he re-
stored the normal atmosphere of gloom by
producing Ellis and Blackburn’s Reports,
and indicating Barronet’s case where the
Court said that “foreigners... .must in re-
spect of knowledge of the law be put upon
precisely the same footing as native sub-
jects. We could not listen to a native who
urged that he was ignorant of the law he
had transgressed, nor can we do so to a
foreigner.”
Another gleam of hope came when Hin-
denburg found in Archbold that to constitute
murder the person killed must be “a reason-
able creature in being.” In other words, he
said, it is allowable in English law to kill
one w r ho is not reasonable, viz., a madman.
Tirpitz observed sourly that it was too good
to be true, that it was the first sensible
thing he had found in English law if it was
true, and that in any event it would be
difficult to prove that the gardener was mad.
Ludendorff said that he did not believe that
the indiscriminate bombing of lunatics was
allowed in England, though he sympathised
with the idea himself. There was something
mawkish about the spirit of the English law
which made such practices distasteful to it.
Still he admitted that the point was a likely
one, and undertook to look it up. Two or
three days later he produced a number of
books which clearly showed that the term
“reasonable creature in being” did not relate
to mad persons, but to the as yet unborn.
They were all very much interested in
Article of the Extradition Treaty be-
tween Great Britain and the Netherlands.
This article says that “where the individual
claimed by one of the High Contracting Par-
ties is also claimed by other Powers, for
offences and crimes committed in their terri-
tories, his extradition shall be granted to
the State whose demand is earliest in date.”
All were agreed that, wherever else he went,
the Kaiser should avoid England. When I
left a plan was at work to get him claimed
by the Republic of Andorra for felonies com-
mitted there. It was argued that a small
escort, armed with Lewis guns, could put
him in a highly favorable position in rela-
tion to the Courts of the Republic; and
that in this way his trial might be prolonged
indefinitely. In the meantime there would
be no chance of any other country securing
him. under the universal rule of Extradition
Law that “there is no extradition, if, and
while, the individual claimed is being tried.”
Life in Amerongen was now becoming un-
endurable. The Crown Prince was responsi-
ble for starting a racecourse outside the
town. Shares in the concern were taken by
the solicitors and paid for by cheques, most
of which were dishonored. Large numbers
of dubious characters —bookmakers, jockeys,
trainers and pugilists—arrived and proceeded
to fraternise with the actresses and quarrel
with the solicitors. The Baron Yon Oppel
(in attendance on the Prince), while pre-
paring to chastise a so-called “professional
backer” with the flat of his sword, had his
jaw broken. The burgemeester was faced, on
race-days, with the problem of throwing
open his cordon of police in such a way as
to allow egress from and ingress to the town
so far as ordinary citizens were concerned,
while continuing to bar out those who
menaced the Kaiser’s life. Finding the task
beyond him, he resigned, and was succeeded
by an extremist who declared all racing and
gambling illegal. The Racecourse Co.
became insolvent, and many of the towns-
people were ruined. Meanwhile the solicitors
—worshippers at the Lutheran Church—
fomented a sectarian dispute between the
Dutch Lutherans and the Catholics, who
previously had lived in harmony. The
wharfingers who were engaged unloading the
Kaiser’s law-books struck for higher wages.
So did the carriers. The typistes followed
suit. The demands of the strikers were
acceded to, and the cost of living rose to a
fantastic figure. On every side one saw
starving bookmakers and attorneys, and
always in his window overlooking the High-
street the fleering figure of the Crown
Prince, actress on knee and wineglass in
hand. The malign spectre of Bolshevism
began to stalk the streets. I left.
J. B. VAN DALLEG.
The Soapsud Saviour.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
THERE was a burst of unwonted ex-
citement in our street the other after-
noon. I noticed the gathering of the
clans before Whitty’s house, and in and over
Whitty’s front garden. The fact that
Whitty Avas not getting after the trespassers
on his carnations and the villains Avading in
his sweet peas and his sacred, double-dyed,
patent, five-petalled dahlias convinced me
that much was amiss. Nothing short of a
house afire and sudden mutilation and deaJi
in the family could account for the apparent
quiescence of Whitty in such harrowing cir-
cumstances. I went across, and found that
Betty, the five-year-old daughter of my
friend and neighbor, had got her head caught
between the stout, upright bars of the lron-
work of Whitty’s front verandah in such a
manner that extrication seemed utterly im-
possible. Whitty, distracted at the prospect
of his only child being a permanent, semi-
detached fixture to the house, land and
estate, was looking like a wild colt fresh
from the hills, tethered to a log for the first
time, restrained with straps, and badgered
with lashes and the yells of men. He was
trying to push Betty’s head back the way it
had apparently come, when it was obvious
to anyone with half an eye that the said
head would not go back. Very many people,
all more or less demented by the excitement
contingent on such an event and the urgency
of the need, were offering Whitty advice.
Whitty tried everything that was suggested
in the most impartial way. He pulled, and
pushed, and screwed, and twisted his hapless
child, Betty protesting the while with the
aplomb and poignant vociferation of _ early
girlhood. “Stand ’er on ’er ’ead, Mister,”
suggested the corporation laborer. Whitty
hoisted his offspring’s heels in the air.
“Work ’er up and down, boss,” said Ander-
son’s gardener. Whitty worked her up and
down, and Betty’s howl might have served to
drill a boiler. “Better tuck her ears in
and two of us pull and chance it,” ventured
a fellow from the hotel. Whitty gave the
idea a trial, tears pouring down his cheeks.
“Look out, you’ll be breakin’ somethin’!’
said Clarke. “It might be her neck,” sug-
gested Green. “S’pose you was to grease
her head?” hinted the journeyman butcher.
A pound of butter was used to lubricate
the child. Even then she resisted pressure, and
the clamor grew'. The public excitement got
well up to the breaking strain. “Send for
the blacksmith, and remove the house,” de-
manded an authoritative person in black. The
blacksmith had already been sent for. Mean-
while someone advised cutting off Miss Betty’s
hair. “It’s the kid’s ’air wot’s blockin’ yeh,”
the thoughtful stranger assured Mr. Whitty.
While Whitty was cutting his daughter’s
hair the blacksmith arrived, and surveyed
the disturbance with the cold eye of an ex-
pert. “Have to cold-chisel her through,”
said he emphatically. Here Mrs. Whitty,
who had recovered temporarily from her
third indulgence in hysterics, attacked che
blacksmith with a rake, acting under a mis-
apprehension. The blacksmith, with three
tooth-marks on his brow, hastened to ex-
plain that he advised chiselling the bar, not
the babe. While the smith was at work,
great cheering arose in the street, accom-
panying the clatter of horses and the rush
of wheels. Early in the agony somebody
had broken an alarm, and now the fire
brigade had arrived. With it came the
seventh policeman. The fire brigade
watched the smith, the police studied the
situation, the crowd waited in dire expecta-
tion. Would the child survive? “It’ll take
about 10 hours,” warned the blacksmith.
Betty yelled, Whitty wept, Mrs. Whitty tried
hysterics again.
At this stage a large woman with soapsuds
on her brawny arms pushed through. “ ’Ere,
I say, watcher doin’ with that pore child?”
she said. “Chisellin’ of ’er out,” replied the
nearest John Hop. “Gor bless my soul!”
gasped the washwoman. She trod on the Hop :
she walked over several people; she hauled
the smith aside as if he had been lewt. of
something in a bag; then she took Betty and
gently pushed her tody through the bars.
“Of all the idjits,” said the washerwoman,
not to know a kid’s body will offfen go where
’er head won’t.”
“Who’d ’a’ thought it?” said everybody.
Everybody looked a little abashed.
I went home.
B ILLY T.
Reincarnation.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
IF ever you should come to France, dear
heart,
Sweet France, who now appears
Triumphant through her tears,
Ah! do not go to see my lonely grave,
For I shall not be there; but rather part
Of every wind, of every silver wave
That dances, mad with Spring, along the
Seine,
Of every flower that folds the summer rain
Into its heart.
My happy ghost will haunt grey broken walls
Where dandelions hold
A cloak of yellow gold
Across the hidden gaps and healing Wounds —
And ancient orchards, where the peach-blow
falls
In drifting eddies on the yellow mounds
Of drying hay, and fields all blazing fair
With yellow mustard and with poppies,
where
The blackbird calls.
When you sit drinking in the summer heat
Some lazy afternoon
In ruddy-bosomed June.
I shall be with you, sitting dreaming, too—
Watching the passers in. the sun-splashed
street,
And on those wild Spring morns of gold and
blue
I shall be laughing in the breeze that comes
To waft faint scent from out the budded
plums,
And - stir the wheat.
But when you see, beneath some future sky,
Lifted from out the mire,
Proud roof and stately spire,
Fair Rheims restored, and every little town
Lifting afresh its laughing face on high,
From out its quiet Avreath of vineyards
brown,
And see with happy eyes the children go—
You see me born again—look long and know
That this is I.
N.S.W.
CHARLES F. PETTINGER.
